"THE BULLETIN,”
(Published at SYDNEY, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonparell
Illustrated Newspaper.
“The Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
h the only paper which has a consistent and dis-
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Complete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians.—The cheap
Clijnaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “nobility. ’
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is nob
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless newspaper-ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form —printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress: it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the cla : ms of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has nob been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and xnost
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

Dear Bulletin :—Your correspondent
“Squint ” states, in your issue of June 30, that
“ woman is a curvilinear, man areotilinear animal.”
“Squint” will probably write next to mention that
man has naturally got three legs. If he can find
even one straight line in the outline of the average
man. I will marry his grandmother. Evidently
“Squint” doesn’t wash often enough to know
how he looks with his clothes off. —Yours, &0.,
T.S.
Oolemane & Sons’ Vie. Ointment is marvellous.
Smith was a new-chum and green, but all
the same he got a billet as boundary-rider. As dogs
had been worrying the sheep on the back run, the
boss handed Smith a butcher’s-knife and a bottle
of strychnine, saying “go and poison half-a-dozen
sheep out there and see what that will do.” When
the boundary-rider returned, says the boss.; “Well,
Smith, did you poison those sheep;” '“Yes,”
replied the new-chum, rather excitedly, “and a
nice job I had getting some of the brutes to take
it.” The boss now does his own poisoning.
Oolemane & Sons’ Eucalypte Pills stir one up.
Steinberger, once a notable figure in
Samoan politics, died in Boston, U.S.A., on
May 3. He was an old comrade and trusted
friend of Grant, and when he arrived in Apia in
’73 in his yacht Peerless, ostensibly merely to
“ report on the condition of affairs ” there, he
brought with him a large sum of money to
further the design of annexation held by the
“Samoan party” in Congress, of which Grant
himself was the working but silent leader. Sub-
sequently, however, a majority-vote of Congress
disapproved of American interference and repudi-
ated Steinberger, but Grant stuck loyally to his
friend and sent further supplies of money; and
then Steinberger played a lone hand, and by mere
force of his personal individuality succeeded in
forming a Government of which he was the
mainspring and working head. A born soldier
and adventurer, as well as a fair administrator, he
soon won the sympathy and support of a
large section of the fickle native popula-
tion who were attracted to him by his
personal courage, eloquent tongue, and open-
handed liberality. But the white people of the
group were his bitter enemies. The planters,
merchants, and traders—German, English, and
American —did not want a man like Steinberger
at the head of affairs. He was a mercenary ad-
venturer, they said, and when he tried to establish
customs-duties they foresaw the end of their reign
of extortionate plunder and robbery of the natives.
They did not want a first-robber. As a brother-
robber he would have been tolerated. Common
cause was made. The English trader, hating the
Yankee trader, swore friendship with the German
trader who detested them both, and a strong and
bitter coalition of commercial greed and rapine
was formed against him. Two of the three Consuls
were the puppets and mouthpieces of the
party, and every power of intrigue, slander,
and misrepresentation was used to weaken and
corrupt his influence with the natives. At fono,
when the little, dark-faced man rose to speak, the
natives listened eagerly to his quick, burning words.
And he believed in the future he painted for
them. He did not want to be the Shirley Baker
of Samoa. Nor did he come there to steal their
money and their land and their liberties. But he
would make a nation of them and train them to
fight against and cast out the papalagi robbers
who were eating up their land day by day. He
displayed the sword that, when with Grant, he had
carried on many a bloody field in the Rebellion
and told them that that was his token of friend-
ship to their cause—Samoa for the Samoans. His
scathing, contemptuous invective of the Germans
—“They who steal your land by forged and lying
words written on the skins of mamce (sheep)of
the British Consul—“ That timorous lo matua
(old woman) who seeks to terrify you with silly
talk about the great power of another old woman
in England”—all helped to further his scheme and
increase the animosity of his white antagonists.
Once, at a big fono on Savaii, a chief —a creature
of the Consuls —rose and said that Samoa was
ever “a land sought out by moneyless wanderers.”
Eor answer Steinberger took a handful of gold and
flung it contemptuously out among the cocoanuts.
But the hidden forces arrayed against him proved
too strong; and Steinberger succumbed, defiant to
the last. The last act was played at a great fpno
held on Mulin’u Point in 1876. The captain of
an English man-of-war, the Barracouta, with an
alleged guard of honour, landed to attend the
meeting. A shot was fired, some say accidentally,
and then a scene of slaughter ensued between
bluejackets and marines and natives, Steinberger
was seized and deported to Fiji, and the one
chance of a free Samoa vanished with the smoke
of the rifles on Mulin’u Point.

The great E. glish Astrologi’st, Mme. DE RAYMOND,
reads your character without a question or mistake wuth
as much ease as if she had known you all her life. Re-
main not in darkness and ignorance, but seek the light
of knowledge, and learn what the future holds forth for
you. Like a meteor flashing across the darkest sky, this
brilliant Astrologist Visits Australia for the First Time to
impart the power, and the hitherto unknown knowledge,
which will dispel the heavy gloom and bitter woe from
thousands of blighted hearts.
Every mother should know the innate nature of her
child, the planet under which it was born, the un-
developed temper, the unformed habits, the particular
trade or calling it is best adapted for, to what sickness,
dictated by the heavenly bodies, it is heir, according

NOrtCK.
By sending direct to Tun Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sferliny—with postage added where required,
which you. may pay by Post. Office Order, One, Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province—you can
have Tub Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city Tub Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish
to interfere.
Cheques , Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Maclbod, Manaying Director
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘Correspondence" column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
Tub Bulletin will return all unavailable MSS
when the address is specified and stamps are enclosed.
But we desire those sending MSS. to distinctly under-
stand that we are not responsible for the preservation or
transmission oj such MSS.

SATURDAY, JULY 14, 1894.
The New South Wales General Election.
It is popularly assumed that every paper and
every person in any community under Par-
liamentary Government must be a supporter
of one or other of the recognised political
parties. The whole policy of Government
by Contradiction is built upon this assump-
tion, and Maoriland, in particular, so firmly
recognises the theory, that the elector who
doesn’t vote for one of the existing parties
is disfranchised, for the law takes no account
of the fact that a rational citizen may
find all parties about equally objection-
able. In New South Wales the State
doesn’t carry the popular idea to
the same logical conclusion, but it clings
to the theory none the less. It formally
recognises only two Parliamentary elements
—the Government and the Opposition ; and
it thereby assumes that the man who isn’t
satisfied with Dibbs must approve with his
whole soul of Reid, and the man who isn t
satisfied with Reid must find his highest
ideal in Dibbs. The possibility that any
elector who is clothed and in his right mind
may find a void in his political soul which
Dibbs and Reid are unable to satisfy, and
which can’t even be adequately filled by
Cook, or by Holman, the fluent young
man who leads the rival section of the
Labour-party and seeks to “down” Cook
and his party, lies altogether beyond the
philosophy of Party Government.
* • *
So far as concerns the three parties, which
practically make up the sum total of New
South Wales politics, The Bulletin’s posi-
tion is practically that it has no definite posi-
tion at all. The paper that supports men
always comes to grief, sooner or later, when
the men go astray after strange gods, as,
sooner or later, they always do ; and the
paper that supports parties—which are only
aggregations of men—invariably meets with
a similar experience. The one thing which
is certain about political parties is that they
are all about equally uncertain. In New
South Wales the Ministerial party consists
practically of Dibbs, and the reasons why
The Bulletin doesn’t support that poli-
tician are many. The Protectionist Govern-
ment, under his leadership, has made its
leading principle a mockery by establishing
a mongrel revenue-tariff which is not Pro-
tection in any reasonable sense, and which,
by assuming the name, brings the principle
into contempt. His alleged republicanism
has gone by the board with his accept-
ance of a cheap and tawdry decoration.
His alleged democratic principles went to
wreck when he declared against payment
of members, and when his Government
strained the forms of law and justice to sup-
press the Broken Hill strikers in their pro-
test against a shameless breach of faith, and
to help the mine-owners of the Barrier to
break their solemn covenant with their em-
ployes. Dibbs’ banking legislation was par-
tially excusable so far as it went, inasmuch
as it was necessary to make a choice in haste
between two evils, but when he took extra-
ordinary measures to save the shady bank
directors of the province, without providing
any safeguards for depositors and share-
holders in the shape of State supervision, he
constituted himself bully and chucker-out for
the Fat Men of New South Wales. And
as the Draconian Dibbs’ party made no
effectual protest against any of these things,
it and its leader are equally responsible.
* # #
The Dibbs Government has apparently no
intention of imposing a land-tax, nor does it
seem to be especially sincere concerning.
Federation, Local Government, or any other
great question. It lent a hand to the Colo
Valley job, and many other similar “ private
enterprises.” Its Minister for Works, Lyne,
is the apostle of shindykits and private
enterprise in general. And every one of its
members are on the list of candidates which
the National Ass urges its party to sup-
port through thick and thin, which fact alone
should be sufficient to utterly damn its
chances with the great bulk of the people.
The politician can be known by the kind of
men who approve of him, and the fact that
the National Ass cordially approves of the
Dibbs Ministry is equal to whole volumes of
execration.
• • «
The Reid party presents all the worst
features of the Dibbs party, and some of its
own in addition. It has approved cordially
of every hopelessly bad action of the Ministry
all through its career. It endorsed to the
uttermost the disgraceful conduct of the
Government during the Broken Hill diffi-
culty ; it helped along its banking legislation
and its various “private enterprises,” and
its leader is an even more cordial opponent
of payment of members than Premier Dibbs
himself. The followers of lawyer Reid have
been associated with almost every gross
extravagance perpetrated during the last
thirty years of the province’s history. The
party in general has lived on exciting sec-
tarian animosities, and it has been angling
of late for the votes of the Single-tax party
by roundabout promises to levy an almost
confiscatory impost on landholders. Now
that the election is at hand, however, it is
backing down from this rashly-assumed
position, and its promised land-tax is evi-
dently on a par with the other land-tax
whichPARKES and Treasurer Burns promised
so enthusiastically some six or seven years
ago. The party preaches Freetrade, yet
promises to raise £2,000,000 annually in
Customs duties, so that it is even an
apostate to its own miserable faith. It has
done nothing at all for Federation, or Local
Government, or Republicanism, or Land
Nationalisation, or any other Democratic
principle ; it is par excellence the party of
the Fat Man and the big landholder,
of the plutocrat, the shoddy importer,
and all these classes which neither toil nor
spin nor do anything whatever for the
good of the community. Also, it is worse
than the Government party, inasmuch as it
is opposed even to the scanty measure of
Protection which the present Ministry has
granted ; its one great and unpatriotic
yearning is to establish a Government of
importers which will wipe out native indus-
try in the interests of the import trade ; it
has not one-tenth the collective brain even
of the Dibbs Cabinet ; it is permeated with
the Parkes leprosy in its worst form ; and
its leaders, like Dibbs and all his colleagues,
are on the National Ass secret-circular list
of approved candidates, which is, in itself, a
sufficient evidence of their worthlessness.
# # #
These are a few of The Bulletin’s reasons
why it can support neither the Government
nor the direct Opposition. The reasons why
it docs not swallow the Labour-party whole
are equally explicit. That party appears to
have sold itself almost entirely to the Foreign-
trade interest, and the curse of Foreign-
trade weighs upon New South Wales almost
as heavily as the curse of landlordism
and National Ass politics. It is travel-
ling more and more upon the assumption
thatno one can beaDemocrat unless his hands
are largo and horny with manual toil, and
is approaching more and more to the ideal of
a Ministry of trades-union officials. It bitterly
opposes even good and reliable Democrats
unless they will take its cast-iron pledge and
subject themselves wholly to the domination
of the Labour Electoral League’s Central
Committee, a disinterested body whose
chiefest aim obviously is to get itself and its
brother Bill into Parliament. It is doing its
utmost to crush the best and most intellectual
men of its own party in Parliament because
they lack the needful subserviency, and to
replace them—mostly by men, so far as
the world knows, of far inferior mental
calibre. Moreover, it seems content to
remain a Labour-party, instead of becoming,
as it should be, a Democratic party with a
comprehensive national programme. On
questions which affect, not merely its own
constituency, but the community as a whole,
it seems as indifferent as either the Govern-
ment or the Opposition. It is growing
narrower instead of growing broader, and
its reputation for patriotism is vanishing
among the scrambles for Parliamentary
billets of its cheap debating-society orators.
# * •
The Bulletin believes thoroughly in the
alleged land-tax policy of Reid and his
followers ; it believes in the alleged Protec-
tionist and Federation policy of Dibbs and
his followers ; and it believes in almost all
the features of the alleged Labour pro-
gramme. But it disbelieves in the Reid
party because that party doesn’t believe in
its own land-tax scheme, and because it is
merely a grovelling agent for the Fat Man
and the importer; it disbelieves in the Dibbs
party because that body cares very little at
heart for Australian unity, because its Pro-
tection policy is miserably lukewarm, and
because it is also sold to the Fat Man and
the National Ass ; and it disbelieves in the
Labour - party because, though its prin-
ciples are wide and noble, its practice is
narrow and bigoted, and its decisive triumph
under present circumstances would only
result in the formation of a Ministry from
which the most enlightened democrats would
be excluded, for no other reason than that
they are not sons of manual labour, and have
on their brains instead of on their hands the
warts of toil. In short, this paper has long
since lost its faith in parties. But it will
give its utmost support to any man or party
or organisation which will put in the fore-
front of its programme the establishment of
the Referendum and the abolition of Party
Government, so that thereafter the country
may be rid of parties—as the term is now
understood—altogether, and for ever.
The Priesthood of Pump.
Herbert Spencer notes the curious fact that
the more things improve the louder become the
exclamations about their badness. “A century
ago, when scarcely a man could be found who
was not occasionally intoxicated, and when
inability to take one or two bottles of wine
brought contempt, no agitation arose against
the vice of drunkenness ; but now that, in the
course of fifty years, the voluntary efforts of
temperance societies, joined with more general
causes, have produced comparative sobriety,
there are vociferous demands for laws to pre-
vent the ruinous effects of the liquor traiiic.”
These demands take no account of the
wishes or character of the people. The
faith of the liquor prohibitionist in
the power of law to destroy natural
appetites is alone sufficient to discredit his
intelligence. For all history, all philosophy,
endorses Buckle’s dictum that “ to seek to
change opinions by laws is worse than futile.
It not only fails, but it causes a reaction which
leaves the opinion stronger than ever. First'
alter the opinions, and then you may alter the
law.” Thus the success of the prohibition
movement in Maoriland is bound to cause a
reaction proportioned to its success. Possibly,
with the aid of women’s votes, the ideal of the
pump fanatic will be reached, and the manu-
facture, sale, and use of intoxicating liquors
Avill be absolutely forbidden by law. What
will follow ? This : the law will be in-
operative in proportion to the number of men
who refuse to submit to the attack upon their
liberty. If these are in the minority, the traffic
prohibited by law will be carried on in spile of
the law, and with worse results to the com-
munity than at present. If they are in jthe
majority, the law will not be enforced. The
efficacy of women’s votes is largely overrated.
They are effective only as they are backed by
the votes of the men, or in questions upon
which men are indifferent. Should an issue
arise which divides the sexes into hostile camps,
and the women have a nominal constitutional
majority, the men will simply appeal to force.
That is the final test of supremacy.
• # #
The weakness of the Prohibition case has
recently been illustrated in Sydney by the Rev.
L. M. Is ITT, a Maoriland lecturer with a glib
tongue, a tine talent for buffoonery, and the
versatile impudence of a quack corn-doctor.
Now, it is easy to pile up blazing rhetoric about
the evils of drink. It is easy to tickle the ears
of the groundlings with coarse witticisms and
coarser sarcasm. But it is hard to compose a
consecutive argument which deals with every
fact and answers every objection. It is hard to
see truth steadily and see it whole, when one is
earning a good living as a paid advocate of a
single side of truth. Men of the I sett stamp,
even when they know better, will always
choose ease, and shun difficulty. They will
always prefer to fool fanatics to the top
of their bent, rather than try and con-
vince reasoning men. That is precisely
what the Reverend Isitt did. His lec-
tures were excellently calculated to charm
emotional ignorance and disgust critical intelli-
gence. He never supplied a fact where he
could substitute a sounding phrase ; he never
arrayed an argument when he could introduce
clap-trap invective. Even when he spoke the
truth ho suppressed the side which did not suit
him. He told his audience that at the medical
conference in Holland last year, the Dutch
doctors advocated total abstinence. He did
not tell them that the British section, through
its president, Dyce Duckworth, spoke in favour
of moderate drinking. He said that the fact
that the prohibition States in America had not
altered the law show’ed that they were satisfied
with its effects. He did not say that of 17
States which have tried prohibition, ten have
abandoned it, and in the others the law is a
dead letter.
* * *
Prohibition has failed signally in the United
States. This is the conclusion of an authorita-
tive and impartial observer, Mr. Michael
Herbert, to the British Embassy at
Washington, whose observations are published
in a report on “ Liquor Legislation in the
United States,” recently issued by the British
Foreign Office. Prohibition has failed signally
in Canada. The Scott Act, which gives to
every province the power to stop the sale of
drink within its own borders, is—the present
writer, who lately travelled in Canada, speaks
by the card—inoperative ; and the cry is now
for Dominion prohibition, which only a war
could force upon Lower Canada. Prohibition
would fail signally in Australia. It has been
tried at Mildura, with the result of demoralising
the community, and the residents have now by
a three to one majority decided to do
away with it. Not Here, where the senti-
ment of liberty is so strongly rooted, can
such an intolerable tyranny be established.
Everyone who thinks sees this. What
man of note, what man of intellect, ad-
vocates prohibition in Australia ? What news-
paper approves it? Its supporters are the
diseased brains of the community, the un-
healthy minds in unhealthy bodies, the men of
stunted physique, narrow foreheads, pimply
faces, flabby muscles, shrunken legs. Take
New South Wales Parliament as representing
the people. Compare G. D. Clark and Hindle,
leading abstainers, with Dibbs and Parke?,
leading drinkers. On which side are the brains,
the manliness, the vitality ? Will anybody
back Clark for two rounds with Dibbs ? Will
anybody wager that Hindle will be a father at
70 ? The contrast everywhere holds good.
* # •
The craving for intoxicants is natural and
universal. The ability to manufacture intoxi-
cants marks the great division between man
and brute. Once evolution progressed so far
that the prehistoric ape-man learnt how to
make a ferment, the rest was easy. Civilisa-
tion followed as a matter of course. And all
through history the nation that loved its liquor
was never beaten except by the nation that
loved its liquor better. Rome, founded
on wine, conquered the world, and fell
partly because, after the introduction of
Christianity, the quality of the wine deterior-
ated ; while the heathen Gothic beer was
unadulterated and strong. The golden age
of England was the beer age, the age
of Elizabeth, the age of Drake and
Shakspere, when tea, coffee, and cocoa were
unknown, and men, women, and children
drank humming ale continuously. Scandina-
vians are to-day the biggest and strongest men
in Europe—also the most drunken. Germany
is the strongest military nation, with the most
enormous beer-consumption. In fact, the world
has never been sober since the dawn of history.
If there were any truth in the eloquent
pictures of diseased livers at which teetotallers
groan, the race would have been extinct long
ago. And English medical men are now begin-
ning to find out that districts where tea and
sop predominate show a yearly loss of vitality,
while the brutal beer and meat consumer dies a
centenarian with 37 children.
« » *
It is no use arguing that if Australia gave up
drinking she would save ever so many millions
a year. If she gave up food and clothing
and went to roost in a tree every night she
would save ever so much more. But she is not
going to do anything of the sort. A purely
vegetable existence may satisfy the apostle of
Pump, but it is far too languid and uninterest-
ing for a young and lusty nation, which means
to eat its bread with joy and drink its wine
with a merry heart, and live joyfully with
the wife whom it loveth all the days of
the life of its vanity. Drink is a natural
instinct, and the desire of morbid zealots to
suppress it is of a piece with the unwholesome
sanctity which refrains from marrying and
washing itself and paring its nails in order to
keep the carnal man under. The world is
gradually learning that the carnal man cannot
be kept under ; that natural instincts are right,
and need only regulation in the light of experi-
ence. We have too long crucified the flesh at
the bidding of bigot creeds; and monasticism
and asceticism, in blighting life, have strangled
progress. Luckily, human nature is elastic,
and human appetites are crushed only to rise
again.
“ Well,” murmured one, “ let whoso make or buy,
My Clay with long Oblivion is gone dry;
But fill me with the old familiar Juice,
Methinks I might recover by and by.”
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Triumph of Tea.
Maoriland goes the pace. A S.M. Herald
correspondent gives a graphic picture of the
results of local option under the Act of 1892.
Never was such an overturning, of precedents,
preconceptions, and habits. The trite truth,
whi(h every generation of legislators slowly
learns, that it is one thing to make a law and
another thing to foresee its results, has received
quaint and delightful illustration, and the
country is aghast at its own handiwork. The
Act is for ever bobbing up serenely in unex-
pected places. To make it effective in any
district half the voters must vote. The electoral
law punishes those who do not vote by striking
their names off the roll. The framers of this
law certainly never intended it to apply to a
licensing-poll. But it does, or it is held to ;
and those who stayed away through careless-
ness, or of wicked intention, find themselves
disfranchised. Yet their negligence in many
districts leaves things as they were. In
others, where the hydropliilista hr on edit half


the voters to the poll, strange things
have happened. The new licensing-com-
mittees have closed hotels by the score, and
with a fine contempt of the fitness of things.
In Invercargill, a leading commercial hotel,
accommodating 40 boarders, was closed ; while
many others with much smaller accommoda-
tion remain open. This was done by secret
ballot, and the Supreme Court is to test
the legality of the proceeding. In Wellington
three°very old hotels were closed. At Dun-
edin, no less than 26 were extinguished,
and the first result in loss of license-fees, gas
and water rates, and other ways, is a reduction
of municipal revenue by .£6OOO per annum, in-
volving an additional 6d. rate. Water must
pay for its whistle. In the Clutha farming
district, the committee decided to refuse
licenses before hearing applications, and were
very properly told by the presiding magistrate
that such conduct stamped them as incapable
of exercising judicial functions. Then he pro-
ceeded to grant wholesale licenses by the dozen,
so that the Clutha farmer simply carries home
his beer in a barrel instead of in a jug or in his
stomach. And all over the province, while
hotels are closed, “working men’s clubs,”
which can sell liquor all day, all night, and
all Sunday, are in full blast, and doing splen-
did business. It is the task of legislative
teetotallers to stop this leak during the
present session of Parliament. Already they
have shown their mettle by the . abolition
of the Parliamentary refreshment-bar by 27
votes to 24, Premier Seddon voting with the
minority. Since there are 74 members in the
House of Representatives this may not be con-
clusive, yet it is thought that on the liquor
question Stout has a majority. That majority
expresses its determination never to stop short
of full prohibition. At Auckland, the prohibi-
tionists are so confident of success that they
thought it of no use organising for the local
option poll, and hotels there remain as formeily.
So Maoriland is to see a glorious set-to between
fanaticism and human nature. The Bulletin
backs human nature at 70,000 to 1.
A Matter of Evidence.
Melbourne Punch, in a recent issue, thus
muses over the recently-discharged assassin,
Bertrand : ,
It was really pathetic to see him walking about the deck
of the Austral. The years and years spent in prison, with
chains round his legs, gave him a peculiar shuffling walk
—a painful dragging of one leg after the other which,
will serve to mark him as a criminal as long as he lives.
As a fair example of how evidence is manufac-
tured, this statement is interesting. Bertrand,
as it happened, didn’t wear any chains at all
during the “ years and years.” spent in prison.
Like many other gentlemanly felons of note and
long standing, he gradually drifted into the
position of a privileged inmate, and spent a
great part of his time in painting pictures and
adorning his cell with nic-nacs. He also played
the organ in church on Sundays, and assisted in
the hospital, and was the patriarch and show
inmate of the establishment generally. There-
fore, he didn’t acquire the aforesaid “painful
dragging of one leg after the other ” —in fact,
he walks very much like any other man.
The Punch man evidently got his ideas of
that remarkable gait through seeing Dam-
pier playing the part of Jean Valjean,
the French galley-slave, and having decided
that the peculiarity should be there, he recog-
nised it at a glance despite its absence. If
Bertrand had been wanted by the police, and
his identification depended on that alleged
dragging of the feet, which wasn’t there, the
same credible witness would probably have
recognised and sworn to it in the same con-
scientiously stupid fashion. Most witnesses,
in fact, don’t see what they really do see ; they
simply see what they had previously made up
their minds that they were going to see. This
is one of the great secrets of police-court identi-
fication. Any person who studies the devious
ways of justice must notice that the man who
is rightly convicted, and the man who after-
wards proves to have been wrongly convicted,
are identified with just the same certainty.
The average, witness goes to court with a pre-
conceived idea that the person in the dock is the
person who should he there ; he sees a brutal-
looking face behind; the spikes—all faces lobk
brutal behind the spikes; even Paul before Fbstus
would have seemed the ideal of a garrotter if
the modern dock had been in use in those days
—and he promptly sees upon it the features of
the real criminal whether they are there or not.
If a squint, or a pair of abnormally large ears,
or some other peculiarity, is required to suit
the evidence he recognises it at the first glance,
just as the Melbourne pressman recognised in
Bertrand the obvious and unmistakable
results of a cause which didn’t exist. The
human 4 capacity for seeing what isn’t there
is practically illimitable. Even the early
Christians, who used habitually to meet with
devils arid angels, and to see the dead walking
out of iheir graves, were no more ridiculous
than their modern descendants ; the difference
lies solely in the fact that the early Christian was
constantly expecting to, meet devils and the
late Christian isn’t. The same rule applies to
the mediaeval person who frequently met with
wehr-wolves and witches riding on broomsticks
and similar trifles ; the kind of witness who is
always at hand to help along a Mount Rennie
hanging would meet any day in
Collins-street, Melbourne, if that animal was in
fashion and there was any demand for it.
"Someday, when a “History of Evidence ” is
produced, in 48 large volumes, this peculiarity
of the human mind will, perhaps, receive the
attention, which it deserves, and the obtrusive
cocksureness of the average stipendiary magis-
trate ami J.P. will receive a severe shock in
consequence.
\’Che Bitter Cry of Outcast Rue /
Australians.
The Bulletin has received from Villa Rica,
Paraguay, the following letter ;—-
Editor Bullbtin, —We are ex-members of the New
Australian Cooperative Settlement Association, having
arriv cl with the second hatch. We found on arrival that
we had been systematically deceived with respect to the
nature of tic country, the conditions under which we
were to work, and the events that h id taken place at the
settlement since its first, formation, about six months ago;
and that, this system of deception was still, as far as pos-
sible, be ing kept up, that everything that was done was
done in secret, and that every means by which we might
be able to receive information from outside was carefully
guarded by the man whom, in a moment of enthusiasm,
we had elected to be “ supreme executive authority
and his satellites. All their care in this way, however,
could not prevent some information from reaching us,
and thus we awoke to the fact that we had been fooled and
“had ” Briefly, we understood that the land we were about
to colonise was uninhabited. Judgeour surprise, on arrival
to find that it was more thickly populated than most
parts of what are called “ the settled districts of Aus-
tralia,” and that with regard to these old residents a
cruel process of “ eviction ” was going on—a thing which
we would never consent to be parties to. Our protest,
however, ivas of no avail ; because, notwithstanding the
fact that this association was supposed to be run on
democratic principles, the government was actually the
most despotic it is possible to conceive, the “Supreme
Executive Authority ” having taken it on himself to dis-
regard entirely the basis upon which the association was
formed, and absolutely refusing to account in any way,
or to give any information whatever with regard to the
affairs of the association, to us, who, up to that tune, had
supposed ourselves to lie co partners in the venture, each
with an equal stiare in the profits and an equal voice in
the management. All our attempts to obtain information
with regard to our position or the proprietorship of the
land, or to ascertain the financial condition of the asso-
ciation were systematically bluffed ; and, therefore,
thoroughly disgusted, w e packed up the few personal
effects we had and launched ourselves on the world to
work our way as best we could in a country
where we hardly know a word of the language.
We wish to warn anv persons who may possibly
be led away by the glowing reports of the pro-
gress of the Association, which are being continually
published, that the affair appears to us to be a deliberate
job ; and that, so far from being conducted on social
democratic principles, we do not think it is possible to
conceive any system more opposed to those principles. It
is in reality an oligarchy, in which a few men hold alt the
power, and the position of the rest is one of positive
slavery. Before leaving Australia a telegram was read to
us from Paraguay saying that eight (8) persons had left
the settlement. On arrival we found that before that
telegram was sent eighty-five (85) had left. We merely
mention this as a sample of the way that we have been
deceived. Whenever a truth was told us by those in
authority we used to treasure it up as a curiosity. The
lowest sum that any of ns paid into the Association was
£6O sterling. On leaving, we were offered 25 Paraguay
dollars —in English money, 15s. llpl. Some of us accepted
this, because we did not wish to start in aforeigncountry,
under the circumstances, without a cent in our pockets;
others refused it altogether. Hoping that this letter may
have the effect of making those who have any intention
of ioining the movement look before they leap, we are,
yours—
Joseph Conlan. Cooper’s Creek, Q.
Alexander Philimbys, Wiloamna, N.S.W.
Thomas Anderson, from Croydon, Q.
John Henry, Fremantle, W.A.
E. Taylor, Longreach, Q.
E. H. Graham, Q.
Charles Dkake, Fremantle, W.A.
Harry Simpson, Isisford, Q.
W. Bateman, Win ton, Q.
James Green, Maekav, Q.
William Janes, Emerald, Q.
Vil'a Rica, April 2, 1894.
This speaks for itself. The Bulletin is sorry
for the writers, and it doesn’t believe in jumping
on a man when he’s down. All the same, New
Australia has got to teach Old Australia a
lesson, which is this : that Australians may go
farther and fare worse. With all its drawbacks,
our country is the best country in the world for
the working man, and it rests with working
pien themselves to make it better. They can do
it if they like. W-hat could not the enthusiasm,
the energy, and the capital squandered in New
Australia have done in any province of the group?
It is no use saying that the laws hinder. What
is wrong in the laws can be altered by men who
are true to themselves and to each other. The
laws have not hindered others from succeeding,
and what one man has done others may do,
especially when helped by fellowship and strung
by a high ideal. Nearly all the wealth of Aus-
tralia is but a generation old. The “capi-
talists ” of to-day were the toilers of twenty or
thirty years ago. Take the richest of them all:
what but labour, high intelligence, and self-
denial, applied to the business of money making,
has lifted him from poverty ? His is a sordid
example, a pitiful aim ; but lie stands in a
country where money is the general god, to
show making all due allowances for the
special opportunities of the hyegone times —
that the avenues to success in Australia are
open to the humblest. And if the writers
of this letter were deceived, they were deceived
with their eyes open, after repeated warn-
ings. The Bulletin is weary of the old
Adam who gets up so persistently to say it
wasn’t his fault, and shove the blame on to
somebody else. That won’t do. It was the
foolish little pig that w'ent to Paraguay : the
wise little pig stayed at home. And, while
grass grows and rivers run, men must sutler
for their folly. It is right ami just that it
should he so. If you put your finger into fire
it burns you; if you put your head under water
it drowns you; and if you go and make a
prodigal of yourself in Paraguay, you will
probably chew a fine assortment of husks before
you get back again. Such punishment is the
index of broken law. It is old Mother Nature’s
method of teaching her silly children the way in
which the world is governed. It is lucky she is
patient; for w T e learn very slowly. Everybody
goes to Paraguay once in a lifetime; hut
Joseph Conlan and the rest went twice, and
put an actual froth on top of the metaphorical
drink from the bitter cup of experience. What
is the settlement assejeiation going to do for
them? It has taken their money by misrepre-
sentation ; it has bamboozled them with oratory
and printer’s ink ;it has landed them desolate
on a foreign shore ;is it going to leave them in
the lurch ? It looks very like it. The intend-
ing pilgrims have been sent home again, and
the association’s ship has been hired out to a
stranger. If this is equality, fraternity, the new
order" and the brotherhood of man, in the name
of the incorruptible Robespierre, give us
rather “ the cut-throat competition of our
social savagery ! ”
The Gentle Art of Gulling People.
Parkes, the inimitable poser, gets cheers from
crowded election meetings by denouncing the
“ abominable Electoral Act”—which must be
repealed forthwith. Not so fast ! The
“abominable Electoral Act” is the single
measure by which the Dibbs Ministry deserves
to he remembered ;it is the most valuable Act
which has been placed upon the New South
Wales Statute-book for many a year. Its worth
is none the less that Premier Dibbs, Repub-
lican turned Royalist, is heartily sorry that he
ever put a finger to it; and is doing his level
best to lessen the terrible weight of its influence
for Democracy. Parkes, with all his years of
office and all his opportunities, with all his
brag and bluster, never wished, never dared,
to pass such an Act. Parkes’s Brutus like
enthusiasm for the destinies of Australia,
his yearning to offer up the*'*’*scanty remnant of
my life” on the altar of patriotism, are visible
only at election-times. Parkes brims over
with pathos when he is out of luck. v hen in
office, he spends his “declining years in hold-
ing on to power with tooth and eyelid, and
sacrificing “ the destinies of Australia to the
exigencies of the road-and-bridge members.
Parkes is at hair a hater of Democracy.
He yearns to bind nascent Australia to
the foot of the British monarchy with his
“ blanky crimson clothesline.” Small wonder
that such a man, not daring to oppose
it openly, seeks to destroy the Act upon side-
issues. Small wonder that the Conservative
press of Sydney is never tired of pointing out
its deficiencies ; and pity that so many demo-
crats should have been misled to follow ! Why,
the Act is the new charter of New South Wales
liberty. It is a measure of radical electoral
reform. It gives pure manhood suffrage,
abolishing the iniquitous plural vote ; it gives
one-day elections ; it does its best to make the
representation of the people full, and the
machinery of representation perfect. Of course,
it fails in details—what measure so new and so
important does not ? When ever did the wit of
man foresee all the consequences of a law? Of
course, it, seems hard to work at first; what
complicated measure does not? Every change
involves accommodation by gradual use. The
Act must be amended —yes ; but repealed
emphatically never ! It has been won too
hardly to be parted with lightly.
Patterson’s Credit Poncier.
The Victorian Land Bank scheme, as it is
gradually unfolded in Parliament, proves to be
just the sort of scheme which might be ex-
pected from the Government of a land-
boom country. The ostensible purpose of
the measure is to raise money on State security
at 4 per cent., and lend it on mortgage to the
farmer at 5 per cent., so as to relieve him from
the usury to which he is now subjected—a per-
fectly legitimate and feasible scheme, as the ex-
perience of Continental Europe has proved for
many years past. But the Patterson Go-
vernment refuse to see that their Credit
Foncier Bill is full of suspicious pro-
visions, apparently put in for the express
purpose of enabling the proposed institution to
go queer at the earliest possible date. The
proviso that the Commissioners, instead of hold-
ing office during good conduct, should retire
annually in rotation, and their successors be
appointed by the Cabinet, makes the control of
the Land Bank a convenient piece of political
patronage. Also, it enables the Government
to keep the Commissioners in a state of due
subserviency in the hope of retaining their
billets. The proposal to restrict loans to a
maximum of £IOOO was promptly extinguished;
the £IOOO limit is quite sufficient if the measure
is intended for the relief of the agricul-
turist only, but —well, it would not suit
a whole lot of people in the city of
the Busted Land Boom. It is decreed, how-
ever, that no loan of over £IOOO is to be made
without “ the sanction of the Minister,” so
every large loan which is granted is an item of
political patronage, and can he made an equiva-
lent for political support. The Bill practically
authorises the Ministry to over-ride the Com-
missioners’ decision, and to order the granting
of loans on almost any security they may
think fit. Also, the Ministry may forbid the
foreclosure of mortgages that are in arrear.
Under these circumstances, every borrower who
wants public money on bad security will
harry his local member to get it for him,
and the member will harry the Minister,
and the Credit Foncier funds will, in
the long run, be practically given away
to purchase political support. No Com-
missioner may lend the public money to a
company of which he is a director, but, accord-
ing to the original bill, he might lend it to a
company of not loss than 20 persons in which
he was oidy an “ordinary shareholder.” Under
these circumstances every commissioner would
invariably be about the most ordinary share-
holder imaginable. The Credit Foncier is to
take over the Savings Bank business, and
Savings Bank funds, it leaked out in
debate, have already been lent to com-
panies in which officials are personally inter-
ested. Particulars of these loans are not avail-
able. Altogether, the State Land Bank
promises to be an enormously enlarged and
bloated edition of the Mercantile or the Federal,
and when it has existed for a few years on the
Patterson plan, and grown to its full size, the
“revelations ” should be something such as the
world hasn’t seen or heard of since the days of
Panama. Even MTlwraith’s land-grant rail-
way project doesn’t contain greater possibilities
of corruption than the Patterson Credit
Foncier.
The Reign of Brummagem.
Dear Bulletin, —I must differ With “Theo-
dore Woinarski ” in your last issue,
especially in his general statement that the
political influx of foreigners into Australia is
merely a question of figures and averages. If
he reads his Sydney Daily Telegraph regu-
larly he’ll see that the candidates won’t obey
the law of averages —they’ll see the law of
averages damned first —and will go to the poll
in the teeth of the law of averages, and in
defiance of simple proportion. I won’t dispute
“ T.W.’s ” figures, as I haven’t got Coghlan
handy, but I think Coghlan says, in the
“Seven Colonies of Australasia,” that the pro-
portion of natives all round to the wdiole popu-
lation is as 61 to 100. I agree with “ T.W.”
that there is such a thing as love of country
—which includes patriotism. The native-born
Australian has only one country to love,
while the “foreign” candidate has two. A
man who has two countries, especially if he
evinces a blackguard haste to govern No. 2,
must be a mercenary —he must keep a political
harem. There is also the danger—not an un-
substantial one—of the double-barrelled patriot
legislating with one eye on each, and with a
consequent squint. We know that there are
certain ’igh persons who look for praise and
reward, not within New South Wales, but
towards a certain little island in the direction
of the soles of our feet. Patterson doesn’t
care what Victorians think of him, except in
Castlemaine—he has his weather eye on Aln-
wick, in Northumberland, where he is looked
upon as a little deity and held up —ye gods !
as an example for youthful emulation. But,
enough. I am happy to say that if the law of
simple proportion dooms us to short commons
in the matter of quantity, we have quality
right enough, for most of the bright debaters in
last New South Wales Parliament were native-
born.—Yours, Cornstalk.

The Piddington Complication.
The curious situation of Mr. “ Turkey ” Pin
dington, jun., Foreign-trade candidate fo
Tamworth (N.S.W.) has served of late to bring
that very small politician into unwonted pro-
minence. Mr. Piddington was a lecturer in
Sydney University, and being to a great extent
paid by public endowments, he was regarded in
some quarters as practically a civil servant, and
as such a person who ought to be disqualified
for Parliament. At the same time he was not a
recognised civil servant, so the disqualification
was not a legal but only a sentimental one, and
not very sentimental at that. Mr. Piddington
some time ago announced his intention to con-
test the Tamworth seat in the Foreign-trade
interest, and to this no objection was made, so
far as the public are aware. But later on,
when he applied for leave of absence in order
to stump the constituency, the application was
met with a flat refusal, consequently Mr. Pid-
dington was unable to address his intended
constituents, and if his election depended upon
his personal influence he was practically out of
the running. A remarkable side issue turns up
for consideration just about here. Some time
ago Education Minister Suttor positively re-
fused to entertain an application by the Univer-
sity Senate for an additional endowment to its
pompous and not particularly useful institution,
and promised instead a substantial reduction in
the existing allowance. About the time of the
Piddington upheaval, however, Premier Dibbs
suddenly and unaccountably assured that august
body that the increase petitioned for would be
granted if he returned to ofiice. Therefore, the
Senate has a powerful cash inducement to do
all it can, in its own humble and not especially
brilliant fashion, to secure the return of
that long and spectacled politician, and under
these circumstances the Foreign-trade press
puts forward, as definitely as it can without risk
of the libel law, these alternative assumptions ;
(1) that the Senate refused Piddington leave
of absence lest he should go to Tamworth and
there utterly blast Dibbs and thus wreck the
promised endowment; and (2) that the endow-
ment was promised tacitly on condition that
the Senate sat on Piddington, wherefore
Piddington was sat upon accordingly. The
evidence on one side is that there is no visible
honest reason for Dibbs’ sudden and altogether
needless change of policy with regard to the
University endowment. The evidence on the
other side is that Dibbs’ policy is always chang-
ing without any reason, and he is only' con-
sistent and unchangeable when there is some
one to be hanged. Also, if he had succeeded
in preventing the weak and harmless Pidding-
ton from doing his own canvass at Tam-
worth, the Freetrade party would almost
inevitably have sent up some one to act
on his behalf, and as that person could
hardly be less influential than Piddington
the change was almost sure to be for the worse.
Further the Senate, which is habitually lofty
and cantankerous, may easily have acted as it
did from purely cross-grained motives; the
fact that Mr. Piddington announced his candi-
dature first and applied for leave of absence
afterwards was eminently calculated to raise
the bile of such an organisation and aggravate
its small dignity. Also, it may have acted as
it did from mere formalism, without its dignity
being aggravated ; or it might have been incited
by the Foreign-trade party with a view to
raising a prejudice against Mr. Dibbs. Alto-
gether the Piddington embroglio is a many-
sided complication. The one thing that is
certain about it is that Premier Dibbs
committed an act of ghastly foolishness
in promising a subsidy on his own
responsibility, at a time when all sub-
sidies are suggestive of bribery and corruption;
and that he was grossly discourteous in can-
celling Minister Suttor’s decision upon a matter
that lay in Mr. Sdttor’s own department.
Meanwhile, Mr. Piddington, having resigned
his University billet, goes on his interrupted
canvass, so to all practical purposes the incident
is closed and only the effluvium remains.
The Camel Odious!
The camel insupportable ! The camel an
offence and a stumbling-block in a civilised
country ! How Major Leonard would rub his
eyes could he be transported suddenly from
England to Bourke on the magic Arabian
carpet ! Why, this case-hardened soldier,
with sixteen years’ camel-commissariat ex-
perience, in his just - published (Longmans)
book on “ The Camel,” becomes all but a
poet in his enthusiasm for the invaluable
drudge of humanity. He looks back into the
dim dawn of history in the East ; and, far
as the eye can penetrate, sees still the camel
jolting patiently on his interminable journey,
living hard, working hard, embruted and
coarsened by continual ill-treatment, his fine
intelligence dulled by the cruelty of savage
nomads, his noble capacities degraded to the
uses of a boast - of - all - work, yet dreeing
his monotonous weird with discontented re-
signation, a fatalist without fear, without
joy, without hope ! Indeed, little is wanting
for Major Leonard to apply Shakespere’s
apostrophe, and say : “ What a piece of work
is a camel 1 How noble in reason ! How infinite
in faculties 1 in form and moving how unusually
express and admirable ! in action, how like an
angel; in apprehension, how like a god ! the
beauty of the world ! the paragon of animals !”
But the Major is truthful ; he admits that his
pet is neither beautiful nor intelligent : hard
work has destroyed the camel’s msthetic
qualities and constituted him on a severe
basis of utility. There, in dry country, the
camel shines like a star. He is tougher,
faster, longer lived, and cheaper (initial
cost over) than either horse or bullock ; and his
ability to do without food or water at a pinch

Leonard, soon get used to camels; their fear is
not instinctive, or due to the camel’s smell, but
is merely the effect which any strange object—
such as a train, tram or bicycle—produces on
their nervous organisation. And when the
Major ends by assuring us that he has no doubt
that Australians, “ with the usual energy and
determination which they infuse into every-
thing they take in hand, will pay the serious
attention to the camel question of which it is
deserving,” what remains but to abolish our
Afghan (“ the dirtiest brute on record,” says
the Major), and set to work in earnest to make
our camel profitable ?
What is the difference, as a party politician,
between Pabkes and the solidarity Labour
candidates? Parses said in North Sydney
School of Arts that both Freetraders and Pro-
tectionists should give up squabbling over the
fiscal question. He explained that this did not
mean an abandonment of the matter in dispute,
but only a postponement of it till Federation be
achieved. On the other hand, the phrase of
the solidarity Labour-party is—“ Sink the fiscal
issue 1” Till when ? Only till certain legisla-
tive results affecting Labour are achieved.
Those, or many at least of those, who “ sink ”
the fiscal issue mean exactly what Parkes
means when he “ postpones ” it ; and when he
expresses the hope that Federation will be
secured in two years and the decks be cleared
for a fiscal fight, he again merely uses “ Federa-
tion ” where the fiscal-sinkers use “ Labour
legislation.” Yet Parkes denounces these
sinkers as “ no men,” and these sinkers return
the compliment.
The Wild Cat Column.
The poor banks hare a lob bo contend'with. One
bank holds shares (on which there is a liability o 1 over
£16,000) in the name of one of its employes, getting £9O
a year. All efforts made by the 00. to call up the £15,000
having failed, it hit on the ingenious plan of offering
the a permanent billet of £250 a year, if he could
get the bank to give him an indemnity against his lia-
bility. The bank threatened “ sack " if he persisted in
his demand, but at last had to give the indemnity. Four
days later the ex-bank-clerk had the satisfaction of serving
the bank with a writ for the £15,000 odd liability for
which he had been indemnified by them.

lb has been rumoured bhab the V.D.L. lottery
has fallen through in Australia. George Adams (“ Tatter-
sail”), who is in responsible charge, explains that this is
a mistake. The fact is that there exists a good market
for shares in England, and the manager has simply made
a trip there to take advantage of it. The drawing will be
held in Australia when the subscription-list is completed.
• • * •
One of the conditions in a big co.’s reconstruc-
tion scheme stipulates that any shareholder who does not
pay the first call within a specified time, immediately
become liable to pay up the other whole balance callable,
which is otherwise spread over a long period. Considering
what a vast amount of the liability is held by one Southern
bank (which intitted some years ago in making an over-
drawn customer transfer to it all his shares in the co.) it’s
little wonder that the first call has been responded
to magnificently.
• • • •
The next half-yearly meeting of Melb. Metro-
politan Gas Co. is likely to be a very painful event for
those short-sighted capitalists who bought their shares
upon a calculation of perennial 20 per cent. divs. Also,
the aggressive person who desires to abolish kero-
sene with a prohibitive duty may as well prepare a
flaming harangue to suit the occasion. The gas-monopoly
is tottering, and only a vigorous change of policy can
save it from coming quite to grief. The company, by
fatuously trying to squeeze the old amount of profit from
a diminished constituency, has given a tremendous im-
petus to the lamp-trade, and at the present rate of
secession there will soon be no gas consumers left to
grumble over the inflation of their accounts.
Formerly ib was the oo.’s policy to bolster up
the profits with a view to selling the property to Govern-
ment at a price based upon the div. per centage, but now
that golden dream has evaporated, and the management
must either provide a good show of gas on reasonable
terms, or lower its limp flag to the cheap but reliable oil-
lamp. There is some pathos in the situation. The co.
has ceased to be very arrogant, and the collector has
apparently been instructed to plead gently for payment
of two months’ arrears lest the customer, already coquet-
ting with the idea of a new and improved lamp, should
bid his oppressors cut off the gas and be d d.

Melb. gas is kept up in price by the London
shareholders.

The State Life Assurance Bill before S.A. Par-
ment authorises the issue of policies up to £5OOO, on the
life of any one individual, and the sale of inalienable
annuities up to £2OO. It further empowers the Govt, to
make life assurance compulsory in any branch of the
public service. Premier Kingston has charge of the
measure—the second bill introduced this session.
• • • *
In Maoriland, when some dead and probably
unidentifiable remains are found in the bush, the local
authorities proceed to realise the “estate” of the
deceased, and the balance is forwarded, together with an
elaborate statement of accounts, to the Public Trustee, to
await claimants. One balance-sheet reads as follows :
“ Found on the deceased, 2Jd. Paid into Colonial
Treasury, 2Jd.” That was a good many years ago, but
the 2Jd. still lies waiting in the Public Trust Office, and
if the descendants of the corpse turn up anytime between
now and the day when Maoriland is finally annexed by
the Chinese, they will find the coppers all ready to be
handed over. This is where the State gets ahead of the
private trustee ; it is next door to immortal, and even
an unclaimed balance of 2Jd. will wait patiently through
the long procession of the centuries till the rightful
owner arrives. Wanted, everywhere, a Public Trust Office.

• * • •
One large Sydney brewery closes now for a half-
day every week. Certainly winter is the brewer’s slack
time; but the fact remains that this particular concern
never closed its doors during any previous winter. By the
way, Sydney publicans complain of the want of considera-
tion shown them, by their being compelled to renew
licences and to plank down £3O in the depth of winter,
instead of at the beginning of the year, when their trade
is brisk.
• • • •
That fearsome speculation, the Midland Rail-
way Co. of Westralia, has intimated that the Government
will have to pay the July interest on its guaranteed
debentures. The Midland has already cost the State, in
money, land-grants and guarantees, about as much as it
would have cost to build the line as a State institution,
and there is nothing to show for it all save an embarrassed
company and a financial scandal which will make Westralia
sit up when it is fully investigated by the successors of the
Forrest Government.

Minister O’Loghlen told the Vic. Assembly last
week that if he were a millionaire he would make a mad
rush to invest his entire capital in Credit Foncier (Vic.)
bonds. “ It’s a lucky thing you are not a millionaire,"
remarked somebody, and the House laughed hoarsely.
The association of O’Loghlen’s name with illimitable
wealth was considered humorous, or perhaps it set mem-
bers thinking of the famous ’Enry Parkes’ statement.
Everybody knows what Parkes said he would do with hia
million—if he had it.

Dear Bulletin, —Really there has been no bigger
malefactor in Melbourne than Mat. Larkin, the caged
convict, as you call him. Unpunished men have practi-
cally embezzled much larger sums of money than Larkin
was able to get hold of, but he took everything he could
conveniently collar with cold-blooded regularity. A
struggling man would pay money over the counter to
jolly Matthias, and jolly Matthias would put it in his
pocket, without even going through a form of crediting
it to the poor devil’s account. He was not so much a
reckless gambler with other people’s cash, as an impudent
thief who undoubtedly had a lot of stuff “ planted ’* some-
where before he went away to “do his little bit.” Let
him do a bit more, if only to call further attention to the
swindlers who escape prosecution.—Yours, South MEL-
BOURNE
• • • •
Premier Patterson’s auctioneer brother was
selling some Melb. suburban properties last week (on the
bank deposit receipt system) when he confidently
stated that the depression had concluded and
confidence was now restored—which makes the 49th
time of restoration within the last sixteen months. The
gall was in the silent insinuation that brother James had
given the auctioneer a straight tip upon the subject—had,
in fact, abolished depression and guaranteed an improve-
ment in prices for “freehold estate." With reckless
inconsistency, though, the auctioneer announced that
deposit receipts of the National, the Victoria, and the
London Bank of Australia would be accepted at their face
value, whilst other bank paper would have to go at the
ruling market price. Which was not very encouraging to
the confidence of people who have coin locked up in the
aforesaid other banks.

Melbourne tailoring firm with a well-known M.L.A.,

an employd in the shop at £5 per week. The unhappy
tailor states that he paid £ll,OOO into unfruitful land
syndicates, and owes a further £14,000 on share calls, but
somehow his application for a certificate was refused. He
had been a “plunger in shares,” said the Judge, who
once felt much pleasure in granting Jimmy Munro an
unconditional release. Munro was merely “unfortunate,”
and not a plunger at all, one supposes.

• • « •
The manager of a Sydney evening newspaper
the other day demanded that his compositors should
agree to a reduction of their scale rates to 6d. per 1000
ens—a reduction of 50 per cent.; or he would introduce
at once Linotype machines and dismiss all but 25 hands.
If they would accept the sixpenny rate he promised not
to introduce the machines for a year. Meeting with a
refusal, he urged a ninepenny rate, and he would not
introduce Linotypes for four months. Being again re-
fused, he gave notice that he would dismiss all but 25
comps., and that the Linotypes should come in as soon as
possible. The Typographical Association’s executive has
advised a “ strike." The Bulletin is afraid that the
Linotype machine is an inevitability effective machinery
invariably displaces human labour. But the Linotype
should make it easy for many batches of a few poor
printers to start co-operative papers of their own.

It is generally believed by experts that 50 per
cent, of all the new shillings in circulation are now made

* « • •
Maoriland proposes to make the issue of bank-
notes a State monopoly. It is estimated that £1,000,009
in paper will supply the province’s requirements, and
against these the Treasury will hold a gold reserve of
£250,000. Consequently, the State will get a practically
interminable loan of £750,000 without interest, and at
the same time will put the currency on a safe footing for
the first time.

_ Editor Bulletin.—Sir, —You have accurately
diagnosed the Mt. Hoaxley case from the first, but perhaps
you will permit me to add a few details. The report of
Mr. Samuel’s flowery address occupied three columns
in Hobart Mercury of 18th May. My short letter,
traversing and ridiculing Mr. Samuel’s statements,
was published alongside thereof. On 19th May Mr.
Montgomery was sent to inspect Mt. Huxley. The Mines
office was put on its guard by people here, but leading
officials scouted the idea that the Government geologist
could be imposed upon. On 28th May Mr. Montgomery
telegraphed There is a large auriferous formation in
places fairly rich. ... I think well of the property."
It was not till 7th June that Mr. Urquhart, M.H.A., at
my request, asked those questions in the House which
dragged forth the admission that the mine had been
“salted.” Mr. Montgomery has since amply vindicated
his excellent reputation, but the fact remains that from
28th May till 7th June the “velvet brigade” had every
facility given them to “unload” not only here but also
on the mainland.—Yours, etc., Theodore Bbvan, Hobart.
3rd July, 1894.
• • • •
Stated that Begelhole, late manager of Bayley’s
Reward, has bought the Londonderry for £50,000 and a
sixth interest. This is good news for the careful members
of the party who sold out for £3OOO apiece. There is
nothing so unsatisfactory as the proverb about a bird in
the band. The bird in the hand invariably turns out a
wretched little victim of pip, while those in the bush are
fat as wild-duck in winter.
* * • *
The prospective litigation with the Midland
Railway Co., also the Hampton Plains Syndicate, who are
alleged to claim that the Londonderry is on their con-
cessions, conveys yet another warning to the Democratic
party against land-grant lines and wholesale land-aliena-
tion. Here are prospectors facing, in the wilderness,
drought and famine and death in the search for gold, and
ail the while a foreign syndicate which grabbed the land
at half-aorown an acre steps in to reap the fruit of thslr
pluck and labour.
• • • •
Another rich find is reported a few miles south
of the Londonderry, but particulars are not to hand. A
240 z. nugget has, however, teen picked up—a decent find
as times go, though nothing to the old Victorian heroes
of 21590 z. and 22800 z.
* « • •
Bayley, interviewed at Albany, explains that
he would never have sold his share in the Reward for
£6OOO bad there been sufficient protection from jumpers.
He still holds 37,000 out of his 40,000 shares in the co.,
and is convinced that the mine has a great future.
• * • •
The finest reef-gold specimens seen in Sydney
for years hail from the Ninety-mile, W.A., and are on
view at Mr. T. Bibby’s officers, Pheenix Chambers, Pitt-st.
* * • ♦
Mr. Wm. Nicholas, F.G.S., lecturer on geology
and mining at Melb. University, and Australia’s best-
known gold-expert, leaves for Coolgardie about 25th July
and will stay in Westralia for a few months examining
mines.
• • • •
After much litigation, delay and expense the
United Coolgardie Co., of Sydney, has got the titles of
the Clyde and Ritanita leases confirmed to it, the W.A.
Executive having refused to forfeit same to the jumpers.
When first the leases were jumped it was reckoned a
moral certainty that they would be forfeited, but all
Westralia has since learned how professional jumping
wrecks reefing. A few months ago a thousand men
assembled at Coolgardie to back up the Ritanita jumpers.
Now, public meetings decide to boycott all professional
blackmailers. A parcel of rich specimens from the Clyde
claim is on view at the Co.’s office, Post-office Chambers,
Sydney.
• • « «
Queensland has just completed a successful half-
year in gold-production, with a total output of all but
300,0000 z. This is just up to the average, the return for
the past three years having gone a little over 600,0000 z. a
year. This regularity is rather surprising when the
diversity of factors included is considered.
Charters Towers is, of course, at the head of
the list with 111,418 tons, for 118,5440 z., last month’s
return being 19,141 tons, for 24,2330 z. Towers stone is
not crushing so well this year ns last, the yield in the
first half of 1893—135,8300 z. from 95,688 tons—being con-
siderably better. Qympie, on the other hand, has won-
derfully improved, the yield for the first half of ’93
being 27,3450 z. from 27,084 tons, while for '94
it is 54,2650 z. from 40,230 tons. Gympie mines are also
worked more economically, so that although they only
crushed 40,000 tons of stone to the Towers 111,000, they
yet paid £112,000 divs. as compared with £127,000 for the •
northern field.
The Gympie June yield was 10,0020 z. from
7599 tons stone, paying £22,558 in divs. The principal
contributor* were Phoenix Pile with £7200, Phoenix with
£6458, and N. Smithfleld with £6OOO. According to B.
Pope’s report, this was rather less than anticipated ; but
three important developments are noted during the
month—the cutting of black slate by the veteran 1 North
Phoenix in a winze down 212 feet from the 666 feet level,
sending shares from 9s. to 215.; the cutting of slate by 1
N. Smithfleld in the winze on the Columbia reef ; and the
cutting of the Wilmot reef in the eastern crosscut of the
1 South Phoenix. The market-value of shares generally
has risen during the month.
Mount Morgan gives the best part of 52,8480 z.
to the half-year's total; but Croydon is low down with
30,0790 z. The hopes of Croydon investors are gradually
falling. In most of the claims the gold gets leaded at a
depth, and the difficulty of working goes beyond profit-
pitch. The most promising claims on the field now are
the True Blue block, which recently crushed 2200 tons for
33860 z., and the True Blue Extended block. In the
average Croydon claim the normal state of things is one
of doubt and struggle.
That state is intensified on the Etheridge, where
the list of “hung-ups” is portentous and awful. The
Etheridge produced last half 11,0120 z., and Ravenswood
70590 z. —the latter showing a good rise, since the yield
for the whole of last year was only 92850 z. The Queen
Constance at Mareeba has had a very good crushing,
considering the bulk of stone, getting 883 oz. from 997
tons, paying a 6d. div,, and carrying £3OO forward.
* • • •
True Blue Block, Croydon (Q.), has declared 3d.
div. (£1250). This is rather a bullion than a gold-mine,
last four crushings showing gold (or bullion) worth from
325. to 35». 6d. per oz. A big diorite bar intersects this
property, but the lode is solid in deep ground behind. A
little more economy in working would benefit share-
holders.
• • • •
Ophir Block, Croydon (Q.), famous for its
33000 z. crushing, averaging nearly a lb. of gold to the
ton, is again coming to the front. Tributors are working
the big lode, raising 100 tons weekly, and plenty in sight.
Practically, the owner of the property, though he has
drawn much from it in profits, doesn't work it on his own
account, but prefers tributing. A few hundreds spent in
the bottom would soon prove the extent of the bar which
has nearly silenced work on Iguana Hill.
• • • •
Funny bub true. Bank charges at Croydon
field, Q. 2/2 per oz. of metal. Same institution only allows
1/8 for silver when purchasing. Hence a mine-owner
loses 6d. for every oz. of silver he sells. Good business
for the banks.
• • * *
Mountain Maid United (Croydon, Q.) last
crushing, 120 tons for 128ozs. 6dwts., not up to expecta-
tions. Result' may most likely be accounted for by
amount of precious metal in tailing-heap. This co. must
soon erect other appliances than stamper and berdan-pans
to save the gold from their extra-heavily-mineralised
stone.
• • * •
Wyalong reports two small crushings—2o tons
for 40-odd oz., and 30 tons for a yield so small that
the reporter is ashamed to mention it. There is a well-
founded impression current at Wyalong that if a little
“ cracking-up ” could attract some floating cash it would
help “ the field ” immensely. So when the Mallee Bull (a
really good claim) put up some 4x2 uprights and a bit of
bark over them the other, day, Sydney evening papers
duly announced that “ splendid poppet-heads had been
erected.”
• * * *
At Hillgrove, the Sunlight miners are out on
strike. An elaborate scale called the “ tonnage system,”
was drawn up under which the miners say they could not
earn ss. per day. When the men’s deputation interviewed
the manager they were met with the reply that the terms
offered were too good for them. The men are very orderly
and confident of winning.
Sinister silence re the achievements of the Han-
cock jigs at Block 14. The South jigs, which operated
on a more favourable class of sulphide, are still shut. down.
Sulphide concentration on the Barrier is under a cloud.
Block 10 prospects do not improve with the
sinking of Campbell’s shaft. The shaft, now about, 40ft.
below the 1115 ft. level, is to be sunk another 100 ft., and
a crosscut put in east from the 1115 ft. level. What the
manager and directors expect to discover, either in the
shaft or crosscut, is difficult to say. Prospects at depth
are as forbidding as could be—in fact, Campbell’s shaft
and the famous deep crosscut from the Extended shaft,
into Central property, have proved that Broken Hill deep
sulphides—at the Block 10 end at least—are hopeless.
Recent rise in Centrals has been caused by the
development, beneath the curious white carbonate of lead
body about the 500 ft. level, of a silicinus granulated lead
body of less lead grad*, but averaging 40oz. silver. The
width of the latter has been proved 50ft., and assays
indicate at least 40oz. silver and 30 per cent, lead, with
only 12 per cent, zinc—a smelting grade. Other -nines
have intractable sulphide at this depth.
• • ♦ •
At Wyalong. Mr. Arthur Tremayne, well-
known in connection with the Mildura strike and the
founding of the Vic. Co-operative Irrigation Settlements,
is on the field for the purpose of starting a battery on the
lines of co-operation. The main features of the scheme
are:—l. That mine owners do not pay to become share-
holders until the calls are obtained from the stone. 2.
That shares cannot be used for speculation, because new
shares can be bought at par from the office of the com-
pany, and the maximum dividend is to be 10 per cent.
• • * •
A good deal of dissatisfaction exists on the
Murchison owing to the paucity of news from the field in
the Eastern papers, as compared with that from Cool-
gardie. “It is the opinion of many that the Murchison
will be a large mining centre when Coolgardie is com-
paratively deserted,” says a correspondent. The old
story—
“ Quid Ireland was Quid Ireland
When England was a pup,
An’ Quid Ireland ’ll be Quid Ireland
When England’s swallowed up."

30th June is 950,0000 z., making the Australian total to
that date 93,620,000 ounces. Say, £875,000,000.

A judicious and interesting effort is being made
to boom a certain “ Mount Taylor ” gold-mine near Bris-
bane. So far, the public hasn’t rushed to any appreciable
extent. Distant hills, somehow, don’t look so green as
they used to—or perhaps the public is getting riper.
