* * * *
Thb Bulletin regrets having circulated an
erroneous statement in last issue about the new
alluvial rush at Garangula, Thb Bullktin is informed
that the leaseholder’s charges were wrongly quoted, the
correct charges beings 10S. per man per month for
each claim after gpld has been struck; no payment
being required prior to gold being struck. No
charge is made for sluicing, but the lease-
holder has to erect and maintain a sluice-box
at his own expense, each owner having the right
to wash his dirt' there. No miner is compelled to
have his carting done by the leaseholder, but if he so
desires it he can compel the leaseholder to do his carting
for Is. per load tor one mile and under; Is. 6d. per load
for any distance exceeding one mile and not exceeding
two miles; for any distance exceeding two miles at a
price to be mutually agreed upon. The only royalty
charged by Mr. Maoaush is 2s. per oz., that being the
royalty which he himself is compelled to pay to the
Crown.


Miss Nellie Stewart has made a great suc-
cess with Aug. W. Juncker’s lovely song, “I was
Dreaming,” introduced by her in “Ma Mie
Rosette.” The publisher has posted to Victoria
fifteen hundred copies of the song during the week.
In spite of an epidemic of waltzes, the “ Duchess
of York” waltz is selling well, W. H. Paling and
00., Ltd., of Sydney and Brisbane, have sold
thousands of copies this year. Write to them,
and forward postal order for two shillings for the
song or the waltz. Four shillings the two; the
prettiest present you can give a lady. The pub-
lisher is Mr. H. J. Samuell, Box 1077, G.PO.,
Sydney.

846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney,
July 10, 1894.
My Dear Mookabinda—
least no sweeping reflections on the sartorial
effects observable in the Land of Wooden Hams
(which, we all know, sticks a feather in its hat
and calls itself Yankee Doodle with most artistic
results) when I solemnly state that I never did
see so queerly-dressed a crowd as that which
advertised its nationality about Sydney streets on
the festive Fourth. I don’t think I will ever for-
get a lady in a costume barred like a zebra’s skin,
and a little s.s. banner flying as if she was a try-
your-weight machine. And another Oolumbienne
in a baggy, virtuous-heroine-looking cloak, with
her hair dyed, and her little flag flying from her
bowsprit, certainly upheld the Goddess of Liberty
—of costume, at least.
And most of the he-Americans whom I met had
on scrunch hats, and wore their hair long, and
wore flag boutonnieres, and one over-proof patriot,
who had evidently been mixing the beverages of
his native land, had a flag for every weskit button.
He looked like the signboard of a “ notion ’ -
shop. Of course, the “real elegant’’ Doodles
kept their eagles in their pockets, and if their
good example induced the other sort to leave their
flag at home it would only be fair to the great
and glorious country they represent. We know
that every American angel whom we entertain isn’t
a song-and-dance lady, but, upon my word, the
number of towy-headed impossibilities who flew
the stars-and-stripes on the Block, last Wednes-
day, looked as if America was one vast variety-
show.
To my idea —perhaps because there really is no
real Australian flag, so far, although the patriotic
“up and arise” bards write as if there is—a
person who hurls his banner at you, in a manner
of speaking, is a most unblushing egotist. “lam
a product of the glorious land of Dash. Gaze
your fill. This is indeed a Dasher,” is what a man
(or eke a woman) who ornaments himself with his
national rag on the least provocation seems to
breathe in every curve. And the more glorious
the country the greater the egotism ! Also, I’m
beginning to think that in this age of carefully-
balanced emotions, when every impulse is analysed
into an absurdity, flags and things like that are
getting played out as vehicles for genuine senti-
ment. So many stage-heroes have died fighting
frantically for the property banner amid a running
fire of caps that the whole business smells of the
footlights.
If women get the franchise in Australia I
predict with my eyes shut that the semi-nude
coryphee will have a serious rival in the he-
woraan. For example, only the other day at a
meeting held to encourage some itinerant shepherd
or other whose golosh had descended upon Sydney,
the president of N.S.W. Woman Suffrage League
said she thought that, in the coming century,
when women took hold of the work of the world,
they would also take off their gloves.
Now it always strikes me (though you musn’t
suppose for a moment that I’m trying to boom
the prospects of Woman Suffrage) that my strong-
minded sisters would peel down to Godiva-esque
proportions if they get the whip-hand. The
first thing a strong-minded woman does—no
matter how fat and wobblesome she may be—is to
cast off her corsets as a rattlesnake slips its skin;
her next step has always been to agitate for a
split in her garments that will show her qualifica-
tions for the ballet more piquantly than petticoats
do, and now, behold ! she threatens that she will
take off her gloves. Shedding her garments as
she goes along the road to Freedom (so’s she may
find her way back by them perhaps, on the
“Babes in the Wood ” principle !) it almost looks
as if my shrieking sisters would arrive at the top
of the poll in their birthday-suits.
Chatting about Woman Suffrage, I think they
must have a prettier and juicier set of political
pusses in boots up North than are those we suffer
from in the South. The other day a Bananaland
M.P. refused to address a W.F. meeting “ because
he never could speak before a lot of giggling
girls.” But I cannot picture a giggling she-
suffragist, can you? In fact, a little more giggling
would do the feminine-political atmosphere further
South heaps of good. In Sydney a W.F. meeting
is a solemn conclave of disappointed-looking ladies
in Vi liculous head-gear and dog’s-eared corners to
their mouths, who generally allude to men as
vipers, but who flutter like canaries with the cat
coming if they can only yard up one of the tyrants
to make pretty speeches from the platform.
“ Giggling girls !” He must have meant giggling
Gorgons.
By the way, one of the most shocking pieces of
disloyalty, not to say treason, I have met with for
years is the latest portrait of Her Gracious
Majesty Queen Victoria in a recent fashionable
London illustrated. H.M. is represented as a lady
the corners of whose mouth are more turned down
than those of any other woman on record. The
very first lesson in physiognomy that should be
learnt by mankind is—never marry a girl—even
a princess—whose mouth-corners turn down.
St. John’s Ambulance Association—founded in
Australia by Lady; Brassey—still keeps its head
in front of the myriad fads of the period, and when
ambulancing is taken up by intelligent people,
who don’t bind up the unbroken leg and leave the
injured member flapping, there is much to be said
in favour of it. But the St. John’s A.A.
enthusiasts go deeper than mere broken bones. At
a recent meeting held at the stuccoed suburb of
Ashfield the medico, who took the floor,
dissected a heart (I) and “ explained the
inner workings of that organ in an interesting
manner!” But if he could have explained the
metaphysical ins and outs of the dead heart on the
table, instead of merely its gristly and gory inter-
E rotation, what a three-volume sensational novel
is scientific pow-wow would have turned to !
The ever-juvenile Bland Holt’s latest piece of
entertaining realism, “A Prodigal Daughter,”
wiled a large and fashionable dress-circle crowd
into Sydney Lyceum on Saturday. A society
theatre-party long-drawn-out (theatre-parties run
in all shapes—some are arranged in hollow squares,
while others are all shop-front, so to speak) was
strung round the front row like a string of beads,
and included a well-known belle in green velvet,
who had draped her pink cloak effectively, only
that the pinkiness and greenishness reminded me
of art-furniture.
Is it an English trick, the habit our superlatives
have of mobbing theatre-entrances on fashionable
first nights as if they were so many lady chamber-
lains watching to see if everybody’s bodice was
sufficiently ventilated to go in ? It is only women
who do this thing, mind you, and I want to beg
them for their own sakes to adopt some other
vantage-ground. To see a flock of women hanging
about the first landing of a theatre while every-
body else is going in looks like the foolish virgins
who turned up to find the S.R.O. board put out,
and it also casts a severe reflection, I think, on the
chivalry and punctuality of the society Johnny.
And there are even worse possibilities attached to
the habit. I hear that two rather flighty-looking
society ladies were doing the usual mysterious first
landing loiter at a Sydney theatre the other night
when they attracted the attention of a Melbourne
stockbroker who had dined unwisely enough to
make him press his escort upon them regardless of
chilling hauteur. But there was some excuse for
the poor man. He perhaps drunkenly mistook
them for a kind of angel who hangs about
Flemington waiting for somebody to smuggle her
in.
Almost the most prodigal thing in a “ Prodigal
Daughter ” was the generous display of heliotrope
hosen afforded by the fetching Henrietta Watson
in her flip-flop in the baccarat scene. If Miss W.
cannot swoon without letting her feet fly heaven-
wards like a bather when his life-buoy slips, she
should fill her little Booklets with duck-shot —a
precaution that leading ladies with more broom-
sticky understandings than the shapely Miss W.
probably never overlook. And I must also rise
and point out that not even Nellie Stewart’s
tricky pink calico slip, which the front stalls mis-
took for her cuticle, was so artful as Miss W.’s
pale-blue tea-gown contrasted with a fleshy colour
interpolated on the bodice in a manner that
made people grab their opera-glasses to see if
the dressmaker had blundered. But calm specu-
lation on this point was impossible in a realistio
whirl of pink-coats, cock-horses, brush-hurdles,
and general superlativeness —the only crude touch
in a wilderness of detail seeming to be the table-
decorations in the first scene, which were reminis-
cent of the showy hash-house where paper-poppies
with arsenic-green calico leaves blossom perenni-
ally on the coffee-stained table-cloth.
I think Mrs. Bland Holt, as Dorcas Gentle, is
about the strongest argument against the equality
of the sexes ever seen on the modern stage. The
whole house smiled when Dorcas came forth in a
check skirt, a fawn covert-coat, pot-hat, collar and
tie, and coolly put three “ monkeys ” on her fancy,
and every smile was a nail in the equality fad.
Then, when Dorcas, in a very ripping set-out of
pink and gold, joins airily in a bachelor baccarat-
party, it is borne in upon the unprejudiced
observer that woman can’t run a dead-heat with
her lord and master and look unimpeachably
respectable at the same time. Indeed, after the
following of the dainty Dorcas to her dreadful
end (in brown brocade and golden beads, with a
jaunty brown hat and feather and boutonniere of
primroses) as a talky Primrose dame, I came to
the conclusion that it isn’t safe for any woman to
model herself on the old man—unless he happens
to be a parson, or she happens to want to be mis-
taken for Mrs. Tanqueray.
The very latest effort of the Sydney Block
jewellers to scare up an ornament boom takes the
shape of the ‘‘Duchess of York brooch,” and I
really must say that of all the unkind reflections
on the—er—sentimental adaptability of her Grace
the Duchess, this little trinket of the moment is
the most suggestive. Read the description with-
out cynically smiling, if you can : It is a double
heart, the outer heart done in gold framework,
while inside depends a glass heart of all colours !
I am sure no ingrained loyalist would ever dream
of wearing it.
St. Vincent’s charity ball (the oldest public
dance left to Sydney now that the School o*
Industry ball has gone under) js to take place in
the Centennial Hall on Aug. 27, and a large
attendance is as much a certainty as that
the tail will follow the dog. And, talking of
charity balls, I hear from a Northern town that
feathers are flying over a hop in aid of the local
Nurses’ Horae. I gather, from information re-
ceived, that the superfine repp committee gave out
with each packet of tickets a list of those impos-
sibly vulgar persons to whom the vendors were on
no account to sell, and as the cards were cheap and
the &ite unplentiful, the unfortunate Nurses’
Home—the catspaw of cheap snobbery—is now
studying how to make itself bailiff-proof, as the
“ gate ” hardly came up to the exes.
By-the-way, it occurs to me in this connection
that the indigent charity in need of a leg up will
some day go back on the high-stepping, but
cheese-paring, female snob who gets a free family
ticket on the strength of talking through her nose
at committee-meetings and voting a guinea more
on to the Governoress’s bouquet. A day will
soon come, Horatio (or whoever you are), when
the Pure Merino, who travels on her visiting-list
as vigorously as Mr. Parkes travels on his old
age, will be voted out of the che-yair and some-
body less superfine and more philosophical hoisted
in her place. It is soothing to have Lady Dash
at the head of the list, but if her presence knocks
figures off the receipts on the eventful evening
conscientious charity committees won’t be able
to afford her.
I am in receipt of three alleged valses, the
“ Ooolgardie,” the “Quarabi” (it ought to be
called the Quisby !), and the “ Silver Moonlight.”
But, what has Australia done that this deluge of
inaccurate, tuneless waltz “ music ” should be
shot here? We are told, in various newspapers.
that N.S.W. is ahead of a lot of places m the
Trin. Coll, musical exams, held at Orne, and that
is probably the reason why we are deafened by
budding Beethovens, who Jglut the market with
shrill and feeble “waltzes” just because they
know enough of theory to keep three beats in a
bar ! Evidently f-time is the alphabet of musical
composition, for everybody begins with a waltz.
Heaven help us when they get more assurance
and begin to fill the country with fugues and Dead
Marches.
New music we have always with us nowadays
but one doesn’t often receive so promising a com-
position as the “Orescent” waltz, by Emile Lud-
low and dedicated to Essie Jenyns. The chief
motive is eccentric and original—-indeed the
whole waltz is more weird that pretty. Never-
theless it will take. In the same parcel with the
“ Crescent ” comes a comic song—though I don t
suppose the composer meant it to be considered
so. It is called “Pleasant Memories,” probably
because it would be so much more agreeable to
remember than actively listen to.
Yours affectionately, Sappho Smith.

If Luke, the evangelist, had only in-
serted a horse-race in his narrative of the son who
went and “blewed” his substance in a far
country, then his story and Bland Holt’s “ Pro-
digal Daughter ” would have been substantially
the same. Also, they would have had the same
moral, saving the fact that the old man of the
Scripture hadn’t been reconstructed during his
offspring’s absence, and so didn’t present a melan-
choly warning to boomers and speculators. The
Lyceum prodigal, who is represented by Miss
Henrietta Watson, is a maiden with an unaccount-
able love for a weak-looking person with a pale
face and flabby figure; and, like most of her kind,
she presently comes down to the husks, which
are Mr, Cosgrove, and the swine, which are Mr.
W. E. Baker. The swine, in this case, is a fine,
manly villain, who plays his part admirably;
while the husks are a dull and lamentable failure.
Miss Watson, who is an exceedingly nice girl and
deserves a better fate, flies with the flabby-looking
person because her father refuses to consent to
their marriage, and they live in splendid infamy
in a hotel until he goes broke and deserts her.
Then she waits patiently for his return, but as he
doesn’t return she goes back to her parent and is
forgiven, and the flabby one is forgiven, and Sass,
who hasn’t done anything, is forgiven, and every-
body is forgiven except Mr. Baker, who is cap-
tured by a policeman as he flies through the
garden gate.
# * *
This story fills three acts, and the other
two are occupied by sundry horse-scenes and the
most realistic steeplechase ever staged in Sydney.
The audience gets at least three times the usual
amount of horse for its money, for the steeple-
chasers tear furiously past the footlights on three
occasions. Also, two welshers are ducked by the
infuriated mob, and one jockey is killed. These
cheerful episodes make up for a multitude of sins,
and, as a matter of fact, there are less than the
usual number of melodramatic sins in the “ Pro-
digal Daughter.” The most glaring absurdity in
the whole proceeding is the spectacle of a lady of
Miss Watson’s physique and attractions wallowing
piteously at the feet of the weedy-looking person
aforementioned, and asking him not to leave
her. The exceeding perpendicularness of Sass,
who has never bent himself anywhere since Thk
Bulletin first knew him, is also somewhat
oppressive ; Sass, in the present case, makes love
in an absolutely straight line, and even in his
most impassioned moments he doesn’t deviate an
inch from his vertical attitude. Also, there is a
gruesome warning to persons about to marry in
the experiences of a flushed lord, personified by
Mr. Leopold, who marries a quiet little Quakeress,
which Quakeress is afterwards found dressed in a
loud-green check, and betting three monkeys on a
horse-race. This lady, represented by the pretty
Mrs. Bland Holt, is also extracted, with difficulty,
from a gambling-house, and takes to whooping in
a frightful manner at elections. Perhaps the most
unobtrusive feature of the play is the gentlemanly
manner in which the old man receives his lost
daughter without asking any questions ; and the
next most unobtrusive feature is Bland Holt him-
self, who is a stable-boy, and is principally occu-
pied in making good resolutions in the third act.
Miss Harrie Ireland has little to do, and does it
nicely, as is her wont, and a number of other per-
sons conduct themselves respectably.
** * *
The weepful story of “Uncle Tom’s Cabin
will be staged on Saturday at Her Majesty’s. I fc
is a considerable time since Sydney had a good
substantial weep, and as it is about due for
another, the persecuted African and the mournful
Eva, who is borne by paper angels to a mansion
in the skies, come in just where they are wanted.
Every effort is being made, in the way of scenery
and mechanical effects, to put on the poor, down-
trodden nigger in the most complete and artistic
fashion, and the horrors of cheap coloured labour
will stand out in the limelight with startling vivid-
ness.
The Shakespearian comedy at Sydney
Criterion gives up the struggle with adverse cir-
cumstances this week, and on Saturday an effort
will be made to improve the fortunes of the house
with Taylor’s “Unequal Match.” If the out-
raged ghost of the Divine Bill is not brooding
over the premises the attempt may be successful,
and as the bard has probably done Uie company
almost as much injury as he has received from it,
there is no visible reason why his apparition
should be present on the occasion.
** * *
Brough and Boucicault opened in Brisbane
with “The Sportsman,” by Lestocq, being the
very first performance thereof in Australia. It is
probably the only time in its history that the
dreary city on the mud flats has been favoured
with a “first performance” of the kind, and the
compliment resulted in great business. _ The
“sportsman,” represented by Cecil VVard, is the
samegood old beaming ass, who tells his wife that
he is going out for a day’s shooting, and then goes
on the tear, and finally returns with a decayed
Cochin China fowl as proof of his honafidcs. '1 lie
idea and the fowl are both old, but the treatment
is tolerably new. Every one has a reasonably
good partin the “Sportsman,” except Boucicault,
who is picked up and indignantly thrown away by
Mrs, Brough, and Miss Dudley, whose large,
frightened-looking eyes stare every one out of
countenance as a housemaid.
** * *
“Mr. Brough and Mr. Boucicault ” have
been playing to S.R.O. in all parts of Brisbane
Opera House, except the d.c. Twice during the
week Mr. B. and Mr. B. have been V.-R., and
have consequently had to play to chilly houses.
The average Brisbane pit is rowdy, but not this
time. On Thursday a row broke out during the
sanctuary scene in “The Village Priest,” and
Titheradge stopped short until dead silence pre-
vailed, following a burst of applause.

bane Royal in exchange for a season at the Opera

Maggie Moore has been turning money away at
Rockhampton and Townsville,
“ A King of Crime,” recently from Syd-
ney, is writing his name in blood upon the classic
boards of Melbourne Royal. The king now ap-
pears to be Charles Holloway, and the penalty of
interfering with plain Bill’s relative is a violent
decease. He is a doting husband, and a staunoli
friend to any right-minded burglar, but he’s rough
on rats who communicate with the police, and a
terror to misers who can’t bear parting with the
cash-box. All vermin of that sort the king chokes,
or otherwise extinguishes, in a highly popular
manner. Indeed, the King, regarded as a work of
dramatic literature, is sixteen times more immoral
than anything seized by the Government thus far,
not excepting Ovid’s “Art of Love.” Another
wicked idol of the masses is an unkempt hag, who
waves an abbreviated clothes-prop over the king’s
proceedings, as though she were a pantomime
fairy. This old remnant of iniquity is ably imper-
sonated by Miss Julia Merton, and the gallery
hails her as “ good old mother.” J. W. Sweeney
plays the comic mesmerist, who strikes venerable
Jimmy Cathcart stiff. Miss Maud Williamson
is a graceful blind wife to the sanguinary hero ;
and the other people are liberally placed upon the
stage. “ A King of Crime” will run till further
notice unless the Social Purity Society obtains an
injunction.

The last nights of “ Ma Mie Rosette,” at
the Princess’s, promise to linger for another week
or so. One visit to this unusual opera, the joint
offspring of many authors and librettists, excites
a warm desire to go again and enjoy it some more.
The voluptuous odour of Henri’s unhallowed
passion, intermingled with the sweet perfume of
Nellie Stewart’s pure rural love, is very intoxi-
cating. Figuratively speaking, no man has lived
until he has inhaled the fragrance of Nellie’s
naughty vision and gazed upon that timid flutter-
ing figure in the robe de nuit. For the ladies
of the audience there are Brownlow’s legs
arranged in a constant succession of amorous
(“ Sundry Shows' ’ continued on page 16.)
A gory joke is the King of Crime,
0, a gallery hero gay :
For he scrags a bloke from time to time
In a frank and honest way.
He rears his head in the limelight red ;
And the barrackers hold their breath
When he points a claw from his thieving paw
To bid the traitor, who broke his law,
Prepare for a midnight death,
For a horrible midnight death.
His Nibs, at the least, would scorn a lie —
Yea, a truthful fiend is he;
The trembling beast that he dooms to die
Has certainly got to dee.
Hark ! the midnight bell tolls forth his knell!
There’s blood on each rusty note ;
Two hands appear from an arbour near
And (merrily now the small boys cheer)
Lay hold of the traitor’s throat,
Of the gurgling traitor’s throat.
This murderous thief is slain, alas !
Yet he like a soldier falls ;
And he comes to grief as bold as brass.
Though riddled by rifle balls.
Then he flits from life to his dear, blind wife,
That deeply-lamented “ tart ”;
And angels sing as he plumes his wing—
“ Forgive the sins of the crimey King,
Because of his tender heart,
His deliciously tender ’art.”

Governor proclaimed the new province. One
Bates, who was living on the island before the
vessel arrived, is, however, still in the flesh.

at all some nights. Every exalted person has his
parasites.

Solicitor Meagher, of Sydney, has lately
defended, or instructed in the defence, of five
capitally-arraigned criminals. Archer was hanged,
Montgomery and Williams ditto, and now
Cummins and his mate, Lee, are to swing. But
nearly all were practically hopeless cases from the
first.

of the enemies of the Government to make capital
out of the incidents surrounding his illness and

Wm. Kemp, the embezzling secretary of
Vic. Tender Board, goes to gaol for five years
with periods of “solitary.” Had Kemp kept
honest for two years more he might have retired
on a pension of £240 a year. As it was he went
straight for 25 years, and then sold himself for a
mess of pottage.
When the late Morgan Augustus M‘Don-
nell, once Vic. Attorney-General, was leaving
London for Melbourne his friend, R.C. Bishop
Grant, thus addressed him : “ Don’t take a brief
for the dirty divorce-court, and when you cross-
examine a witness watch his mouth as the old
Romans did. Good-bye ! ”
The deceased Victorian millionaire, Silas
Harding, did at least one memorable action in his
dreary, miserable, and utterly sordid existence.
Many years ago he got a judgment against a
railway company, and in satisfaction thereof
he seized the Geelong train as it was leaving
Spencer-street station full of passengers. His
later years were mostly spent in efforts to dodge
the land-tax, and with this end in view he made a
nominal transfer of a lot of valuable property to
sundry relatives. One of the relatives stuck to his
share of the property, and the Government made
Silas pay the tax all the same, so the miserable
old man was hit on both ears at once.

Last time a man named Robert Louis
Stevenson (who wears long hair and a velvet coat
and writes novels) visited Sydney, a hasty dinner
in his honour was got up in the Cosmopolitan
Club (now defunct, and no wonder). The Presi-
dent of the Club on that occasion delivered the
following speech : —“Gentlemen, it is arrived that
I have the honneur to propose the health—
naturally—of our guest, Mr. Robair Louis Steven-
son. When I was asked to take the chair I said
to my friend, ‘ But, good gracious, Ido not know
Mr. Stevenson.’ He said, ‘He write novels.’ I
went and ask&d for the novels, and I stopped up
last night to read one, absolutely. It was ver’
good. Truly I did not make to undairstand it all,
because it was in Scotch. But I think it is truly
very good very excellent. And therefore,
gentlemen, I ask you to drink with me, naturally,
the health of Mistair Stevenson now that I know
who he is.”
Because she has been silly enough to write
a book—a diary of half-formed opinions concern-
ing her travel-glimpses in N. America, Australia
and Maoriland—the Sydney dailies style the
Duchess of Buckingham and Chandos a “ dis-
tinguished visitor.” But the lady is not distin-
guished. She is only a swell tramp.
In Sydney, whenever a press-boss is at a
dinner or other “function,” the reporter finds it
necessary to include him in the list of “ eminent ”
or “ distinguished ” men present. Now, if a Melb.
reporter committed the gross indecency of men-
tioning the editor or owner of his paper as being
present at a gathering he would be looked on as
hopelessly incompetent.
Maoriland Premier Seddon at one time
kept a pub., and it is an open secret that even
now he has an interest in the same grog-mill. An
enemy of his relates that one evening, recently,
while the Premier dozed in the lobby, the affairs of
the house_(the public-house) seemed to come upper-
most in his mind, and he was heard to murmur, in
his dreams, “Charley, is the lamp all right? Don’t
give the new euchre-pack to that rough crowd in
the front room.”
The late John Bray, despite his good
nature, used to sit hard down on anybody who
interrupted his oratory. Once he was wading
through a long list of new Customs proposals
when a chronically-inebriated member demanded
to know, in a husky voice : “ What about tea ?”
“Tea,” said the beaming Johnny; “1 don’t
think the hon. member knows much about tea.
Wait till I get down among the W’s, and then
he’ll find something to interest him,”
A dead-beat waited upon the benevolent
and Herculean barrister Field, at the latter’s
chambers in Sydney, the other morning, with a
pitiful tale. The result was half-a-crown. Some-
thing in the man’s manner having aroused the
barrister’s^ suspicions, he followed him, and found
him dodging into the corner pub. With a hop,
step and jump the lawyer entered the bar, seized
the half-crown, which lay upon the counter, and
triumphantly returned to his chambers.
Boss Orangeman Simon Fraser (the “Aus-
tralian gentleman ” whose portrait was painted by
Millais), one of the trio representing Victoria at
Ottawa Conference, happened to travel to Rome
some years back in company with Martin Lough-
lin, who held influential letters of introduction to
the Vatican authorities, and showed his Orange
friend the religious grandeurs of the Eternal City.
Simon acted as a most devout pilgrim, and
reverentially kissed the hands of many cardinals.
The death of squatter George Melrose gave
the S.A. Treasurer .£15,000 in succession duty.
Had the estate not been divided among eight
children the duty would have been much larger,
as the progressive tax is collected upon individual
bequests, while wife, descendants, and ancestors
pay special rates. When the octogenarian mil-
lionaire Elder, G.C.M.G., dies, the State will reap
a harvest—say £BO,OOO. Mr. Elder is a bachelor,
and has no immediate relatives to come in cheap.
The late Judge “ Tommy ” Cope, of Mel-
bourne, had got into such a habit of writing short
pithy sentences in his note-book, that when he
wrote sketches or reminiscences (as he sometimes
did) the style was like “leaves from a Judge’s
note-book.” For instance (a verbatim transcript):
“ When I landed, in ’52, I took cheap lodgings at
Prahran. My boots were not cleaned for me, and
I couldn’t clean them myself. When I went out I
stepped into a puddle. They then looked as if I
had had a long walk.”
When the last Parkes Ministry resigned,
the old man sat in the N.S.W. Secretary’s office
awaiting the arrival of Dibbs—who, by the way,
didn’t turn up to ceremoniously see his predecessor
off the premises. While the ancient waited, a
gossip told him that Kidd was to succeed O’Con-
nor as Postmaster-General. “Ah,” said Parkes,
“Hi ham not surprised. It is very proper that a
Kidd should follow a Goat!” From which it is
plain that Parkes has a high opinion of politician
O'Connor, and is grateful for the gigantic slaver
performed by Daniel in naming the big Sydney
Post-office clock bell “ Great Parkes !”
Charles Wyndham, the “pretty,” though
clever London society actor, once, after much
solicitation, sent his autograph to a lady, now of
Sydney, who still possesses the “ Better late than
never.—Yours, Charles Wyndham,” forwarded
in answer to her gushing request. One night the
lady boasted of her prize to some young lady
friends, also Wyndham-gushers, and found that
he had sent the same charming, apparently-im-
promptu—but evidently stock—sentence “Better
late than never, etc.,” to them all. Amidst a
chorus of “ Did he send you the same, too,
dears?” it was admitted that the shock had
cured the whole pack of gushers. Not once in a
thousand times has a mummer any more origin-
ality than you find in the average chimpanzee.
The rapid though deserved promotion of
Charles Russell, Q.C., first as a Judge of Appeal,
and now to the position of Lord Chief Justice of
England, seems breathful of good luck. Though
perhaps a score of years ago, it appears like yester-
day to those who knew Russell when he was a
young, shy barrister, not overburdened with briefs,
and addicted to the wild recklessness of gambling
for such stakes as were within his means. A partial
parallel is perhaps found in the career of Mr.
Way, 0.J., and Lieut.-Governor of S.A. When
Sammy took to politics he was certainly a Q.C.,
but by no means a leader of the Bar in the popular
estimate. That was in Feb. ’75, and, in June
following, Premier Boucaut (now a Judge) made
him Attorney-General. About that time Judge
Wearing was drowned in the Gothenburg, but
Mr. Way didn’t snap at that vacancy. Not he !
He earned all-round commendations for his un-
selfishness in appointing to it Randolph Stow,
Q.C., the loader of the Adelaide Bar. On March
4th, ’76, Chief-Justice Hanson died, and Way
calmly appropriated his mantle. Probably
Boucaut could have done it, but he didn’t take it.
Then, Way being securely elevated for life, the
Boucaut Government came down smash three
months later. Boucaut became Premier again in
’77, and was glad to seek the haven of a pusine
Judgeship in ’7B—under his old subordinate and
junior at the Bar. Some men have great luck.
Shiels’ to-hell-with-the-Pope bimetallic
joke was magnanimously tolerated by the rev.
fathers, though some of the tender R.C. flock
writhed in the Melb. press. Shiels can never make

testant though he is, he attended a recent lecture

Catholics, and in the course of a speech said :
“ Although I have not attained to the dignity of a
spiritual father I have, by good fortune and other
efforts, become the father of three children with
Irish names.”
HERE’S
THE “DIBBSOMANIAC.”
N.S.W. Premier Dibbs told the Bathurst
people that he had to give up business when he
entered Parliament. The words are neither more
nor less than those used by most men who have
made a permanent mark on the Parliamentary
history of their time. But what a clinching
argument they furnish in the present state of
controversy on the question of Payment of Mem-
bers ! If masters —and Dibbs and those who thus
spoke before him were employers of labour —were
compelled to surrender their businesses or abridge
their Legislative usefulness, how much greater is
this compulsion on the man who is merely in
another’s employment?
G. H. Reid is understood to have in view
for N.S.W. Treasurer, in his still-to-be-boru
administration, a politician who has not handled
figures in Parliament before. But, like Treas.
See, the person has kept shop and done a deal of
sharebroking. Anyway, Bruce Smith is out of it,
M'Millan won’t, it is said, be allowed to spend
more than a given amount of time away from his
warehouse, and confident little Burns is likely to
get plucked ; so that a fresh face in the Treasury
will be almost a necessity. Parkes was acting-
Treasurer twice in his long life, but only for a few
days on each occasion, and he says he won’t take
it.
John M‘Elhone’s relation to the N.S.W.
politics of the day may be thus briefly expressed :
“ When I went into Parliament before, I was as
strong as a bull and as mad as one. Let me in
again.” One good thing John did, however,
while in the House. A windy orator was one
night yelling on the floor a speech written Ly
somebody, which he had imperfectly committed
to memory, and had frequent recourse to the MS.
in his hind coat-pocket. When everybody was
sick and tired, but disallowed by the rules of the
House to throw an inkbottle or a ruler at the
orator, John slipped up behind (not like a bull
that time) and adroitly extracted the MS.
Half a minute after the orator broke down and an
afflicted audience thanked God.
Probably the most horrible example of
misplaced candour on record appeared in the S.M.
Herald last week. Speaking of the King divi-
sion of Sydney that paper said: “On the other
hand, there are in the same district . . . quite
a phenomenal number of pawnbrokers who, being
interested in unrestricted commerce, will probably
vote for the Freetrade candidate.” Of course the
three-ball artist will vote for Freetrade. Free-
trade produces poverty, and the Israelite whose
burnished nose looks out from beneath the gilded
arms of Lombardy, lives and moves and has his
monish by means of that same poverty. The
cause which receives the enthusiastic support of
the pawnbroking industry is always a thing to be
viewed with suspicion.
There appears to be a conspiracy between
the N.S.W. Opposition leaders and the Opposi-
tion daily press to regard “ Tom ” Slattery as a
great and masterful genius for intrigue, as the
mysterious power behind the throne in N.S.W.,
and the secret of Dibbs’ greatness. This is a very
good fiction for stage and election purposes, but
would hardly gain credence in any community in
which there was not a section which welcomed a
sectarian scare as part of its political life-breath.
Being merely a commonplace man, without those
large esoteric powers, Slattery is known to be
immensely flattered by the rumours of his remark-
able doings, which are whispered in awful whispers
in the trembling editorial ear. It became known
afterwards to David Gopperfield that the “in-
exorable Mr. Jorkins ” was a mild, little man,
whose terrible reputation was a fiction of his
partner, Mr. Spenlow ; Tom, of Burrowa, is
the Jorkins of N.S.W. politics.

“That person, Dibbs.”— Parkes.
Adelaide single-taxers advise George H.
Reid to Read H. George and go in top.
In 58 years S.A. has passed 1438 Acts of
Parliament, of which 400 have been repealed.
Victoria is going to give bonuses to en-
courage the local manufacture of vegetable oils.
Palm-oil?
“The moneylenders, the lawyers, and
thieves in general are Parliament.” Billy
Maloney.
There is talk of establishing another Con-
servative daily in Adelaide. The Register is
accused of wobbling.
“ Queensland politics are machine-made-
turned out to order, in fact, when the banks and
the mortgage companies touch the spring.”—
Parkes.
Single-taxers have now a “red van”
touring the Vic. country districts. Cornwall,
Melb. solicitor, away with it, holiday-making and
lecturing.
“ The Treasurer,” says Melb. Herald,
“is delayed at Sandhurst. He is suffering from a
rather severe chest affection.” Theodore Hook’s
joke is immortal.
The time of troublous agitation, political
and industrial, is that “darkest hour” that pro-
verbially precedes the dawn. Thus Hope is born
by the parturition of Despair.
After all their years of exaggerated
emotions, the N.S.W. liquor-prohibitionists are
absolutely kicked aside in the present electoral
contest by the three big parties.
The Q. police, called out for the ’9l strike,
are to be again sent to “ the seat of war.” Mean-
while they have not to this day received the extra
expenses due from the last campaign.
Parkes : “ Hi ham quite prepared to fight
that battle (Freetrade) when we ’ave hentered
hinto a grand Hostralian union.” Everlasting
Parkes ! the man of the past, present, and to
come.

‘look at me now !’ We’ve heard some of them
say that. But now they can’t be looked at without
an order from the Governor of Pentridge.”—
Trenwith,
A journalist declined lately to accept the
editorship of an Australian committee-managed
paper unless he were allowed to reject on his dis-
cretion the letters and articles sent in by members
of the committee.
Agent-General Playford has been shocking
London society by affirming that S.A. will soon
insist upon electing its own Governor. He pro-
phesies that brewer Smith, squatter Barr Smith,
or Chief Justice Way will be selected.
One of the
few remarkable
things about that
uninteresting de-
funct politician,
D. M. Davies, of
Victoria, was the
eulogy which
Shiels pronounced
over his dust in
the Assembly.
The long, thin
man was in a
desolate mood and
wailed consider-
ably concerning
things in general.
“Counting up its
moments of plea-
sure against its
daysof darkness,”
he said ; “ reckon -
in g its joys
against its sor-
rows, no man of
sense would vol-
untarily launch
his own harque
upon the sea of
this life.” Which
is a long and de-
pressed way of
saying that Shiels
is sorry he was
ever born. His
creed is practi-
cally this: it is
good to be; it is
better to cease
from being; it is
best of all never
to have been. But
Shiels doesn’t sui-
cide, all the same,
and whoever kills
him will have to
climb over his
dead body to do it.
The man who tries
to kill a pro-
fessional pessimist
generally has a
bad time.

The N.S.W. Protectionists go to the polls
with three knights—Manning, Abbott and Dibbs;
the Freetraders with one—Parkes. Were Jen
nings’ health good he would count with the Free-
traders, though most of his personal friends are

Conference in Melbourne: “As a staunch conser-
vative I can only support one principle of electing
representatives one-man-one-vote. “As a
staunch conservative ! ” Well, what’s in a name
if the heart’s Holroyd ? ”
Although there are six Labour-inember 8
receiving .£2OO each in the S.A. Council, the
Labour-party desires to abolish that body._ If it
progresses at future elections as it has previously
done, it will soon have power enough to vote the
Council out of existence.
The whole of the old N.S.W. labour mem-
bers have been declared “bogus” (by the crowd
which has appointed itself to replace them) just as
they were getting their political wisdom-teeth. Is
Democracy always going to drown its own pups
before their eyes are open !
A Sydney merchant asserts that a testi-
monial from a M.P. is more against than for the
success of an applicant for employment in com-
mercial circles, and thus indulgently excuses the
persistence with which members dog Government
for billets for their friends and supporters.
Mr. Henry Parkes, who can, when he
chooses, drink as much at a sitting as any man in
N.S. Wales, without showing the slightest evil
effects, is, according to Sydney Telegraph, to be
supported by the solid hop-beer vote at St,
Leonards. Hop-beer and Parkes ! What a world
this is I
S.A. Govt, intends further reducing public
salaries from 6 to 25 cent. Agent-General Play-
ford is to come down from .£l5OO to ,£1230 -a
figure which ought to cover the representation in
England of all Australia. But until Federation
we shall continue to keep many snug roosts for
disabled politicians.
The proposal to charge a license of £2 14s.
per year to women for the privilege of wearing
trousers, as is the case in France, was informally
submitted to B. R. Wise a few days ago as a
means whereby, in addition to taxes on narcotics
and stimulants, the deficit in the N.S.W. revenue
of the future might be met.
Lawyer Dully, M.L.A., informing the Yic.
Assembly that departmental regulations must
always be in perfect harmony with the letter of
“the Act,” was reminded by Treasurer Carter
that Duffy pere had no difficulty in receiving his
pension in London, notwithstanding that the Act
made it payable in Melbourne.
When S.A. Labour-members were elected
in ’93 they had to fight the Civil-Service vote in
every district. Now the Labour M’s. P. stand
alone in fighting against Civil-Service mutilation.
The C.S. snob, always a Conservative, finds that
Asa politicians don’t care a rap for him after
all, but vote for a common Labah-membah, oh,
no!
South Australia has, for over 30 years,
urged Victoria to settle the disputed boundary
question, but Vic. Govts, have successfully dodged
the inevitable. Premier Kingston has been urged
to force a settlement by assuming legal control
of the strip of land in dispute, but this may mean
£50,000 in litigation. Victoria refuses to arbi-
trate.

The anti-bribery clauses of N.S. Wales
Electoral Act appear to have been inspired by
Scotch ideas. A Parliamentary candidate said
t | (e ot | ier day at a hotel bar, while putting down
his sixpence, each of his companions also deposit-
i„g a co i n> that it reminded him of the time he
was member of Queensland Johnsonian club—an

were peculiarly Scottish,
The chief feature of next week’s N.S.W.
elections will be the prevalence of voting accord-
ing to dictation. Labour started the idea, and it
has been imitated by both the protectionists and
foreign-raiders. The wrath of some of the un-
selected candidates is fearful to behold, and most
of them will take their revenge out by going to
the poll and splitting the vote of their own party.
0. F. Langier, Enfield, Vic., has hatched
a scheme for the representation of minorities in
Parliament. It is that rejected candidates who
have polled over one-third of the votes registered
by the lowest successful members should be ad-
mitted to the House, allowed to speak for a
limited time, and to cast a vote of the relative
value of the electoral power represented by them.

A DWELLER FROM AFAR.
WANDERING SALLY : “ I’m no vagrant, constable. I have a good home.
My home is beyond the grave!”
TROOPER DELANEY: “Faith yez can’t hev me that way. I n[?]ver saw
yer face before, an’ I hav lived the lasht four year beyant
the cemetery above.”
When the Ballance-Seddon Ministry took
office in Maoriland the net public debt was .£59
15s. per head ; now it is £57 9s. The interest
charge has also decreased from GOs. per annum for
each inhabitant, to 555. This is the result of three
years of democratic government—a result such as
no other province has produced since borrowing
first began in this debt-laden land of Australasia.
One Westralian correspondent affirms that
the Perth meat-ring, which keeps mutton and
beef up to 7d. and Bd. per lb., has Ministerial
tentacles —whence the stock-tax ; and goes on to
hint that more than one member of the “ six
families” is directly benefited by the Govt, rail-
way from Southern Cross to Northam, completed,
working, paying and—left in the contractor’s
hands till September next.
At the capitalistic meeting in Sydney Cen-
tenary Hall, last week, one speaker claimed that
the province was his debtor because he had
had 22 children born in it, and all still
alive. At that meeting, by the way, ad-
mission was on ticket. Yet Teetotal - lecturer
Isitt had said in the same hall a few days before,
“It must be a bad cause that can't trust itself to
the people.” Appropriately enough, the fat
policeman who does duty at N.S.W. Legislative
Assembly kept the outer door.
Though the Australian Natives Association
in Adelaide languishes and looks like death,
natives themselves are in the ascendancy in
local politics. The President and Chairman
of Committees in the Legislative Council
are both South Australians, while the Speaker,
the Premier, the Treasurer, the Commissioner of
Crown Lands, the Government Whip, and the
Leader of the Opposition in the Assembly also
claim this continent as their birth-place.
The latest N.S. W. Electoral Act provide B
that every candidate for Parliament must possess
an elector’s right, and must personally sign the
nomination paper. On account of his being dis-
qualified as to both these requirements, by reason
of absence in England, Tiger Inglis, by his local
agent, has withdrawn his candidature for Armi-
dale. If Inglis is thus disqualified, what about
Minister Suttor, who is at present in Canada?
Did he sign his nomination-paper in advance?
The Vic. Civil Service Association has the
folly to issue a pronunciamento to the effect that
the members will not submit to further reductions,
but, of course, everyone knows they will. Let
them strike, and see how many thousands of men,
of equal ability, are ready to replace them at half
the figure. The former fulmination of the Service
on the eve of the general election, in a circular to
the candidates, threatening them with the power
of 30,000 votes, had the same effect as R.O. Arch-
bishop Gould’s Pastoral before the Education Act.
Shiels, as Premier, was furious when he found it
out, and there were dire things to be done, but the
specific offenders could never be traced.
Specimen of heckling as done in Banana-
land on the unfortunate capitalistic M.L. A. ques-
tion ; “Are you in favour of one-man-one-vote ?”
M.L.A. : “No! not just at present.” (Howl of
indignation from the audience). “Do you believe
in Socialism in our time?” M.L. A. (timorously):
“Well —No not quite.” (Terrific uproar).
“ Will you, if allowed, make a present of your
150-acre paddock, just outside the town, to" the
municipality, provided it be christened Eat Man’s
Park?” M.L. A., eagerly, “O, certainly; with
pleasure.” And the free and independent dis-
perse, richer by a public park, to get their names
on the roll, and be prepared to vote to a man for
he Labour candidate at the next election.
The commonest gibe against ex-Labour-
members is : “What about that .£3OO a year?”
Coining as it does from men who can only bo
represented while payment of members lasts, this
is a strange proof that the feeling against the man
who draws the salary is always intensified when
he is poor, and mitigated when he is rich. To
him who has much, more is not begrudged.

obnoxious question, Parkes exclaimed from the
platform—“Hi ham, hinformed that ha hinmate
hescaped from Gladesville Asylum this morning.”
Parkes relied on the ladies he had stuffed into the
gallery to restrain his audience from violent
phases of rudeness.

has been the abolition—by 27 votes to 24 of the
sale of grog in the refreshment-room of the Hons ?

Leaders of old political parties in Queens-
land call him a good party man who, on all
occasions when ordered by his chief, votes to turn
the other side out of office; but all these old
politicians contend that it is the duty of fail our
members to vote only as their consciences dictate,
and that when they subordinate the merits of any
question to instructions from another, they piove
their unfitness for responsible jtositions. The
Bulletin for years has advocated the abolition of
Party Government.
Now that a new set of N.S.W. Labour-
members has almost pushed out the former crowd,
it must be about time to organise the set which
will, in the course of nature, push them out. How
many times will Labour change its seasoned
political leather for the raw greenhide before it
tumbles to the lunacy of the thing? During the
Commune in Paris, the old executive was regu-
larly taken out and shot before breakfast every
morning, while the new executive swaggered
about for the ensuing 24 hours. And yet the
Commune was a failure !
The Bulletin in advance declines with
malice aforethought to recognise the next N.S.W.
Parliament—which will probably be elected by
one-third or less of the voters —as representing
anybody or anything definite or special. There
are an incalculable number of issues, a multiplicity
of parties, a superfluity of programmes, and a per-
fect maelstrom generally. The 6nly hopeful
possibility seems to be that immediately after the
election both Parkes and Dibbs will be “ elevated”
to the Legislative Council dustbin.
Sydney D.T., and the Reid and M‘Millan
school of politicians, base their electioneering
attitudes on the assumption that the people of
N.S. Wales form “ a province of freetraders, who
have had protection forced upon them by a com-
bination of accident and treachery.” Any para-
dqx is provable if the pretended logician starts
with an artificial and false assumption, and this
freetrade lie is as magnitudinous in its way as is
the Anglo-Saxon orthodoxy that John Bull is
the salt of the earth, the light of the; heathen,
the boss of all nations, the rightful possessor of
the planet, the mortgagee of Heaven, and the
favourite son of God.

At the request of some of the clergy, Dean
Cowper has prepared the accompanying form
of prayer, to he used in divine service In An-
glican Churches from to-morrow until the
close of the'elections:—“Almighty God, who
by Thy divine Providence ordereth all things
both in heaven and earth, and hast taught
us in Thy holy word that by righteousness
nations prosper, we humbly beseech Thee so
to influence the minds of the people of this,
colony at the present time, that they may
choose as legislators meu who fear Thee, who
love truth and do righteously. Grant this,
we beseech Thee, for the sake of Jesps .Christ,:
our Lord. . Atoen.”

Society
A damp family grave tells no tales after a
time. Substantial coffins with adult corpse en-
closed have been known to disappear amidst the
moisture of 20 years, leaving no trace of the in-
terment save a little ball of human Lair powdered
with wood-dust. Had the late Mr. Needle and
his two children found rest in a low-lying spot at
Booroondara (Melb.) cemetery it would have
seemed almost useless to exhume their bodies,
since arsenic, though indestructible, is soluble in
water. Presuming that there was any poison in
them once, it would, in all probability, have been
washed away ere now from their rapidly vanish-
ing remains. ■ It appears, however, that they were
deposited in a very high and dry place, therefore
analyst Blackett expects to find arsenic, if any
existed in them at time of burial. An evident
moral is pointed by these particulars. When an
elderly gentleman purchases a young and amorous
wife he should invest in a well-drained grave-plot
at the same time, nor forget to impress upon his
spouse the advantages of a dry bed in suspicious
cases. Better still to build a vault and direct his
coffin to be placed on the top shelf, for any
ordinary grave, after all, is at the mercy of a
watering-pot. When you see a buxom widow
pouring quarts of water over a sandy allotment
she may be ministering to the shrubs and flowers,
but she also may be doing her level best to disWve
the arsenic below. A vault is safest.

On the occasion of the visit of the
Bacchante to Melb., in 1881, one gleeful and
grammarless groveller sent a couple of gilt-edged
superfine invitations to the “young princes” to
attend a dinner-party at his private house. An
acceptance came in due course, and the slobber-
some one travelled round to all his friends in a
semi-hysterical condition spreading the glad
tidings, and finally brought up at a furniture-shop
where he ordered, regardless of expense, two
ormulu and gold-framed chairs with red plush
backs and the Royal arms emblazoned in seven
colours. But, 10, when the time arrived, no
princes turned up, and the solitary second-class
aide-de-camp, who rolled in at half-past nine, ex-
plained, haughtily, in reply to enquiries, that it
was etiquette for Royalty always to accept. Then
he raised his single eye-glass, and glancing round,
muttered that the whole show was a “ ter-
rible boah,” and left. The host’s loyalty has
somewhat decreased since that date, and two
shrieking theatrical unsaleable chairs, stowed
away in a lumber-room, are about all that remain
of that intangible virtue at the present day.

Rev. Evans, of Middle Park, Melb., has
been preaching in praise of billiards. “ Why not
have billiard-tables in the home,” lie asks, where
the fathers and sons can play without the evil
associations that usually accompany the game '
Why not have a ballet in the drawing-room, also
linker parties-for buttons—and combats between
Bandy Mick and the Brighton Bantam? We
don’t know, only that men, fathers and sons don t
care a Fink for these things without the alleged
“evil associations.” “Evil associations,’ alas,
are the salt of life.
A double-fronted fehop in Collins-street,
Melb.. is now let to two people ; in one window
are monumental figures of the Virgin and Child,
and in the other are specimens of a new patent
American corset.

This is the Book of Genesis in leprosy as
firmly believed in the far north : —Act I.—The
Chinese discover a leprous brother and hurl him
forth into the outer darkness of the back blocks.
Act II. —The aboriginal, touched with com-
passion, embraceth the outcast, leadeth him to
camp, and taketh him in (fricasseed). Act 111. —
A horde of leprous aboriginals rot piecemeal by the
waterholes of Carpentaria. And so the leaven
spreadeth even from Hong-Kong to Diamantina.

Bishop Dawes, of Rockhampton, tells the
following story with much gusto : —Some time ago
a lady parishioner of the Anglican church in the
city of sin, sweat, and sorrow, who intended to
get married, communicated her intention to the
incumbent who was then in office. After giving
the necessary particulars in writing, she_ came
to the heading, “condition” (widow or single).
Now, the lady was somewhat well-favoured and of
roomy dimensions. “ Condition ?” said she, glanc-
ing Complacently in a glass, “condition —good !”

Editor Bulletin, —It has arrived. Yes.
An official from Sydney Labour Bureau, in-
vestigating the case of a workless man with a wife
and young baby, said the other day to the wife :
“ If your husband cannot get work, perhaps you
can, and he can stay at home and mind the
baby.”—R.

Editor Bulletin, —Chas. Lever, in “The
Irish Dragoon,” objects to coram publico endear-
ments ; many a man agrees with him. Here, for in-
stance, am I, a poor devil of a tutor in a family with
a couple of grown-up, pretty-there’s the rub-
daughters. Perchance —pour passer le temps —I
join the home circle. Lo! when the time comes
to say “ good-night,” I have to stand by and look,
with at least an appearance of calmness, on a
tantalising tableaux —a beautiful girl’s arm thrown
round the neck of a fat, ornary, bullet-headed
squatter. I protest against the fashion —especi-
ally before the eyes of men like me, to whom,
unless a revolution takes place, matrimonial joys
and domestic felicities are closed chapters,—
Yours, &c., Tim Bobbin.

An Adelaide city alderman, speaking re
the proposed cabling of condolence to Mine. Car-
not, drew the attention of National Ass Mayor
Willcox to the fact that now all the potentates of
the earth are in peril at the hands of murderous
anarchists, and warned his Worship that even his
life might be aimed at. Willcox looked very
serious, and in future will, it is expected, walk
abroad encased in boiler-plate.

There is one consolation in re the appar-
ently causeless suicide last week of a bright and
seemingly happy N.S.W. girl. By being success-
ful, she escaped the indignity of being haled
before a magistrate, and probably of being sent to
gaol for a mouth for failing to complete the act.
Which prc >ves, as have millions of events, that
there is no bad situation that might not be worse.
There is a difficulty about hanging Lee and
Cummins together, the gallows at Tamworth
(N.S. W.) gaol being only constructed to accommo-
date one patient at a time. So the authorities
propose to strangulate Lee at 8 a. m. and Cummins
an hour later. In this emergency, the writer
offers a patent suggestion to the public execu-
tioner : “Put them back to back; strap them closely
together ; then a swingle-tree arrangement over-
head with two nooses, and—thawp ! ” “ Horribly
brutal,” say you ? Well, not a whit more brutal
than the ceremonious ordinary ..angiug. When

The true story of the hanging of Mrs.
Knorr, and of the period between the passing of
sentence and the execution thereof, has yet to be
told. It will be an eloquent commentary on the
capital punishment system.

Now that the Apostles of Pruriency have
scented out the classics ic will be interesting to
see where they will stop. There is hardly an
ancient author-and the adjective may embrace
all writers prior to the year 1800 —in whose works
some cobalt passages do not appear. When the
second-hand book-shops are weeded and generally
“ prurified,” the Melb. Public Library —it has
already withdrawn from circulation Hawthorne's
“ Scarlet Letter ” ! ! !—will probably receive att'en-
tion. Indeed, it is hard to see how a Government
which forbids Ovid’s “ Art of Love ” to be
privately sold, can continue to permit the work
—and others which, viewed from the same stand-
point, are even worse—to be handled by the public.
There are several editions of Dryden’s famous
translation in the Lending Branch of the Vic.
Public Library, and, since attention has been
directed to it, all of them have been asked for
and issued, so that for the first time on record
there has been a run on the book, and many
applicants have been disappointed. One of the
editions, by the way, bears on its calf cover the
inscription : “ Presented to the Public Library
by His Excellency Sir H. Barkly, K.C.B., 3rd
August, 1863.” What a wicked Governor ! Mean-
time, there is a classic boom in Smellbourne, and
Longus, Theocritus, Lucretius, Ovid, and other
gay old dogs, have for the time, being eclipsed
Sarah Grand, Mrs. Mannington Caffyn. and the
rest of the modern straight out school. It is
another singular inconsistency that while Zola has
been ostracised by the Vic. Customs Department,
“Rabelais,” “ Boccacio,’'etc., are openly offered
for sale, while indisputably obscene prints are
circulated unchecked,
• * #
Editor Bulletin, — You are wrong about
four charitable institutions in Sydney carrying on
operations without reference to each other. Bene-
volent Asylum and Labour Bureau work into
each other’s hand. Former only gives relief in
cases latter will not entertain. When a husband
is able to work he is sent to the Labour Bureau.
If he is sent into the country fossicking, the wife
has to go to the Benevolent Asylum. In no case is
the relief given sufficient to keep the family with-
out work or further help. It only consists of food
and a few shillings towards the rent—no fuel,
candle, or soap—and the rent-receipt must be
given up.—R.

A very popular young lady, niece of Aus-
tralia’s most eminent surgeon, who for some
seasons past lias figured prominently in Sydney
society, has opened a tea-room on the Block, in

assemble and drink their souchong. She has
pluckily determined to do the waiting herself.

The shabby-genteel petition to Chief Jus-
tice Way (S.A.) praying that publication of
the “immoral details” of a fashionable divorce-
case might be prohibited, again illustrates
the “ society ” creed that the penalties and odium
of the law were not intended for the person in
broadcloth and a silk hat, but only for the man in
dirty moles and greasy fustian. Though every
divorce-suit inherently depends upon “immoral
details,” the unco’-sensitive never make a single
move towards the suppression of the rorty tit-bits
of evidence, so long as it is an affair of housemaid*
and knockaboutsi

An Australian police-officer lately had a
little domestic difficulty vvitli his better-half. She
was under the imprecision that hubby was too
friendly with another woman, and conceived the
brilliant notion of reporting him to head-
quarters. To her consternation the officer lost a
stripe and with it a certain amount of pay.
Jealousy sometimes crushes a poor dividend.

terviewed the Vic. Minister of

handed in a list of the bad

enough indecent books to make
any sort of list worth speaking
about. It is only the W.C.T.U.
and Y.M.C.A. and Y.W.O.A.
saints who know all the inde-
cent books, and know exactly
how indecent they are. And,
considering that one person of
average memory can only keep
a mental grip of a certain num-
ber of books, the saint who
knows so many indecent books
can hardly have time to know
any decent ones.

That beneficent institu-
tion, the Melb. Charity Organ-
isation Society, . was heard
from, the other day, in the
local police-court. Some little
time ago the society took com-
passion on an unemployed
carter, and bought him a horse,
so that he might earn a living
for self and family. The cash
value of this shattered equine
was about 55., and it was so old
and feeble and forlorn that
when the recipient was found
driving it the police ran him in
and had him fined £2 for
cruelty. The wretched animal
was killed and given to the
lions at the Zoo. As for the
carter, he is 40s. worse off than
he was before the society came
to his assistance, and his posi-
tion is another awful commen-
tary on the ingratitude of the
lower orders.
Thus a correspondent:—
UuijjKTiK,— Apropos the divided skirt: The liark
view of a woman in baggy pants is as handsome as the
baok view of an elephant.
If the Lord designed woman for trousers, how is it she
looks so huge a joke in them ?
Providence gave woman a head to hide her body, but
the woman who wears trousers shows no head—only body.
Woman in dress often looks a triumph of Art over
Nature: the woman in trousers looks a triumph of Nature
over Art. “Now, most women,’’ remarks the anatomist
Velpeau, “look much better when dressed than when
undressed.” Trousers are a step in the direction of being
undressed.
There are three things which pass our understanding—
The love of a woman, her cruelty: and why she should
ever want to wear trousers.
For trousers on a woman are as out of place as the blue-
ribbon on a cabman.
And a woman in trousers loses even the virtue of a bad
egg, for that has a good exterior.
Man can admire the nobleness of woman’s soul and the
lace on her petticoat; What man could admire the noble-
ness of woman’s soul and the bagginess of her trousers?
Now, abide these four tilings which a good woman may
not give: Her Religion, her Honour, her Lover, her
Petticoat. And the greatest of these is the Petticoat.
* * *
The alleged humour of the police-court
bench is a coarse and ghoul-like affair. A fine
specimen of it turned up last week at South Mel-
bourne. A sad-looking, thin, shabby-genteel man,
with an infant in his arms and a child toddling by
his side, appeared to ask for advice. His wife, he
said, had left him. He had five children, the two
infants there in evidence and three other small
youngsters ; and having no female relatives to
look after them, he was compelled to stay at home
and mind the family himself, and was thus pre-
cluded from earning a livelihood as a wharf-
labourer. He had no means of paying for legal
advice, so he asked the bench to advise him as to
the steps necessary for compelling his wife to
return. Then the bench opened its mouth till the
stopping in its back tooth was plainly visible, and
guffawed at the poor, broken-down applicant, and
advised him to “get a good-looking young woman
in to mind the children,’’ and when she heard of
it the lost wife would probably return. Upon
which truly Solomonic deliverance the audience
all yelled with laughter, and the seedy husband,
seeing that something of the kind was evidently
expected of him, smiled feebly and went away.

DOMESTIC ECONOMY!
WHA.T IS XT ?
DOMESTIC ECONOMY is primarily the art of feeding and clothing the family at as low a rate as possible,
without impairing the family digestion, or taking away the character of its members for being well and com-
fortably dressed. Domestic economy is not comprised in buying a bad article for sixpence when better can be
got for sevenpence ; but if the ruling price for the best is eightpence, and somebody comes along and offers it for
sevenpence, then it’s domestic economy to close with the merchant so offering. By all means study this great
and glorious science of social life, because its due and proper observance cannot fail to make poor people rich, and
rich ones richer. What say you? Will you do your business at
?

Dear Bulletin, —Enough of the mean
man; let me start a “ tight place ” series. Has
any reader been in a worse hole than this of mine ?
Seven years ago I called on the manager of
to inspect a mob of rams. The glass was well past
the 100 in the shade, and my host suggested a
bath before dinner, “ I always go in for a plunge
at this time,” said he, “as I find it gives one a
first-rate appetite.” The water was cool and re-
freshing, and I lay long enjoying it. Suddenly
the dinner-bell sounded and I jumped out, applied
a rough towel, and started to dress. My shirt
caught round my head, and before I could disen-
tangle myself—Presto! the door burst open, and a
lady rushed in and folded me in her arms, shout-
ing, “Dick, you stupid, when will you be ready
for dinner ?” She did not appear that evening.—
Tim Bobbin.

The tightest place The Bulletin ever was
in was a prohibitionist settlement- Mildura.

Some dull people persist in regarding Max
Adeler’s “Out of the Hurly-Burly ” as a work of
the imagination. Here is a solemn extract from
the correspondence column of Sydney Mail
(weekly edition of the right-thinking Sydney
Morning Herald), sandwiched in between an
item about bushrangers and an answer to a person
in search of a soap-recipe:—
E.G. (Camden): 1. Yes, it is believed by those who
study the Scriptures that the Millennium (which it is sup-
posed you mean when you refer to the “ end of the
world ’’) is not very far off. We are living in the last
days, but how far off the second coming of our Saviour is,
cannot be stated. The signs of the times will be the best
guide, and when you find that the Gospel has been
preached to all nations (not that the world has been con-
verted), and when the Jews are restored to Palestine,
then the second advent of our Saviour will take place,
and those who have died in Him will rise from their graves
to meet Him in the air, and then they will reign over the
earth for 1000 years. The rest of the dead will remain in
their graves until the second resurrection. Many of the
prophesies in the Bible have been fulfilled, but some of
them have still to be accomplished. 2. You want to
know, how an intelligent person may know whether he
has “ heart disease ?” The best way to find out is to get
sounded by an intelligent physician, who will perhaps
point out to you that people sometimes mistake indiges-
tion for heart disease, and that people sometimes have
heart disease without being aware of it, and consequently
are likely to drop dead at any moment. The remainder
of your question will be answered elsewhere. See the
“ Agricultural Column.”
Which—and especially the fact that the rest of
the answers will be found in the “ Agricultural
Column ” —is about the strangest mixture of the
practical and fanciful that can well be imagined.
Why the Millennium wasn’t also put in the “ Agri-
cultural Column ” is a mystery.
# # •
Dr. Chas. M‘oarbhy, of Sydney, testifies,
in a Melb. paper, to the wonderful powers of a
certain lady-medium. She can materialise little
Josephines and Cissies and other fashionable
spirits without going into a cabinet to do it. “ I
have frequently,” says the confiding medical man,
“seen with my ordinary sight this medium and a
materialised form out (of the cabinet) together,
have spoken to both of them, and have found the
form to be as real, warm, animated flesh and blood
as the medium herself.” The unconscious humour
of a sperrit-worshipper is very touching. Dr. M.
believes in the mystic genuineness of a plump form
that he can pinch, and he believes also that he has
photographed a half-formed entity in process of
materialisation—“ which appears as a vaporous,
though shapely, mass.” The vulgar question as
to why he didn’t keep his camera fixed upon
the process, and obtain photos, of the entity
in every stage of development seemingly doesn’t
trouble Dr. M’Oarthy. He has “ taken ” a
mass of luminous vapour, and pinched a warm
flesh and blood form, and the intervening evolu-
tion he takes for granted. There is a showman in
Melbourne who can produce more spirits and
accomplish more eerie things in a strange room
than Dr. M’Carthy’s medium would dare attempt.
Consequently the whole gang of local spiritualists
have tried, at one time or other, to convince the
aforesaid trickster (who is, or was, a compositor in
private life) that he possessed supernatural powers
without being aware of the fact. He lacks,
however, the peculiar power to humbug himself,
and so the soulless creature continues to call him-
self an entertainer. For a respectable fee he
would, no doubt, be happy to give Dr. M’Carthy
plenty of luminous entity and a fine materialised
barmaid form, and even teach the scientist how to
work the process himself.

* * *
At a recent meeting of a Yic. Australian
Natives’ Society, the exercise of the evening was
impromptu speaking, and the theme allotted to
the unfortunate president was “ Chinese Meta-
physics.” But he grasped it manfully. Prefacing
his remarks by regretting that ho knew little of
the subject—he relieved himself of the following :
“I have no faith in them at all, gentlemen. I
believe there are a few in Melbourne, but I have
never heard of them being successful. I know
this, that if I was sick, I would die before I would
submit myself to a Chinese doctor .” Australia for
the Australians ! Whoop !

The other Sunday a suburban (Sydney)
omnibus was coming into town at a good pace.
Presently it clattered past a church, and a man on
the box-seat remonstrated with the driver.
“Isn’t there a regulation about going slow when
you pass a church while service is on so as not to
disturb the congregation ? ” “I b’lieve there is,”
said the driver meditatively, “ but that place don’t
matter. That's only a Wesleyan Church.” And
he addressed some strong language to the front
horse and galloped on,

A Maorilander has invented a new way of
getting round the miserable publican. This person
stayed a good many weeks at a hotel in a Southern
town and paid nothing; even his drinks were put
in the bill. When the score got up to .£l2 or so
he attempted to get away with his luggage, but
the landlord seized the “ props.” as security, and
retained them, despite sundry desperate efforts on
the part of the ejected lodger to recover posses-
sion. Then the lodger had an inspiration. He
got a friend to take out a summons against the
publican for damage to an alleged fence, and
subpoenaed all the family as witnesses. When
the case came on they all went to court in pro-
cession, leaving the bar in charge of a stranger.
and when they returned an hour later —there
having been no appearance of the prosecutor—the
luggage was gone. A man had called round,
stating that the landlord sent him for the boxes,
and the amateur barman gave them up without
the slightest suspicion. The scene closes with an
infuriated publican prancing in the bar, and using
language of the kind that is generally written out
and handed up to the magistrate.
# * *
The most thriving trade, just at present,
is that of the signwriter. As with the pawn-
broker and the bailiff's, the measure of the sign-
writer’s briskness is that of the community’s
depression. Everyone who has, or can borrow, a
few pounds opens some kind of shop these hard
times, in the desperate struggle to stave off star-
vation. A sign-writer is engaged to paint the
name over the door, and, with all its stock-
in-trade in the window, the little shop makes a
good show—for a month or so. Then it closes
up in a hurry. These is never anything to
prepare one for this contingency. _ No notice of
sale “owing to the proprietor retiring from busi-
ness,” or that he is “moving to larger and more
commodious premises.” The shop is simply
opened to-day and to-morrow it isn’t. That is
all. Anon thereduced rent ” tempts some other
unfortunate ; the same old sign-writer comes along
with his ladders and demolishes his late work,
and in its place paints the name of the new
tenant. Again the shop is opened, and, in a little
while, closed. No one ever buys anything in
these little shops ; yet, if the stock is edible, it
grows beautifully less as time goes by. The pro-
prietor, poor devil, must live on it; and when
at length he has eaten himself out and been
moved on, someone is always ready to take bis
place and “keep shop.” And the only one to
reap any benefit out of all this opening and closing
is the sign-writer—if he gets paid.

by the spiritists.

A donkey, with its legs encased in frilled
trousers, is being paraded about Newtown
(Sydney), for advertising purposes. Local she
suffragists regard this as an onward step in the
cause.” If a donkey can wear ’em, why not
women?” they ask. To which the other crowd
answer : “ But the donkey’s pants are not made
of tweed.”

The worldliness of professional church
singing receives bold advt. in Melbourne of late
from the performances of a certain “St. Paul’s
Cathedral quartette party.” These salaried mem-
bers of the holy choir accept society engagements
to go around o’ nights and sing glees, which, by
the way, they do uncommonly well. There is just
a faint odour of sanctity about warblers who have
been joining in the anthem swell only a short
time previous, so the more serious-minded portion
of the community derives comfort as well as joy
from their secular music. Even the host, who
cashes up the fees, feels like cue who is lending to
; the Lord, and coughs dubiously before suggesting
drinks to the quartette.
A similar spiritual something is credited to the
personality of Ernest Wood, the gifted young
organist at St. Paul’s, A while ago, when
Sterling wanted the best available talent to mani-
pulate the Town Hall organ for her “Lost
Chord,” the old lady’s manager, waiting upon the
Cathedral artist, was staggered to find him a
highly-groomed and tailored genius, vvitli a waxy
moustache, and proud, commercial spirit. The
showman had expected to ‘strike a mild, long-
haired person of the Tom Pinch type, who would
come and play for nothing to oblige a hoarse
female in distress. His account of the disillusion-
ment was quite pathetic.

Editor Bulletin, —Where is the use of
whining about the political prominence of new-
chums in N.S. Wales ? Don’t you know that, in
Sydney, new-chums are regarded as the salt of the
earth, and they can get their pick of the best
billets going, at the highest rate of pay, and
almost without credentials, while men of proved
ability and established character are walking about
unable to get employment ? The man fresh from
Hingland is preferred even for positions that
demand special local experience, and he is indul-
gently allowed to go on for months bungling and
muddling in a way that would qualify an old-chum
for the noble order of the sack in a week.— Old-
Chum.

Fitz Gibbon, the one and only, suggests a
delicate improvement in the arrangements at Port
Phillip quarantine station. When women are
detained, there should, says gallant Fitz., be a
female attendant to disinfect their garments,
instead of a male creature who gathers up the
mysteries of beauty’s toilet, and probably criti-
cises the lace trimmings and things in secret.
Fitz Gibbon is right. Women there are who would
die rather than “hand over their garments, to a
male attendant.” The harp, if you please :

THE SERVANT DIFFICULTY.
“ Why don’t girls go to domestic service?” — Premier Dibbs.
I would be like a servant
And with the servants stand 1
Black soot upon my forehead
And coat-dust on my hand ;
Or polish knives and carvers
So glorious and so bright!
And wake the noise of saucepans
From morning until night.
And when hot winds are blowing
Across the sandy street,
How sweet to take my dinner
In burning kitchen heat!
How sweet to get up early
And grind away all day,
To wait upon the children
And wait nix months tor pay 1
Oh, yes, I’ll be a servant
And with the servants stand }
No curls to grace my forehead.
Fierce redness on my hand ;
One evening out in seven,
And Sundays, one in four,
No followers or sweethearts,
If otherwise—the door 1
Hobart.
