LOVE AND DUTY.
Peter, dreaming dreams of wealth,
Nods beside his Beauty ;
Charley takes her hand by stealth—
Love may vanquish Duty.
Peter pays a handsome price:
Peter trusts her blindly—
Charley makes himself so nice,
One mutt treat him kindly.
“ What’s the betting on the fight?’*
Cries the cynic snarly.
Now her eyes with passion light—
Six to four on Charley !
Love is sweet, but Duty old
Hath a charm yet sweeter,
Duty’s shrine is solid gold—
Any odds on Peter!
Melb.
EDMUND FISHER.
Araminta Mary Mould
Was tall, and lean, and healthy.
And, if the gossips truly told,
She, furthermore, was wealthy.
A modest lady, A.M.M.,
Who, in a painter, would condemn
AH studies from the nudish.
No male eye should ever note
The edging to her petticoat,
Declared this person prudish.
Araminta voyaged “ home ”
To see her people well-born:
Anon she spent a week in Rome,
Then back again to Melbourne.
Alack 1 for this unhappy trip I
A case of fever on the ship
By doctors was suspected.
Miss Araminta, long and lean,
Was landed into quarantine,
Where clothes are disinfected.
Picture now her maiden woe—
Her state of agitation 1
Her what’s-its-name and so-and-so
Were wanted at the “station.”
When told to “ change ” in hottest haste
Her gown and vestments, white and chaste.
This spinster independent
Did never dream her sex must bear
The shame of handing underwear
To, ugh! a male attendant.
“ Monster! What dost thou request 1”
(The monster said “ Your linnin !”)
Smiting hard her bony chest
She drove the bonnet-pin in,
The weapon struck a vital place,
The spinster fell upon her face,
And rent the.air with screeches.
Ah ! death was better than the shame
Of handing up her what’s-its-name
To anyone in breeches.


Parliamentary Elections.
MAIXTIFUBTO
—OF—
The Central Election Committee of
the Protectionist Party.
To THE
Electors of New South Wales.
We earnestly ask you to consider the follow-
ing facts in connection with this Election
Under the influence and rule of the Foreign
Trade Party we have piled up a debt to
foreign countries of £55,000,000, for which
we have to pay two and a-quarter millions
interest out of our produce.
The expenditure of these loan funds created
a boom, and directed labour into the con-
struction of public works, to the neglect and
destruction of reproductive industries.
The collapse of the borrowing boom,
coupled with our fiscal policy, has left us
without the necessary industries to yield
employment to our present army of workers,
while we have 25,000 young Australians
from our schools seeking admittance annually
into the industrial ranks.
To meet this position foreign trade ad-
vocates and journalists are advising us to put
all our energies into the production of law
material for export to Europe. In fact, we
are advised not to encourage the home pro-
duction of ordinary necessaries, so that their
import may provide EMPLOYMENT FOR
FOREIGN VESSELS to carry our raw pro-
duce to European markets at low freights.
Has such a policy ever established a civi-
lised and prosperous community in any of
the new lands into which the surplus popula-
tion of Europe is flowing ? Has it done so
in any of the old settled countries of the
world ? On the contrary, its failure stands
out as a warning to avoid such a policy.
Even last year we imported, over and
above what we exported, produce SUIT-
ABLE TO OUR SOIL and Climate exceed-
ing the value of £3,000,000, not counting
iron nor manufactured articles. To supply
this home demand is our best market, and
will assist in the general development, of
other and varied industries, without which
we cannot find occupations and employment
for the rising generation.
The Single-taxers have deluded the
LABOUR PARTY into the belief that a tax
on land would benefit them. They have
likewise befooled the Freetrade party that a
land tax would be a taking cry at the very
time we are making FREE GRANTS of
land and money to induce Village Settle-
ment.
All obstacles to Agricultural Settlement
must be removed, and this can best be
effected by a law empowering cultivators to
take up, on perpetual lease, any uncultivated
land, at a rental equal to 5 per cent, on the
capital value, subject to a maximum value
of two pounds per acre.
A Land Tax for Local Government will be
as much as selectors can bear at present,
while the duties arising from a Protection
tariff will provide the necessary revenue
When increased Colonial production de-
creases the revenue from imported goods,
Lands, Incomes, Absentees and Legacies will
form fit sources of taxation.
The injustice of singling out land from
other forms of property for taxation, more
especially as a TAX ON AGRICULTURAL
LAND IS EQUIVALENT TO AN EX-
CISE DUTY ON ITS PRODUCE, must be
evident to every intelligent person not
blinded by party bias.
We have all the elements required for the
production of iron, and ABUNDANT
LABOUR for its manufacture, while we are
IMPORTING iron to the value of HALF A
MILLION ANNUALLY. Our neglect of
this matter is a disgrace to New South
Wales, and shows to what an extent we have
allowed the foreign trade element to domi-
ate our councils in the face of the fact that
NO COUNTRY in the world has been able
to establish ironworks without the aid of
PROTECTIVE DUTIES.
All the Colonies are on the same plane of
civilisation, with equal standards of living,
salaries, and wages, and therefore we have
nothing to protect against each other. It is
therefore our duty to make a great effort to
have the Colonies FEDERATED into a
GREAT AUSTRALIAN NATION, having
Freetrade throughout Australia and Protec-
tion against the world.
The time has now arrived when we must
pass a LOCAL GOVERNMENT BILL,
giving greater power to the local bodies than
was given in any of the proposed Bills made
public during the last twenty years, and
such Bill should provide that certain of these
extended powers should be submitted to a
REFERENDUM of the Municipal Elec-
tions.
The question of LOCAL OPTION lias
been a disturbing element in all elections
for the past fifteen years. Its importance
demands settlement on just and well-con-
sidered lines in the interest of the public
and publicans. This can best be accom-
plished by handing over the entire control
of the Liquor Traffic to the Municipal Cor-
porations constituted by the new Local
Government Act.
We earnestly ask all, and particularly
young, Australians to throw educational
opinions and PREJUDICES aside in the
consideration of our position, and put an
end to this INSIDIOUS INFLUENCE,
which is being exerted by foreign IMPOR-
TERS calling themselves AUSTRALIANS.
ARCHD. FORSYTH, Chairman.
Offices : 15 Oastlereagh Street.

Which can hold love enough for twenty girls

** * *
Melbourne Alexandra is still the scene of
wild domestic dissension arising from “ Ally
Sloper’s Night Out,” and a fair number of weak
mortals seem to revel in the contemplation of
Ally’s bulging umbrella. Dalziel, as the limp,
gentlemanly husband of a certain lively Tottie,
would deserve considerable praise if he made his
languid accents more audible; but the same re-
mark applies to Dan Barry and many other people
who struggle with the acoustic properties of this
unhappy house. The Bijou and Alhambra are
held by their respective variety companies, a
shilling’s worth of variety at either show being un-
commonly like twelve pen’orth of mixed items at
the other.
** * *
On Wednesday next the Costume Football
Match and Fete, in aid of the Homes for aged
pros., will be held at East Melb. cricket-ground—
tickets, one shilling each, or two shillings for
proud aristocracy who want to be specially re-
served. Forty enthusiastic performers from the
theatres and ’alls have undertaken to kick for a prize
cake, whilst a brigade of dear creatures in tights
are entered for a Ladies’ Bicycle Race.
Acrobatics, saok-raees, pony-races, and side-
shows in abundance, are in course of pre-
paration. Above all, “ the principal ladies
from the various theatres will have refreshment
tents, flower tents, cigar and cigarette tents, etc.
Here there is plenty of scope for reckless liberality.
The value of a cup of coffee from Nellie Stewart
has no well-defined limit, nor can rosebuds and
cigars from a Varley, a Florence Young, or a Lucy
Cobb be judged by ordinary rules of commerce.
A male customer may tender any coin of the realm
up to a pound sterling, after which the goddess
will smile upon him until his brain reels and he
forgets to ask for the change.
** * *
Dear Bulletin,— l attended a recent Melb.
Sunday night “ concert,” in the interests of the
Y.M.C.A., and found the Saturday night’s pro-
gramme in full blast, barring the burnt cork and
the dancing. Most of the performers made
one concession to the conventionalities—they sang
their comic songs with a profound solemnity that
was wildly comical. One song-and-dance damsel,
however, after an earnest effort, found that she
positively could not go through her little ditty
without the usual gyrations. She was as uncom-
fortable as a cat in skirts, and at last broke into
the familiar prance, and finished up with a running
high kick that evoked wild applause. But the
people on the stage looked as if their religious
convictions had been severely tried.—Yours,
S. Snell.
At Poverty Point.
Miss Myra Kemble writes from London to a Sydney
friend:—“The only thing worth looking at now here that
Lve yet seen is ‘The Living Pictures,’ at the Palace
Theatre, late the new English Opera House. They are
mostly studies from the nude, and exact copies of Royal
Academy pictures by different artists. The girls who re-
present the figures are all artists’ models, and the result
is everything that is artistic and charming.’’
The wail of an “advance” in Victoria:—Times are
awful: we are always retreating from Moscow. People
have no money for vanities, and what little they have is
generally somebody eise’s. With a company of 14 we
have opened to 125., and this in a mining town. Every
spare 6d. was going for “ calls.” If the co. don’t happen
to strike a pay day they can confidently expect to come %
cropper, and consequently anticipation softens the knock.
Victoria needs more protection—from rogues in high
places.—H.F.C.

Adelina Patti sang a Wagner air, for the first time in
her public life, at a recent Albert Hall concert. It was
the simplest of songs, with harp accompaniment, but a
critic says of Adelina’s rendering that it was a “ revela-
tion to those accustomed to the harsh German method of
singing.” Our Amy Sherwin was the second lady vocalist
at this particular concert, so she ought to be gleaning
some good artistic wrinkles in her middle age.

“ A Society Butterfly ” has brought Robert Buchanan
and Clement Scott once more to loggerheads. In my
opinion (says Thk Bulletin’s London correspondent)
Scott’s furious onslaught upon the play and the acting of
Mrs. Langtry was unjustifiable. We all know that the
Lily is not a Bernhardt or a Duse, but really the did not
act so badly as one might think from reading the Daily
Telegraph. It she failed at all it was in her gowns, which,
as a man remarked, failed for their absence of indecency.
All her frocks were very simply made, and in one of the
big scenes she wore a high-necked dress, an unpardonable
sin in the eyes of those who “ went out for to see.” The
newspapers are not by any means to be taken as safe
guides to the merits of a play. Who ever reads a harsh
word in the Referee about any of Q. R. Sims’ work? And
to read “ Carados,” one would think that “ Jaunty Jane
Shore,” the new burlesque with that title, was one of the
most brilliant pieces of the day, whereas it is the most
stupid, dreary farrago of stuff that ever was put upon a
stage. And why the good notices? Bless you, are not
Riehard-Henry the greater part of Carados ? The same
thing obtains all through the chapter.
Ellen Terry will be retiring from the boards in anothe r
two or three years, for she has no desire to play middle*
aged women, and she can’t be an artless girl for ever*
Irving’s case is quite different. The great Henry is stil*
on the right side, so to speak, of his strongest impersona-
tions—Burgomaster Matthias, Louis XL, Vicar of Wake-
field, &c. The older he gets the more naturally he takes
to them.

The clever little daughter of Dr. M‘Carthy, of Sydney,
is fiddling herself into people’s hearts, writes The Bulle-
tin’s London correspondent. If not spoiled by injudicious
adulation, and not allowed to read fulsome paragraphs,
she may one day be a fine performer. But that will be in
the distant future. One is always doubtful of the lasting
of the infant phenomenon. Here is a par. which has been
going the rounds. The child, it is said, was one day
romping with some young companions in Hyde Park,
when she heard a poor Italian boy rasping a violin in a
most excruciating way, after the manner of his country-
men. She at once rushed at the lad, seized the fiddle and
commenced to show him how it should be played, and so
charmed a crowd which had gathered to hear her, that
when she had done they gave a round of cheers, where-
upon the young lady took off the boy’s hat and, going
round the impromptu audience, gathered a tile-full of
coppers, which she handed him. It is not by such yarns
as this that the child's reputation will be made.
Competent judges prophesy that little Cissie Lottus,
the young damsel who gives imitations of popular
favourites at the ’alls, will grow into a great original
aolress. Cissie’s mother, Marie Loftus, is a clever serio-
comic singer with a reputation for extreme spiciness. A
few years back Marie used to give them some rather
naughty songs in a very naughty style.
Leumane, a man of brains, positively had strips of
Westralian press “opinions” on Leumane printed for
transmission to journalists in Sydney, Melbourne and
elsewhere. What is there in stage life to create this
fatuous regard for “ notices’’ among people who are sane
enough on all other points ? Actors and actresses fritter
away a considerable per-centage of their lives in collecting
stupid paragraphs from stupid papers and sticking them
into a book. Though they have reason to feel certain, in
many cases, that the manager wrote the notice himself
and took it round to the office, still they are prepared to
paste the gammon in a book. They are, most of them,
even capable of writing their own puffs in order to gloat
over them in secret. The appetite increases, instead of
weakening, with age, and it is universal in the profession.
Sarah Bernhardt is said to have a gigantic collection of
printed comment upon herself gathered from many lands,
and she reads the volume sometimes—reads it, mong Doo I
Few actresses, remarks The Bulletin’s London corres-
pondent, have made such big strides in her profession
as Mrs. Patrick Campbell, whose performance in “The
Masqueraders” is so disappointing-, after her success in
“The Second Mrs. Tanqueray.” The critics to a man have
been “down ” on her, and she appears to act more in-
differently each evening. As there is no effect without a
cause, a gentleman, who sat next her at supper a few
nights since, ventured to broach the subject, and soon
discovered that very strained relations exist between her
and Alexander. This is her story. She entered into an
engagement with Alexander at a small salary for three
years, which period is nearly at an end. When she was
allotted an important and difficult part in H. A. Jones’
play she spoke to Alexander and asked him to increase
her salary so as to make it somewhat commensurate with
the importance of the part. He curtly refused, and hard
words followed. "The fact is,” she said, “I am an
underpaid and badly treated woman. I loathe Mr. Alex-
ander, and he hates me just as cordially. He knows that
when my agreement with him is completed I will at once
get a salary four or five times as great as he pays me, and
that several managers are desirous of securing me. This
is gall to him, as I will not renew my engagement. I am,
of course, compelled to go upon the stage and repeat
certain lines, but no one can compel me to act, and I will
not wear myself out with playing my part in a passionate
way for such a man. Had Mr. Alexander behaved as a
gentleman tome 1 should have done my best, as it is I
decline to do so.” This is the true story of the Mrs.
Patrick Campbell failure. It is her own fault and is of
malice prepense, and, under the circumstances, one can
hardly extend much sympathy to her, for no matter how
badly Alexander may have behaved, and there is only her
word for it, she is not justified in refusing to do her best
simply because her manager, for no matter what reasen,
declines to go beyond his agreement. . . . But Mrs.
Campbell very soon realised the mistake she was
making in playing indifferently, and she now shows
something like her true form in “ The Masquer-
aders,” which is going stronger than ever. The ridiculous
and impossible “card-scene” is being most amusingly
burlesqued in “Go Bang,” and surely never did author
give so good an opportunity. H. A. Jones has, it is said,
received from Alexander the very largest weekly cheque
for “ rights” overpaid loan author. Alexander, how-

would draw a waggon-load of wool.
Only three of the Queensland Parlia-
mentary Labour-party were in favour of the
shearers striking forthwith. The A.L.F. influ-
ence in the party is seemingly on the wane.
Adrian Knox, who opposes Jawbone
Neild for Woollahra, pronounces against Payment
of Members, and he has also had conferred upon
him the support of the National Ass, as per
secret circular.
A curious fact about the Bombala-Eden
electorate is that the candidates go in alphabetical
pairs, thus: —Cohen, Goman—two C.’s; Wood,
Wood—two W.’s; Murphy, Myers—two M.’s ;
Harrison, Hyde—two H.’s; and Roche and
Stiles, in which case the letters come as near to
pairing as possible.
“ The meaning of the word Labour,” says
James Watson, who splits the Democratic vote
with Traill for Cook Division of Sydney, “is
wage-earner, and the Labour-platform embraces
all wage-earners.” Ergo, according to Mr. Wat-
son, the Labour-platform oannot possibly include
all workers. Traill has been an employer, there-
fore he oannot be a good Democrat. However,
Mr. Watson, who represents only one section of
workers, will not be returned for Cook, and neither
will Traill, who claims to represent all; but Mr. S.
T. Whiddon, boot-importer, who is nominated on
the National Ass secret list, and is, therefore, per-
force, opposed to payment of members and land-
taxation, will be returned.

In trying to please everybody he pleased
nobody and lost his National Ass into the bargain.
His name was Parkes.
A tremendous sensation is brewing in
Sydney Masonic circles —with a pretty woman at
the bottom of it, of course.
N.S.W. Education Minister Suttor in his
heart loves not Sydney University, which long
ago rejected his father when he sought election to
its Senate.
G. H. Reid is on the National Ass’s secret
list of favoured candidates —which fact goes to
show how much the Ass believes in his land-tax
professions.
It is reckoned by somebody that the
“ good men,” in meeting assembled at Sydney
Centenary Hall the other day, were worth about
nine millions among ’em.
Mr. B. R. Wise’s political programme,
which deals almost exclusively with land-tax
reform, appears on page 17 of this issue. Wise,
by the way, is not supported by the National Ass,
which (as per secret circular) is running Olliffe
against him.
Hawthorne is the National Ass nominee
for Leichhardt, N.S.W., where Darnley, Clarke
and Holman are cutting each other’s throats in
the name of democracy. Hawthorne must win,
as things stand now. Will the three so-called
democrats at the last moment spin a coin for the
seat?
Arrangements are on the tapis for
bringing up to Sydney, from a Southern coal dis-
trict, a large number of men for the purpose of
personating sailors who will be at sea on the day
of the N.S.W. election, and who leave their elec-
toral rights behind them for the purpose. The
Bulletin hopes to see all concerned in the scheme
get three years’ hard.
It is denied that M‘llwraith is afflicted
with dropsy ; it is also denied that he is mentally
queer. The actual trouble is phlebitis with
which, by the way, Agent-General Garrick was
laid up for seven months when MTlwraith was in
London. The ex-Premier’s doctors have forbidden
him to discuss politics or public matters. Premier
Nelson lias seen him once, and his private secre-
tary, clever young Dutton, twice only since his
return to Brisbane. The news of the Ayrshire
Downs woolshed fire has, of course, been withheld
from him.
It is now clear that the Queensland
Government will need additional strength. MTl-
wraith cannot hope to be of any assistance during
the first three months of the ensuing session, and
the not very brilliant Walter Horatio Wilson
may soon have to resign one or both of his port-
folios owing to the serious illness of his law part-
ner. Though the dailies carefully suppress the
fact, Mr. Wilson has decided to look after his big
business and drop politics awhile. Should he do
so, Philp will probably succeed him at the Educa-
tion Office. The loquacious Dairymple may be
the new man in the Ministry.
Sydney Telegraph remarks :
It is stated that fully half the adult male population
have been disfranchised by the operation of the new
Electoral Act.
This is such an obvious lie that the Telegraph
daren’t assert it on its own account, so it vaguely
says, “It is stated.” And it hasn’t even the
courage to say who stated it, perhaps for the suffi-
cient reason that it wasn’t stated at all, or perhaps
because it was only stated by the National Ass.
The Electoral Act, even if it and the people
who run it did their very worst, couldn’t
disfranchise “ fully half ” —it is questionable
if it could disfranchise even a tenth—of
the male population. But the Telegraph
loathes manhood suffrage in its inmost soul,
and is already aiming at its repeal by trying to
prove that “it is stated ” to be less liberal than
the old property vote. Before the country accepts
this glaring Ananias it wants to know who
“ stated ” it. The thing that is only reported to
.'■have been stated by some party unknown doesn’t
carry much weight.

Parliamentary Elections.
Preetrade and Land Reform
Electoral Committee.
Extracts from the Manifesto of the
President, Mr, B. JB. Wise, to his
constituents, the Electors of Flinders
Division
A Parliament, which no lover of his country will
remember without shame, has at last been allowed
to die; and you have thus regained, so far as the
defective Electoral Law permits, the constitutional
right of controlling your own representatives,
which was taken from you in October last by a
dishonest exercise of the Executive power.
At no period in our history has there been a
more urgent necessity for candour, sincerity, and
courage in the treatment of public affairs. An
honest effort to remove or mitigate the pressing
difficulties of our social and industrial life can no
longer be evaded or delayed. The conscience of
the people is being stirred to its depths; and every
man will have to bear his part in dealing with the
social questions of the day.
One of the most appalling phenomena of modern
times is that in a country such as ours, of limitless
resources and enormous wealth, our lands should
remain unpeopled while our people starve.
Evidences are accumulating from every side,
with a force which no one can overlook, that the
root cause of the social unrest and industrial dis-
tress, which are paralysing the energies of the
human race, is to be sought in faulty conditions of
land tenure.
Under existing systems of taxation every effort
of brain or muscle, by whomsoever or wheresoever
made, tends to increase the value of land and
render its use more costly. Much land is accord-
ingly held back from use in expectation of a rise
in price, and encouragement is given to a most un-
wholesome form of speculation.
Yet every rise in the price of land, which is not
due to the direct application of labour to the soil,
but to the expenditure of public money or the
growth of population, is at the expense of
wages and profits in every industry. Land being
necessary to the carrying on of all pursuits
'and industries —being, in fact, the raw material
of human existence—the citizens of a community
cannot be continuously raising its price against
themselves without crippling their own energies*
Under present conditions men no sooner come
together to enjoy society than, by the mere aggre-
gation of numbers, as if they were haunted by
some fatal destiny, they began to deprive them-
selves of that which is necessary to them as the
air they breathe —the use of land. Must this
Nemesis ever dog the footsteps of the human
race? This is the riddle of the Sphinx which we,
as a nation, must solve, or die I
One most urgent measure of relief is an adjust-
ment of the burdens of taxation to the proper
shoulders. There should bo neither taxes upon
industry nor taxes on improvements; but every
encouragement should be given to those who gain
a livelihood directly from the soil, whether as
farmers, pastoralists or miners, to put whatever
land they occupy to its most productive use.
A small tax upon the unimproved value of land,
which would probably not exceed one penny in the
£ on the true value of land, as ascertained by
periodic valuations after deducting the value of j
all improvements, would not only stimulate in-
dustry by bringing into use much land that is
now closed to settlement, but would make possible
the remission of all duties of Customs and Excise,
except those on stimulants and narcotics, and
thus destroy a wrong-headed system of taxation,
which restricts the purchasing power of all classes
in the community. Further, such a tax would
restore to the State some portion of that increment
to the value of private lands which the State has
itself created. Viewed in this light, a tax on un-
improved land values becomes nothing more than
a just, simple, and general application of the prin-
ciple of Betterment.
The State-earned increment to the value of
private lands in New South Wales amounts to the
enormous sum of .£143,000,000; that is to say,
while the State has parted with these lands for
=647,000,000, of which .£13,000,000 still remain
unpaid, their present unimproved value is
-6190,000,000, while the improvements on them are
separately estimated at .£126,000,000.
No misrepresentation or appeals to class preju-
dice should be allowed to obscure these plain facts.
The Democratic policy in finance is not to increase
but to reduce taxation ; and at the same time to
re adjust its incidence. Nor are those who advo-
cate a tax on land values necessarily supporters of
the single tax. For my part, I would vote for the
continuance of Stamp Duties. Probate Duties
Customs Duties on stimulants and narcotics*
Excise Duties on the same articles, License Fees’
and other miscellaneous methods of obtaining
revenue; so that I can only be called a Single
Taxer by someone who has never learnt arithmetic
and does not know how to count.
As an Australian, I shall devote my beet
energies to aid in bringing about the union of the
colonies without further delay.
William Harding ,
General Secretary,
Sydney,
Mr. G. .Baumann, the big Sydney caterer
and restaurateur, of 107 Pitt-street, announces by
circular that he has reduced his prices all-round
to suit this desolate and hungry epoch. Bau-
mann’s price-list is a wonderful essay on the art
of feeding, and embodies more rich and abstruse
provender than The Bulletin ever heard of
before, but as the staff generally takes a banana
at one o’clock, the document is more interesting
than useful. So far as its knowledge goes,(how-
ever, the tariff is about the lowest, quality con-
sidered, upon record.
Parliamentary Elections.
REDFERN ELECTORATE.
Mr. H.C. HOYLE,
Who is a straight-going Protectionist, is running
for the Red fern seat.
1. —The Parliament will not be complete
without him.
2. —The Country requires his Services.
3. —Red fern must no.t lose him.
4. —He is a Consistent Democrat, a Fearless
Defender of the Working Classes, and a
Staunch Advocate of the National Cause.
As the Country is in need of men with plenty of
backbone, VOTE for HIM to a Man.
RAND WICK ELECTORATE
Gentlemen,—
I respectfully beg to remind you of
the absolute necessity for all sterling Free-
traders, and lovers of freedom and liberty,
to record their votes in my favour on next
Tuesday.
Don’t forget that Freetrade is true Federa-
tion, and that Protection and Federation
are diametrically opposed to each other;
and, further, that it is impossible to sink
the fiscal question.
Ifc will come up in the next Parliament,
and every member will be compelled,
whether he likes or not, to declare which
side he is on.
Again urging all Freetraders to be true to
their principles, and vote according to their
conscience,
I have the honour to be,
Gentlemen,
Yours faithfully,
David Storey .
Allison Road, Randwick,
July 10, 1894.

Parkes has been bombarded with con-
gratulations on the attainment of his 79th birth-
day last Sunday. A Sydney D.T. interviewer
went with a blithesome, springy step to dive into
the old man’s recollections of infancy, and ex-
tracted much information regarding his capacity
for goo-goo utterance and mud-pie manufac-
ture. Singularly enough, however, the veteran
omitted the one thing material, and did not say
that he owes his strength and health, under Pro-
vidence, to a judicious use of Marshall’s Tonic
Nervine, sold at corner of Pitt and Park streets,
Sydney. .
Sir W. Muddle had been speaking for
three solid hours all about Freetrado and its
drawbacks ; extolling Protection and the benefits
to be derived from this system, for the country
in general and his constituents in particular—
provided, of course, he was not left in the cold.
When he and his friends adjourned to refresh
themselves —“ I tell you what, Sir William,” one
of them remarked, looking wise, “I fear you have
not much show. Next time you address electors
see that they are provided with Frossard’s Swiss
Cigars, which are light, aromatic and do not try
the nerves; they will then listen to you like
lambs; they will be as happy as possible, and
will vote for you to a man. The expense would
be but a trifling one, considering that the price is
2s. fid. per bundle of 25.
In this enlightened age of scientific inven-
tions and mechanical contrivances fresh novelties
are constantly coming under public notice.
Messrs. Hannam and Go., Queen’s Place, Sydney,
have patented a very ingenious apparatus for the
supply in less than three minutes of hot water
sufficient for a warm bath. With a mere handful
of shavings and a few chips, sufficient to light an
ordinary fire, Mr. Hannam, having applied a
lighted match, closes the door and within the
time specified, having turned on the cold-water
tap, a supply of hot water is produced not less
than ten gallons at 150 degrees Fahrenheit. The
warmer, in which the heat is used in its passage
through the internal tubes, produces this won-
derful result. A visit to Queen’s Place will con-
vince the most sceptical of the domestic comforts
to be derived from this clever invention.
That enterprising firm, Messrs. Joshua
Bros., Ltd., Melbourne, have succeeded in estab-
lishing for themselves a world-wide reputa-
tion for their Australian brandy, the product
of Australian grapes. They have invested
no less a sum than .£300,000 in this gigantic con-
cern, and during the year 1893 fully 90,000 gallons
were disposed of in the English market. It is
now a favourite brand with the British public,
and has successfully coped with the vigorous com-
petition of French exporters. The Sydney agency
which was established some six months ago, reports
favourably of the support which has been accorded
since its inception. The partial failure which has
attended the grape-crop during recent years
in France has materially affected both the
quantity and quality distilled in that country.
The consequence is that a greater demand has
been created in the consumption of whisky, and
the excessive run on the latter has led to the
exhaustion of old distillments, the natural result
being that Scotch distilleries have been compelled
to send to Australia whiskies of less maturity.
Joshua Bros.’ brandy has a beautiful mellow
flavour, and is devoid of that unpleasant after-
taste on the palate which brandy-drinkers often
experience.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
George Adams has taken prompt action in the
matter of the leakage in one of “ Tattersall’s ’’ sweeps,
complained of by a correspondent in a recent Bulletin.
The blame has been traced to a clerk, at once dismissed;
and all possible precaution has been taken to prevent
information regarding drawers from slipping out in the
future. Further, Adams engages to pay no money except
to the holder of the ticket; and if any drawer wants to
hedge, he can apply to G.A. to do the business for him.
This sets things on a satisfactory basis, and nothing less
was expected from the conductor of “ Taltersall’s,” whose
word is gold with the sporting community of Australia.
• « • •
The Bulletin’s attempt to improve upon
Apsley as a “ tip ” for the G.N. Hurdle Race was a dis-
tinct failure, for poor Bill finished fourth only. There
were but four horses in the race a quarter of a mile from
home, and any chances that might have been possessed
by Neli, Ixion, or Bill aforesaid, were probably washed
away by rain. The light-weight won easily through the
heavy going. Just now the Messrs. Manifold are having
the good time that comes to good sportsmen occasionally,
wherefore Corangamite seems the least unwise investment
for Saturday’s Steeplechase. If|Busaco should start a hot
favourite he may be reckoned to have a great show, and
Mikado 11. is bound to carry top-weight into the front
rank, anyway. Trojan’s queer leg is, perhaps, not queer
enough to ruin his chance of taking the race a second
time, but on the principle that nothing succeeds like
success, Corangamite is better worth backing than any of
them.

Bob Ellis, physically the soundest and best of
the N.S.W. older jockeys, is having a run of luck just
now. With rarely more than one mount he has managed
to score a win almost every Saturday for some time past.

Lord Rosebery, it appears, was wildly excited by
the victories of Ladas in the Guineas and Derby, and Lon-
don Referee suggests that he will lose his youthful appear-
ance if he owns many more crack racers and waxes so

He merely cultivated a calm demeanour when his
favourites got beaten.

S.A. v. Vic. interprovinoial football match is an
annual farce for which the former province is responsible.
Last season, when the match was played in Adelaide, the
Port Adelaide club was in Melbourne, thus weakening the
S.A. team. This year the Norwood club refused to go to
Melbourne second-class and no Norwood player was in-
cluded. What sort of an amateur is the player who has
all his expenses paid and then refuses to travel other than
first-class ?
• ♦ • •
Victorian Legislative Assembly has thrown out
Murray’s Totalisator Bill by 28 to 16 votes, although
Murray urged that the tote would cure that hitherto
supposedly incurable disease known as “metallic fever.’’
Frank Madden opposed the Bill on the sapient ground
that “the totalisator would not improve the breed of
horses, because owners would have no means of recouping
their expenses.” Ergo: Owners now have means? But
this opens up too many dark labyrinths.

Ante-post betting on the G.N. Hurdle Race
was scarcely worth mentioning this year, although not
relatively tamer than the Derby and Cup, which used to

• ♦ * •
Whilst the Vio. Assembly was throwing out its
Totalisator Bill on the alleged ground that it would
increase betting, the Maoriland House of Representatives
was objecting to any interference with the machine in
that country. There the members can speak as experi-
enced critics.
• • * •
Frank Madden gob off some hyper-puerile bal-
derdash on the tote question in Vic. Assembly. He even
discovered a quotation from some unknown authority—
a hook-nosed tobacconist, perhaps—“ Anything is good
enough to run under the ‘ tote.’ ” For the life of him
Madden could not have found a grain of sense in his sug-
gestion, Under the tote system of betting a horse
couldn’t be good enough to run unless it were good
enough to win occasionally, whereas, with a bookmaker to
work for him, an owner may get some “ milking " money
out of a three-legged animal. The immediate effect of
the tote is to produce a lively competition between good
horses, and reduce the number of stiff ’uns. And com
petition in machine-betting must result in good odds all
round.
• * • •
Although the Melb. dailies, without exception,
continue to grovel before the football god, the glory of
that unseemly idol is fading apace. It may be that the
marked superiority of one score of players over all the
other scores of players accounts somewhat for diminished
attendances at matches and the dearth of football fights
at street corners, but anyhow the game must have passed
the meridian of its popularity. Intelligent people are
sick of trying to feel an interest in the legions of Billies,
and Petes, and Jacks, and Geordies who are all of them
so wonderful that the evening rags must publish hideous
portraits of them. There are too many distinguish* d
footballers for a man to remember their names, if he
wants to remember anything else in this life. Also, they
are too monotonously brainless in appearance to be dis-
tinguishable one from the other, except to the trained
larrikin eye. It generally happens that a respectable
enthusiast cheers Geordie in mistake for Billy, and when
he makes up his mind to get excited over the real Billy
he wastes his lung-power over Pete, or Sandy, or Mac.
He discovers his errors occasionally, arid bis enthusiasm
becomes damp.
• * -
What is the reason of the kicking of Australian-
game footballers being so much superior to the Rugby
players? Forty yards is reckoned quite a fair distance
among the latter, while the Melb. or Adelaide man who
couldn’t do ten or fifteen additional yards would be soon
put out of a senior team, unless other points of his play
compensated.
• • • •
Proudfoot and Collins, Melbourne’s two giant
footballers, are both members of the police force, and
play respectively for the Collingwood and St. Kilda clubs;
the bell-bottomed, tobacoo-juioe-stained barrackers of
those suburbs being in the uncertain position of either
having to abandon their patriotism or to howl eulogies at
a hated oopman, who next week may be "lumbering”
them for insulting behaviour.
• • * •
No less than six fights between Sydney and
Melb. representative stoushers have taken place within
the last month down South. So far, the result would
infer that the smellful air of the Yarra is more conducive
to good boxing than Harbour ozone. There is a belief
that most of these fights were arranged privately by
Parkes and Patterson to stave off Federation.
* ♦ • •
Dan Keely, a rising Vio. pug., is matched to
fight Pyrmont Quigley at an early date. Keely is a man
of capitalistic build-in fact, stripped, his body and limbs
are smooth as bladders of lard, not a line or muscle show-
ing, but nevertheless he is about the smartest heavy-
weight the writer has yet seen. If he can stand punish-
ment and last out he should shortly be an aspirant for
the championship with a fair probability of obtaining it*
Perhaps the Romans weren’t such fools, after all, in train-
ing their pugilists “ fat.”
• • • •
St. James Gazette digs out an old story with
a moral in it for the backers of horses. A rich and enter-
prising sport used to keep an elaborate record of the
form of all the great horses of the day. He worked the
results out with arithmetical precision—and then he lost
the book, and with it all the plans for a whole season’s
racing campaign. His anguished advertisements all
failed to bring back the missing volume, but it turned up
at last in the pocket of a suicide in the Thames. Said
suicide had backed the tips until they made him home-
less, and then he ballasted himself with the book and
went overboard.

street, Melbourne.

HERR RASSMUSSEN, PH.D.
A Few Sketches of His Interesting Life and
Work.
Here. Rassmussen,' who is known throughout
the world as “The Celebrated Danish Her-
balist,” was born in the little kingdom of Den-
mark, shortly after its last disastrous war with
Germany. His parents being wealthy, and
belonging to the most influential class, he
received a first-class education, and displayed
exceptional talent in the science of botany and
botanical chemistry, and very soon surpassed
everyone of his fellow scholars in those sub-
jects, and eventually made the science of botany
his life-study.
Finding his native country too small, and its
flora too limited to extend his botanical re-
searches, he left for Australia, and landed here
in the beginning of 1879. His first object was
to learn the English language and become
familiar with the customs and habits of his
adopted country, as well as with its botanical
history and medicinal flora. Herr Rassmussen
had quite his share of the troubles and difficul-
ties which usually befall a newcomer in Aus-
tralia ; but he soon overcame every difficulty,
and after five years’ travelling through all the
colonies, investigating every known Australian
medicinal plant, and discovering many new
ones, he eventually settled down permanently
in Sydney in 1883, and then commenced a
most noble mission—that of turning his most
wonderful botanical discoveries to the benefit
and relief of suffering humanity. Herr Rass-
mussen succeeded in this gigantic undertaking
far beyond all expectations, and his name very
soon became a household word, not only in
every Australian camp, village and city, but
also throughout Europe, Africa and America.
Space will not permit to mention the
numerous cures effected by his now world-
famed Alkaline Remedies, suffice it to say:
that thousands of original testimonials may be
inspected by anyone at his Head Depot, 547
George Street, Sydney, many of which may
also be seen in his celebrated work, “The
Natural Doctor.”
There is no doubt whatever that Herr Rass-
mussen deserves all the success and praise which
he has met with, because he is a most scientific,
conscientious and genuine herbalist—his last
ten years record being so wonderful that his
remedies must have cured more human suffering
in that period than is usually effected by a
hundred average doctors in a similar number of
years. He does not hold any consultation
similar to a doctor; his whole business is
purely a Proprietary Herbal Remedy Business.
He simply prepares a genuine herbal remedy
specially for the nerves, and another for the blood,
and so on, which can always be relied upon.
He prints on the box containing the remedy its
price and all advice as to doses and diet and
other particulars, and advertises same, which
is the fairest way possible to conduct a business
of this kind. Any information or particulars
desired are given or sent absolutely free of
charge. Pamphlets are sent post free to any
person desiring one, containing all particulars
and many useful and valuable hints on health.
Herr Rassmussen has very large branches
of his extensive business in Melbourne,
Brisbane, Adelaide, Perth, Wellington (N.Z.),
and throughout the world. He is an
honorary member of several scientific socie-
ties, a gold medalist, diplomee and Hon.
Corr. Mem. of the Academic Parisienne des
Inventeurs, France ; last year he received the
Honorary University degree of Doctor of
Philosophy ; in 1887, he was presented with a
magnificent gold medal by several of his cured
patrons in Sydney, and also awarded a silver
medal in Denmark, 1888, a bronze medal,
Melbourne, 1893; and in 1891 he issued his
“ Natural Doctor,” a herbal medical work of a
high standard for which he received great
praise from the press, and which is now trans-
lated into many languages.

Peter Maher, the “Irish champion,” is being
boomed in America since his defeat of the old darkey,
George Godfrey. John L. Sullivan and others now swear
by him, no doubt in the hope of getting someone to take
down Corbett. As Australians Goddard and Fitzsimmons
made easy meat ot the Irishman, it is a forlorn hope
indeed.
• • * *
Of course, we all how! at Corbett’s delaying of
a meeting with Peter Jackson, but what a fool the Yank
would be to chance losing such a money-making concern
as the championship ! Times have changed, in the old
days of the ring, a champion was in luck if he made
enough out of a couple ot hours’ smashing match to last
him until his features had resumed something like their
normal condition, and some other aspirant could raise a
score or two of backing. To-day the pugilistic champion-
ship is a marketable commodity. The holder wears a
frock-coat and glossy tall-hat. He is sought after by all
sorts and conditions of men—and women—and his title is
worth so many pounds a week in hard cash. Small
wonder he puts off, until fairly cornered, the chance of
parting with such a bonanza.
♦ • * »
W. G. Grace, junr., is a slim, smallish, rather
delicate-looking young man, who hats in spectacles. Thus
the athletic genius, like others, Jo* s not pass it on.
.*. * • »
Editor Bulletin,— Re the blind sportsmen of
King-street, Sydney. A few years ago there lived in
Waverley a middle-aged man who was totally blind. He
was a tobacconist and his shop was the resort of most of
the sporting residents, for “Oid Crad ” was a sporting
encyclopedia. He attended all Randwick and suburban
meetings, and at times even went as far as the Hawkes-
bury. For a long while he laid the odds on the Flat or in
the St. Leger reserves at these places with the assistance
of a mate, Crad carrying the bag and shouting the odds
while the mate did the booking. He was able to pick a
sovereign or any other coin out of a handful, and I never
knew him to give incorrect change. After hearing the
weights for any big event read over a couple of times he
could invariably tell you any horse’s handicap without a
mistake. I have heard him describe a race, how it was
run, what led at such a point, where the favourite was at
the turn, how he was going, and whether the jock, was
sitting still or riding—in fact, he could give a most
minute description of the race from start to finish, and ot
how it was won and lost. “ When I seen Tommy Hales
sitting still on Abercorn at the turn, and Mat. Harris
riding Melos, I knew it was ail over "—and so on. When
the interprovineial yacht-races were being sailed here I
walked into his shop one night and happened to mention
that I had witnessed the race from Bellevue Hill. “Ah !”
said he, “ I wish someone had called in for me. There’s
nothing I like to see so much as a good yacht-race.’^—
Bat.

George Upward writes “ Rowing Notes ” in the
Leader—perhaps the best of the kind in any Australian
journal.
* * * *
Stevenson, the late brilliant three-quarter back
of the Dunedin Pirates F.C., is now playing- with the
Manningham Club, of Bradford, Eng., the crack team of
last season. The Bradfords wouldn't give him a show at
first but now are making big bids for his services.
• * * ♦
At the various Melb. boxing matches it is
curious to see how the order of “ Pipes out,” usually
given at the beginning of each evening, is regarded. In
the two-shilling quarter, smoking stops almost instantly
while, in the five and ten-shilling seats, the gilded youths
and dead beats continue to pollute the air that the boxers
have to breathe, until the force of cries from the other
parts of the house frightens them into obedience.
* » * *
Little Johnnie Sheridan, when at Melb. Opera
House, grovelled to his bookmaker patrons by singing
topical verses condemning the tote. The confusion of
Johnnie’s arguments was utterly reckless. He would
start with the statement that nothing under Heaven
could give greater satisfaction than Ikey Mo. Then the
misguided comedian would admit the tote’s claims to
regard by promising that his Hebrew friends would, if
necessary, “lay even lonyer odds” than the accursed
machine. Whereat the little mob of jewelled “metal-
licians ” always cheered vigorously, but never a gallery-
boy had wit enough to remark that a man who betted
only a little more liberally than the tote might as well
give guineas for pounds.
♦ ♦ * •
The revelations over a recent running match—
one man swore in court that he gave the other money to
put on—prove that footracing is as rotten as ever.
* * • *
It is melancholy to find ex-“ demon ” Spofforth
still maundering on about the Australia v. England match
of 1882. Even more melancholy to find newspapers
solemnly quoting Spoff.’s opinions from his latest book.
Who cares whether the extinct volcano “ firmly believes ”
that somebody lost the match by not stepping out to hit,
or Grace has never swerved from his conviction that
another fellow might have changed the result by pursuing
different tactics ? Bah !
• * * •
George Buhear (writes The Bulletin’s London
correspondent) has suddenly improved out of ail recogni-
tion, and is now sculling in such form that, almost
beyond all doubt, he would very easily turn the tables
upon some of his recent conquerors, including the re-
doubtable Sullivan himself. It is even said that Sullivan
is not by any means anxious to meet him. Buhear is now
“gaffered” by Pony Moore, so money is not lacking.
* * • »
Thechances in the coming Jackson-Corbettaffair
may be summed up thus. The men met two or three
years ago, w’len honours were even. Jackson was then
in had condition and suffering from a sprained ankle, they
say. Yarns of this kind, however, are suspicious, for, as
“ Pendragon ” once wrote, no defeated pugilist acknow-
ledges himself fit. And, in this case, a draw was tanta-
mount to a defeat with Jackson, who, probably, held his
opponent too cheap, and, anyway, could not have re-
cover d from Masonic junketings during his flying visit
to Australia. But Corbett was (hen almost a novice,
facing a man of world-wide prestige. To-day, on the
contrary, the white man has the credit of having put
dowm Mitchell, Sullivan and Kilrain, and can face Jackson
far u more confidence. Then he is a careful-flying
leuow who throws no chances away. Time, too, has beta
on ms own side. In the ordinary course of nature, ho
must have gained strength and stamina, while Peter must
have lost, "there is an old sporting aphorism—•• Youth
will be served.”

a fashionable doctor undertook to do the job at
the bazaar half-price, and give the money to the
kirk, up came the beauties, baring their arras,
and the reverends said, behold it was very good.

Dear Bulletin,—What is Tyson to Skin-
flee, the N.S.W. squatter, who sold his station
for ■£40,000 cash and declined to give a receipt for

M'Burney, Wallis & Co.’s Report.
This week has been marked by the receipt of a cable-
gram from our London agents quoting a 5 per cent, rise
in wool, except for coarse bred. Owing to the.receipt of
this good news we were enabled to make some very satis-
factory private sales. We would caution the wool-grower
against the expectation that the rise in wool will develope
into a large increase. We still think the real test must be
looked for when our sales open here, and we have good
reason to believe we will then be in a position to report
better prices to those who entrust their wool to our care.
The recent good news from London seems to have given
a stimulus to operations at the sheep sales during the
week, and the large number of sheep offered were worked
off so readily that a looker-on would be inclined to think
money is not as tight in our colony as we are led to
believe.
The abolition of the American Import Duty on Wool
seems to progress favourably, and if not protracted too
long must have a favourable impression on the Sydney
wool sales of the coming season.
July 7, 1894.

REVELATIONS
of an ex-Agent of the Paris
Morals Police, translated
from “La Lanterne,” and issued by the National (London)
Association for Repeal of the Contagious Disease Acts.
The lesson to be learnt is obvious to all who have a
knowledge of the frightful personal power given to a
special class of spy-police in England over all women in
the districts where the Acts are in operation, and should
the Acts be extended to the whole of the United King-
dom, and also to the Colonies, who will dare to say that
the liberty of all English women will not be seriously
imperilled? Post free, in sealed envelope, Is. 6d. Only
Of the Agent, Mr. A. Jopling, Box 695, 0.P.0., Sydney.

Re bullet-proof clothing. Many late ex-
periments- are denounced as mere tricks. The
force of impacts is known to experts. A regula-
tion bullet fired from a regulation rifle strikes with
the power of three thousand pounds, or a ton and
a-half, and this would kill a bullock, even if_ the
bullet did not penetrate the prepared medium.
The recoil of such a rifle is equal to a blow of one
hundred pounds, and yet the music-hall chit of
sixteen is supposed to oppose these forces with
perfect impunity and the set smile of a circus-
rider.
A new and rather promising paper —the
Searchlight, published at Auckland (M.L.) —has
come to hand. It contains eleven pages of read-
ing, about three of which are interesting matter
borrowed from The Bulletin. Most of these
borrowings are not acknowledged in any way,
and are cheerfully passed off as the Searchlight’s
own original ideas. Those columns which are not
borrowed are tolerably dreary, and the illustra-
tions look as if they had been engraved with a
dinner-fork.
Australia last year contributed £4OO more
to the China-Ireland Mission than in the year
preceding. That means several things, but
chiefly that religious fanaticism is not extinct in
Australia. In a year of unexampled suffering
and distress, the godly, under the influence of
their spiritual guides, were willing to take ,£4OO
from their starving neighbours for a fad which
has not even success to recommend it; seeing
that of all the proselytising agencies at work
among “ the poor heathen*” the G.I. M. has the
smallest return in converts in proportion to the
magnitude of its machinery.
A recent criticism of the work of Charles
Keene,- the beslavered London Punch artist,
expresses a pretty general opinion : “ Keene drew
one, and only one, scratchy type of comic man —
who was in turn butler, gardener, publican, duke,
college don, cabman and farmer —for 30 years in
Punch. He could not draw a pretty woman, a
gentleman, a lady, a child or a horse to save his
life. After death he has been accorded as much
insincere praise as Wordsworth himself.” Even
Du Maurier has more variety than Keene, for the
one-eyed Frenchman certainly depicts the changes
in drawing-room furniture and dresses as the
years roll on. But perhaps D.M. has ceased to
illustrate the cockney standard “ funny ” paper.
From a private letter received in Sydney
from Fort Salisbury, Mashonaland :
During the recent war we were nearly starved ; nothing
to eat but sodden Kaffir corn. We received no pay, but a
farm, God knows where, in Matabeleland. The Chartered
Co. have occupied Matabeleland now, and are selling
township blocks in Buluwayo at £4O each. It is a fine
country compared with this dreadful fever-stricken hole.
Our hospital is full of fever-patienls just now'. Everyone
gets it sooner or later. lam surprised at your account of
wages in Australia. In the Transvaal and Orange Free
State stonemasons and bricklayers, when they work, get
30s. per day, and carpenters 255. Work here at Fort
Salisbury is completely at a standstill owing to the rains
stopping all traffic. We have a Bishop of Mashonaland
now. I see that, in an interview, he denies that the
Matabele prisoners of the late war w'ere shot, or ill-
treated in any way. Also he praised “the devotion of the
Chartered Co.’s doctors 1” I wonder where he expects to
go to when he dies?
Rev. Thomas Broughton Tress, who ha s
cared for the spiritual needs of Woolloomooloo for
12 years, with a salary of £4OO and residence,
received an offer of £6OO and residence from
Richmond, Vic. After “careful meditation and
prayer” he perceived a distinct “divinity” in
the “call,” and, of course, like a faithful soldier,
obeyed. At the send-off, the other night, the rev.
gentleman “seemed much affected.”:—
THE MUG’S IDEA.
Whilst sitting here it just struck me
I’m where hundreds would like to be.
But what a great surprise, old boy,
For them that come their hick to try ;
The boomers with their skilful art,
Aided by the press, have done it smart
By advertising with fiendish glee
The extensive wealth of Coolgardie.
But there’s a great surprise in store
For them that tramp the sa <1 plains o’er,
As the golden country on the map
Is found to be nought but a trap,
And it’s a fact it kind of narks
To know we’re had by m ney sharks.
Yes, you may bet the business lot
Reckon to have us in their pot.
For prices here are just hell-hot,
Too bad for any sinner’s lot.
With onions a bob per pound
Surely there is grumbling ground,
But we stick to our dishes and go dry-blowing,
The longer we work the more we’re owing,
And all but a tory would excuse our mag
O’er the cost of filling a water-bag.
Yet bits of bait are still being set
To get fresh mugs into the net.
Some editor reports a find . ..
Foy which he’s been well paid and wine d
By some shrewd speculative nob
Whose working fakes upon this job ;
But, God, the prayers that's raised so thick
Forbid to fall upon this clique.
They work it nicely to appear
That water’s all that’s wanted here.
This water-cry is cronk, you bet,
For sixty yards down it is always met.
With these remarks I’ll end my wail,
I guess it’s getting rather stale ;
But if an enemy you wish to pay
By all means get him o’er this way.
A MEREWETRER MUG.
There came a call from Richmond, Vic.,
It was a call divine,
For T. B. Tress to fight Old Nick
Across the border-line ;
And one of Thomas Broughton’s eyes
Did gently close its lid,
The other gazed into the skies
And read—“ Six hundred quid 1”
Clear came that call—“ O come, O come 1
You need not long delay ’’ —
And T.B. softly murmured “ Hum,
I think I’d better pray.
And, if it be bright Heaven’s choice,
To Richmond I will ‘ skid
He prayed, and lo! a “still, small voice"
Said, “Take six hundred quid !”
And goodly people gathered round
To cheer him and to bless.
And speak of all the virtues found
In Thomas Broughton Tress—
The “spirit moveth in his heart"
And Tress said, “ Yes, it did,
It makes me draw the Gospel cart 1” ,
(And draw “ Six hundred quid I’*);

OF
FORECLOSURE
UNDER REAL
PROPERTY
ACT.
In the matter of the application of Charles Gibson Millar and Edwin Franks Millar, mortgagees
under memorandum of mortgage from John King, No. 171,355, dated 30th September, 1890, over
the land comprised in Crown Grants, vob 475, fol. 139 ; vol. 543, fol. 91; vol. 581, fob 108 ; vol. 751
fob 45 ; vol. 757. fob 173 ; vol. 787, fob 227; vol. 425, fob 59; vob 997, tols. 233 and 234: vol. 999,
fola. 168 to 199 inclusive ; and vob 1015, fob 169.
WHEREAS the abovenamed mortgagees have made application for an order of foreclosure of the
mortgage above referred to, accompanied by such evidence of default for six months and other facts,
as required by section 113 of the Real Property Act : Notice is hereby given, pursuant to the said Act,
that the land comprised within the said mortgage, as hereunder particularised, is offered and will
remain open for sale until the 17th August, 1894, which date has been appointed as the time upon or
after which I, the Registrar-General, will issue to the applicant an order for foreclosure, which will
exclude all claim by the abovenamed mortgagor, and of any person claiming through or under him,
unless in the interval a sufficient amount has been realised by the sale of such land to satisfy the prin-
cipal and interest moneys due, and all costs and expenses occasioned by such proceedings, and interest
up to the time of such sale.
beth-street, Sydney.
Dated at Sydney this 6th day of July, 1894, at the Registrar-General’s Department.
CHARLES PINHEY, Registrar-General.

Rats.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We smuggled in the bag all right—
M'Pherson shut his cabm-door—
When smoke and grog half dimmed fyis sight
We let the rats loose on the floor,
All painted white and pink and blue.
“Good God!” he shrieked; “you chaps— look
there!”
Right, left, and up and down they flew —
We stared, and calmly asked him, “ Where!’*
He shifted in his seat, flushed red,
And bit his lip. Whilst all around
The din would have aroused the dead
We swore we couldn’t hear a sound.
We raised our eyebrows as we spoke,
We shook our heads and took our hats.
He stammered—“ It was all a joke !
You chaps ! I never saw those rats I"
G. ESSEX EVANS.
Potatoes were at famine price, in Melb.
this time last year. It led to great planting in
Gippsland, the failure being of the Western crops.
Now potatoes are 501bs. for a shilling retail in
Melb.
A mice-plague rages in some of the S.A.
farming districts. During one week in June,
20,520 of the little wheat consumers were killed
at Koolnnga, the record for one night being 4686.
In other localities they measure corpses by the
bushel and the gallon.
Sydney Sunday Times gives free medical
advice, but so far it apparently has little vogue in
its quack-doctor capacity. The most momentous
prescription yet seen in its columns was long ago
and consisted of three words only ; “ Take four
pills.”
“A family of 12 children, and an invalid
wife, cause no reproach to those who fill the most
holy offices of the church, but a healthy, happy
mother and child, outside the bounds of legal
wedlock, are pictures of sin.—Mrs. Cady Stanton,
in Westminster Review.
During ’93, England imported 2,514,541
carcases of frozen sheep and lambs, and 171,640
quarters and pieces of beef, from Maoriland and
Australia, without counting 1,373,723 carcases
from the River Plate. Not a carcase per head for
the population of London alone.
Shortly after the wreck of the American
steamship the Monumental City on Talleuberger
Island, in 1854, rabbits were placed on the island
by the Vic. Government for the use of castaways.
Through eating certain herbs which grow there,
their flesh has become almost poisonous.
Maoriland law now compels shopkeepers
and others to supply seats for the use of their
female employes, who formerly had to stand on
their weary feet for ten or twelve hours per diem.
The seats are supplied accordingly, but the tired
shop-girl who sits down on one of them is “sacked”
on the instant, so things are very much as they
were.
Dear Bulletin, —A champion mean man
was he who shipped with his motherless children
on board an interprovincial steamer for a five
days’ sea voyage. The good man took a saloon
ticket, but the young ones had to go forward to
the steerage, with strict injunctions not to know
their pa when they saw him abaft the funnel.—
Shellback.
Dear Bulletin, —You have not yet dis-
covered the meanest man. He lives in the Goul-
burn valley, and owns a magnificent orchard.
Last grape-cutting a child of his, playing with a
dog, fell into the river, and was drowning when
an old man went placidly to the rescue, and
brought the little girl safely to land. What did
the wealthy father give the hero? Guess again.
A recipe for a bad cold. — Snell.
Colemane & Sons’ Special Eucal. Oil is A one.
The meek and harmless Chinaman soon
becomes aggressive when he gets the upper hand.
When the big Chinese rush to the Palmer set in,
about 1880, and there were 7000 Chinese to about
300 Europeans, the proud white was told every
day: “What for you Engliseeman come here?
This Chinaman rush. Engliseeman no likee
Chinaman to go Englisamanee rush.” And at one
Carpentaria town, a few years ago, all the stores
were in the hands of Chinese, and a drover, want-
ing a bit of meat, overheard John tell a woman,
who brought back a cancerous lump of bone:
“Lookaheah, missee ! Suppo you wantem meat
gotem no bone >ou buyem bit of
libbah, thasaw ! ” After that he went out and
killed a beast of his own. Things are better now,
thanks to the bush-unions.
Colemane & Sons’ Eucal. Loz. are for the voice.
The British Medical Journal publishes
an interesting story by Dr. J. R. Wolfe, of his
successful eye-operation in the case of M'Outcheon,
of the Commercial Bank. Dr. Wolfe, who
didn’t arrive in Melbourne until the end of Nov.
last, found Mr. M’Cutcheon suffering from one eye
blind and the other an all but “goner,” There
was a detachment of the retina of both eyes.
The specialist, to the surprise of the local faculty,
elected to perform upon the optic that was said to
be hopelessly gone. Three weeks later it was
equal to reading the time from a watch, whilst
the other eye, upon which no operation had been
attempted thus far, was wonderfully improved.
The case is destined to become a classic in the
literature of ophthalmia.
The successful removal of Piles of the
most stubborn description is being effected by
Sharp’s new “Cannalin” treatment, large quan-
tities of which are being forwarded to sufferers
throughout the colonies. It is obtainable, with
advice, from 257 George-street, Sydney.
When the Australian mail reaches Van-
couver, the artless local pressman extracts all the
murders, suicides, fires, brutal assaults, and corpses
found in the various harbours out of all the papers,
and summarises them for the benefit of his readers.
Naturally he gets the same outrage several times
over. Consequently the Australian record for
last March consisted of 337 captured burglars, 23
of them being shot dead in the act. In the same
month, 1000 unemployed waited on Premier Dibbs,
who told them, according to the Canadian press-
man, to “go and starve,” whereupon there was a
riot, and 100 arrests were made. Also a man in
Adelaide cut the throats of eight women, and
there was a dismal horror concerning a whole
family of small children eaten by pigs.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
19 of this issue.
The last Saturday Review mentions,
what is already well-known, that John Wesley
was “a very leaf in the flame,” for his innocent
amours. The latest exhumed is with Miss Pelhsm-
Dale when she was 16 and he 63.

O Heart!
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“ O heart I by dreams and fancies led#
By vain delusions fanned and fed,
There is no love,” I said.
“So rest thee; e’en though rest be rust.
The truth of things is writ in duet,
And all men’s love is lust I ”
As one smit down before his time—
Smit down to earth from heights sublime,
I fell, before my prime;
And hope went down, and on the day
Impenetrable darkness lay,
And so—l lost my way.
I lost the lines of height and hill,
I lost the power to work my will,
And my cold heart lay still.
Till, in the desert as I stood
In bitterness and solitude,
I deemed that death were good.
But, when there seemed no deeper deep,
I felt my soul rise up in sleep
And swell to flood, from neap.
I saw a light that flashed like flame,
I heard a voice that called my name.
And then, O God ! she came :
With eyes whose lights no sunsets knew,
Nor all the star-depths through and through,
Nor skies, intense with blue.
No subtle scents the seawinds bear,
Nor all the summer’s blossoms were
As fragrant as her hair.
And softly as the murmuring sea,
Or winds that whisper o’er a lea,
She said, “Come thou with me !”
And, hand in hand, we passed away
Through meadowlands with wild flowers gay,
To meet the newborn day.
Behind the hill we saw the sun
Through silver streams of radiance run,
With souls that blent in one.
And ere the day had flamed to flower.
In one too brief and blissful hour,
I knew my whole soul’s power.
Beneath her spell it seemed to grow
And gather strength, and swell and flow
From line to sky-line low,
And, like an uncaged snow-white dove,
To soar through fields of light above,
On new-found wings of love.
But on we went o’er down and lea.
Through glades made glad with melody,
Until we reached the sea.
And on its softly breathing shore
She said, “ My time on earth is o’er,
And we shall meet no more !
“The bond of love that here we tie
Shall lift thy soul from earth to sky,
And my kiss sanctify.
“Though all thy days my face shall be
A bygone dream, thou wilt give me
Thy life, ungrudgingly.
“ Wear then for me this flower of song,
And when the days to come grow long,
For my sake be thou strong.
“ I am henceforth of thee a part,
Thy inmost, undivided heart,”
She said —“ My name is Art 1”

“ The King of Greece is very polite. He
understands twelve languages, and never speaks
angrily to his queen in a language that she com-
prehends.” Feminine comment: “ The wretch !’
The Southern Cross Hotel, Bourke-street,
West Melb., where the alleged murder and
attempted suicide took place, was for a consider-
able period the headquarters of Bishop Perry,
when he first arrived in Victoria.
The prediction of the first Queensland
Premier (now Sir Robert Herbert) that the time
was not far off when Brisbane would possess
magnificent shops, with splendid stocks, and
crowds of customers which would vie with the
shops of London, is fulfilled to the letter to-day at
Finney, Isles & Co.’s establishment. Judging by
the continuous stream of balls containing cash
travelling along the cash-railways to the main
desk from all parts of the building, Mr. Finney '
has certainly solid grounds for saying times are
improving rapidly.
A picture of an American prohibition
city ;
In Lewiston the gin-palaces are under the streets, and
a labyrinth of passages known only to “ members in good
standing ” leads to the interior, with ever and anon a
door marked “ pull ” when it means “ push,” and
‘‘push ” when it means to the initiated “pull." This
confuses the officer, so that by the time he gets in fairly
liie decanters have been emptied dow n the sink, followed
by an acid that removes all traces, even of the odour. A
guard is above both day and night, whose duty is to press
the button when the sheriff enters, and when he gets at
lart through this devious way he finds the bar-tender
engaged in lathering and shaving a customer or shampoo-
ing him with a bottle of Angostura bitters.
SNAKE POISON AND HUMAN POISON.
Thu bite of a rattlesnake is almost always fatal to
man. Yet this snake is never affected by the
injection of its own poison into its own blood,
neither when enraged it bites itself nor when its
venom is injected by artificial means to test the
question. Speaking on the latter point Dr. S.
Weir Mitchell, an expert on snake poison, says,
“ I have over and over tried this experiment, but
in no case have I seen death result. Why should
this be ? ”
Dr. Mitchell further says : “The many noxious
compounds man carries in his liver and gastric
glands (the stomach) are fatal if they enter the
blood in any large amount. There is scarcely an
organ in man’s body which is not a possible source
of poison to him. Small doses are constantly
passing into and out of his blood. The question
is whether he can get rid of it as fast as it ac-
cumulates.”
Alluding to death by rheumatism, gout, and
kidney disease, an eminent London physician says:
“Thus man is poisoned by the products of his own
body.”
Now let us cite a passage from a man’s experience
as related by himself. The time extends over a
period from March, 1888, to May, 1889, more than
a year. He says :“ My head used to whirl around,
and I had frequent bouts of faintness and giddi-
ness ; and at such times I used to fall down, no
matter where I was. This would occur two or three
times a day. For three weeks I was confined to
my bed. I grew gradually weaker and weaker
and lost a deal of sleep. I felt more tired in the
morning than when I went to bed. After a while
I became nervous, and my legs trembled and shook
under me to such a degree that I feared to walk
out. I had great pain in my kidneys, and the
secretion which I voided from them was thick and
yellow as the yolk of an egg. Month after month
passed and I failed more and more, and could
hardly crawl about.
“I had a doctor attending me, but his medicines
did not benefit me. He said my liver and kidneys
were in a bad way, and that he never saw secretions
passed in such a state. After treating me six
months, he told me that medicine could do no
more for me and advised me to go to a hospital.
I went to Peterborough hospital, but got worse
while there. The hospital doctors refused to tell
me what ailed me. Having spent two months
there, I got anxious and returned to my home,
utterly disheartened. I continued to send to the
hospital for medicines, which I took for three
months longer. I was now so emaciated that my
friends who came to see me said I would never
get well.
“In this condition I continued until May, 1889,
when one day an umbrella vendor called at my
house, and, seeing how ill I was, said his wife had
been cured of a serious illness by Mother Seigel’s
Curative Syrup. He spoke so earnestly of it that
I determined to try it. After taking the Syrup
for ten days I felt in better spirits; my food
agreed with me, and from that time I gained
strength daily. Persevering with it, I was soon
able to return to my work as healthy and strong
as ever. Since then I have been in the best of
health. You are at liberty to publish the above
facts, and I will gladly reply to any enquiries.
Yours truly (Signed), Read Welfare, Ramsey,
St. Mary’s, Hunts, February 18th, 1892.”
No brief comment can do justice to this remark-
able case. What the public needs to know and*to
remember is this: Mr. Welfare’s whole system
was poisoned by the products of a torpid and
inactive digestion. These had entered his blood,
as Dr. Mitchell describes. The nervous system
was disordered and half paralysed; hence the
faintness and falling fits. Rattlesnake poison
kills by paralysing the nerves which actuate the
lungs; it kills by suffocation. Human poison,
arising from indigestion and dyspepsia, always
operates in the same direction, causing asthma
in its worst forms. It then attacks the
heart and kidneys, causing the state of things
Mr. Welfare mentions. Nothing more noxious,
or, in the end, surely fatal exists in any poisonous
reptile. And yet people trifle with the disease !
and doctors seem not to understand it.
Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup cures by stimu-
lating the kidneys, skin, and bowels, and toning
the gastric glands.
Who, then, is man’s most deadly enemy ? Care-
less and ignorant man himself. Use the remedy
then when the earliest symptoms appear.
OUT ON THE DEEP.
A westerly wind is blowing,
Tis furious, fast, and free ;
And the trim-built barque is going
To her haven beyond the sea ;
And the passengers murmur, knowing
How ill they will shortly be.
The steward, no doubt, is handing
The basins to those distress’d ;
Our hero, you see, is standing
With his hand on his manly breast;
He wishes he might be landing,
And so give the ocean “ best.”
But ST. JACOBS OIL, the tempest to foil,
Is pour’d on the foaming main ;
And anon comes peace, as the waves decrease,
For ST. JACOB can conquer pain.
