Man and Woman.
Editor Bulletin, —Civilised life has its com-
pensations. The world is too much with us;
our youth is age, and our age bankruptcy ; we
grow anaemic, neurotic, paralytic ; but we can
enjoy Havelock Ellis’s new book, “Man and
Woman,” in the Contemporary Science series.
The unhappy happy-savage knows naught of
contemporary science. His iood dangles from
every bough, his drink is the spring (when he
can get nothing better); but his brain is not
complex enough to realise the force of the
charming reservation in the statement that “ at
Belluno, in Italy, it is customary for a priest and
a naked young girl to go (separately as a rule)
early in the morning into the fields to drive
away the caterpillars.” He pays no taxes, the
landlord never worries him; but he cannot
have the pure joy of reading that “ very likely
one of the reasons for the persistent habit of
tightening up the girth among Christian
damsels is that such constriction renders the
breathing thoracic, and so advertises the
alluring bosom by keeping it in constant and
manifest movement. The heaving of a sub-
clavicular sigh is likely to cause more sensation
than the heaving of an epigastric or umbilical
sigh.” He is frankly unconventional and im-
moral, no policeman “ runs him in,” no “ fatal
fourth ” whitens his wool; but he lives and
dies unaware that “ Dr. Savage has been
struck by the frequency of the occurrence of
general paralysis in the husbands of some
women of voluptuous physique.” Savage life
has its drawbacks.
• • •
All these things Mr. Ellis tells us. He also
supplies the information that in East Central
Africa sewing is men’s work, and a wife may
obtain a divorce if she can show a neglected
rent in her petticoat; that among many
savage tribes, at the birth of a child, the
father is put to bed, while the mother goes
about as if nothing had happened ; that
Tertullian, a cheerful early Christian writer,
remarked that woman was janua diaboli — the
devil’s front door; that Chateaubriand says
if you give a young girl her choice, Adonis or
Hercules, she will blush—and take Hercules ;
that a sculptor has said the breasts should al-
ways live at enmity—the right should look to the
right, and the left to the left; that, relatively to
height, in women the head is longer than in
men, the neck shorter, the trunk longer, and
the legs and arms shorter; that crooked legs
are a conspicuous aesthetic defect in woman
(which is probably the reason why Miss Squeers
was so enraptured with Nicholas Nickleby’s
beautiful, straight ones) ; that women are lead-
ing the movement to rob the little toe of a
joint—not being game to hit a toe of their own
size ; that the backbone is getting shorter, and
the Dibbs of ten thousand years hence will only
have 23 vertebrae, instead of 24, as at present;
that woman’s body-weightisto man’s as 83 to 100,
her height as 93 to 100, and her brain-weight
as 90 to 100; that men are more sensitive than
women, though less affectable—they see better,
smell better, and feel more—their taste is less,
and hearing is doubtful; that men are less
awkward than women ; that in the solitude of
the Australian bush one finds repeatedly that
while the settler is embarrassed and silent, or
scarcely able to utter more than monosyllables,
his wife is comparatively fluent and in pos-
session of a fairly rich and precise vocabulary ;
that women are more industrious but less in-
telligent than men, have better memories and
worse judgment, see parts better than the
whole, facts than the law ; that women need
less air than men ; that while a basso need not
fear any kind of excess so far as his voice is
concerned, a tenor must be extremely temper-
ate ; that little girls get hungry oftener than
little boys; that “ souvent femme varie ” is a
physiological fact and not a caprice ; that
married women dream less than un-
married, and men sleep sounder than
women ; that a man who administers anaesthe-
tics to a woman should always have two unim-
peachable witnesses; that noise and depravity
are much more common on the female than on
the male side of an asylum ; that the mixture
of heaven and hell in woman are different sides
of tlie same thing, and if you cut off the devil’s
tail you shear the angel’s wings ; that men are
better sculptors, painters, musicians, and writers
—women better actors ; that Scotch women are
the most criminal in Europe ; that in the saying
with which Goethe closed his “ Faust ” lies a
biological verity not usually suspected by those
who quote it.
* * #
The final statement is the keynote of Mr.
Ellis’s book. It was written with the a priori
view of showing that “das ewig-weibliche
ziebt uns hinan ;” and that when Paul said
“ the head of the woman is the man” he was
wrong, when Tennyson said “ woman is the
lesser man ” he was wrong, when Spencer said
“ woman is undeveloped man ” he was wrong.
The author has marshalled an immense number
of facts, but, unfortunately, most of them oppose
his theory ; and though he easily comes to his
foregone conclusion, the verdict is against the
evidence and the weight of evidence. So far
from woman leading, she is characteristically
the following sex. All progress is due to males
—they are the energetic variants; women
merely conserve and perpetuate the steps in
evolutionwhich men have won. Ellis remarks
that “ not only by his large brain, but by his
large pelvis, the modern man is following a
patli first marked out by woman,” though how
the possessor of a larger brain can follow the
possessor of a smaller brain when the relative
d.fh reuce is increasing, it is not easy to show.
The evidence goes to prove, on the other hand,
that mile and female brains were originally on
a relative equality, and that the male brain
grows continually larger and more complex,
while the female brain grows relatively smaller
and possibly less complex. As to the pelvis, that
is large in woman because some variable pre-
historic man took it into his head to become
a being erect on two legs (like that which
seduced Mrs. Bardell), and woman had liter-
ally to live up to him. But so essentially con-
servative is she, so slow to improve, that her
pelvis has not increased quickly enough, and at
the present moment it is hindering the progress
of the race. The boys with the biggest brains
die in infancy; the girls and small-brained
boys are easily born—which is the reason why
priests and monarchs are so well supported,
“ true mateship” is so scarce, and “socialism in
our time” is such an empty dream. Wait till
the backward female pelves are as highly
evolved as the progressive male brains, and the
millennium may begin. Only das ewig-weibliche
retards it.
« • •
Mr. Ellis’s statement that the infant is
higher in the line of evolution than the female,
and the female than the male, is mere fanciful
assumption. The adult male produces the
infant, not the infant adult males; and the
evolution of the infant depends chiefly upon the
variation of adult males. How, then, can it be
said that the infant, the effect, is higher in the
line of evolution than the cause—i.e., typically,
not chronologically? “Man, in carrying on
the line of evolution, started, not from some
adult male simian, but from the infant ape, and
in a less degree from the female ape.” What,
but the variability of the adult male simian,
gave to the infant ape the power to start man ?
Woman is the primitive sex physiologi-
cally ; and man has grafted his special
qualities on hers, losing something but gaining
more. The race, as the individual, is born from
her—the man is by the woman ; but, in evolu-
tion, the woman is of the man. She is his
echo, the shadow of his predominant charac-
teristics. He goes and grapples with the uni-
verse and selects such variations as aid the
progress of the race ; then returns and pours
into her lap, sitting at home, the wealth which
she doles out as needed. When a boy is playing
at marbles, the mate who “picks up” is nearly
always inferior in skill. Woman is the picker-
up of race-variations for the male player, and
she can rarely play as well herself. The play
of sex is analogous to the play of force
and matter. Probably force and matter are
one—so was originally sex. The object of
division is to give variation, which means
progress. Force is active, matter passive ; and
again the sexes are typified. The power of
motion is the essential factor in progress ; hence
the more active sex is always a bough higher on
the tree of evolution. But from a truer point
of view there is only sex-difference, not sex-
inequality ; since one is the complement of the
other, and neither can develop alone. Both are
among the innumerable manifestations of
those laws unknown and unknowable, which,
for want of a better title, men call God.—
Yours, Brigham Brown, M.D., F.R.C.S.
Now parsons grab at Parliament,
And fain would be Ms.P.
While sticking to the saintly cent }
But, no, it must not be.
To bear the present dose is tough ;

Says a Samoan war correspondent : “On
Saturday night the Aana rebels fired upon a small
Government party engaged in prayers. The sup-
posed loss on the Government side is two killed
and four wounded.” This is the sort of thing
that knocks the stuffing out of missionary teach-
ing, and sends the disgusted savage howling back
to unregeneracy and idol-worship. Cromwell’s
alleged maxim : “Trust in God and keep your
powder dry,” was built half for show and half for
use. If the Samoan rebels stick to the use half
and the Govt, troops to the show half, the rebels
will soon be Govt, troops. There never yet was a
prayer fervent enough to stop a bullet.
Norman Cameron, landholder, beaten in
the Deloraine (Tas.) election by Jonathan Best,
butcher and local preacher, was not satisfied with
his defeat, and threatened to dispute Best’s right
to a seat, on the ground of intimidation during-
the election on the part of the successful candidate
or his agents. Cameron has a spice of the bull-
dog about him, as those who witnessed his battle
with Gloster Picket, of Chudleigh, some years ago
can affirm. After three-quarters of an hour’s
slogging the Bentley man acknowledged he had met
his superior, but Picket was in hard fettle whilst
Cameron was just off the grass. Apropos, Father
Murphy, of Deloraine (Tas.), rather astonished
his audience on a recent Sunday, after morning
service, by throwing aside the ecclesiastical robes
and treating them to an animated discourse on
the evils of land monopoly. Best got in on the
R.O. vote.
Victorian Commandant Tulloch lately en
tertained the Tory lights of Melbourne with a-
second-hand lecture upon Russia’s advance towards
India, the prophetic portion of the Major-General’s
deliverance being based on the blind old military
assumption that Russia’s political state is immu-
table, and the Czar’s miserable subjects will groan
under a military system for ever. The mind of a
Tulloch rejects all thought of a social advance in
Russia itself. The present despotism is eternal
according to the gospel of professional sojerdom,
and so Russia will continue to “creep” and
“creep ” onwards unless Great Britain takes Tul-
loch’s advice “that any deliberate infringement
of the boundary-line as now established shall be
considered a casus belli.” Strange to say he
doesn’t anticipate a casus belli in the near future,
which is a matter for alarm, because these epau-
letted gentlemen seldom fail to sniff danger except
when danger is under their noses. The only
humorous features of Tuiloch’s Imperial enter-
tainment were on the face of M. Pontiata, the
Russian Consul, who sat around disguised in the
ghost of a Tartar smile, whilst the lecturer was
imploring the British Lion to keep its tail cooked.
Federation —"Under the Crown.”
(A BATTLE HYMN.)
They say that we Australians are of cold and
sluggish blood ;
That we only love the racecourse and the rolling
football’s thud ;
But the time is quickly coming when we’ll have to
make a stand
For our “leaders,” lads, are in it, and they want
to sell our land.
There’s to be a Federation, and they can’t deny
this, lads,
“ A great Australian army ” and a fleet of iron-
clads ;
Some British Prince will let us lift our hats and
loudly cheer
When he comes from “ Home ” to rule us for ten
thousand pounds a year.
There’s to be a new-born “ nation ” with its mimic
Court and king .
That will daze our knighted leaders with the titled
host they bring.
Is it still the sad old truth, lads, to be always so
on earth.
That a man is king and noble by the accident of
birth ?
Or is it all a fable that will pass and surely die ;
In the Old World still a “ symbol,” but for us an
outworn lie ?
You are called to give the answer, lads, at every
risk and loss,
“The noble man must prove his claim beneath
the Southern Cross.”
There’s to be a Federation “for the purpose of
Defence;” .
Some question we know nothing of may bring the
war-dogs hence. . t
We won’t have any vote, lads, but they tell us it s
all-right,
“ You find the ships and arm your men; we 11 tell
you whom to fight.”
They talk to us of “ Empire ” and the glory that
it brings,
But, looking round, in times of Peace, we’ve seen
the strangest things—
A woeful lack of glory on the pinched and pallid
face,
The pride of Empire ending with a starving,
desperate race.
We have listened to their broken prayer, and
seen their outstretched hands,
And, oh! their eyes were turned towards the latest
of the lands,
And, whilst we waited for a sign, the millions’
stifled moan
Was broken by a warning voice that .whispered,
“Stand alone!”
It has come to us, this message, in its own wild
stirring strains,
In the language of the fierce hot wind that sweeps
our boundless plains;
In the sullen, silent ranges, when with crash of
falling trees
The clearer light illumes the land, it bursts upon
the breeze.
Will you sing the old, worn anthems? —ye who
know of one so grand,
That the soul of him who hears it is for ever with
this land ?
Will you cling to deadened symbols, graven images
of stone,
Or with wider reverence learn to love and under-
stand your own ?
Shall we tell them, lads, it’s hopeless, that we fain
would find the track,
But the dense, thick scrub and dark’ning range
have turned us sadly back ?
Are you going to stop disheartened—ye who’ve
pressed on in the van,
And cut your way through country never trod by
foot of man ?
They are listening, lads, across the seas, and well
they understand;
They will know to what we’re “ loyal ” when they
see us axe in hand ;
They will cheer us on our journey, ring us out
with joyous chimes,
They will know it is no parting, that we’ll meet
in happier times.
And in the days of doubt and gloom, when all
seems drear and black.
We will see the God of Nations, lads, a-standing on
the track 1
He will pass with light’ning speed ahead, and
blaze each passing tree,
He will lead us to the distant scene we do not
ask to see.
He will make a sign : in silence we will push on
through the dark;
He will raise his hand, and, listening, the whole
wide world will hark ;
He will tell the joyous tidings of a new land in its
birth,
A Christ amongst the Nations / bringing hope to
all the Earth.
Perth, W.A.
W. E. CAREW.
In Melbourne, about a month ago, com-
positors’ pay was reduced |from Is. Id. to Is. per
1000, and on both Age and Abgus the salaried
hands were likewise retrenched. The other day the
Age proprietor informed his employes that the old
rate of pay would be reverted to, and also that
they would receive the amounts retrenched during
the month. The Argus men are—well, expectant.
The proprietors of that organ are awaiting Lino-
type developments, and the general manager has
been Sydney-wards inspecting the machines at the
Daily Telegraph office.
At the last Melb. tote-raid two establish-
ments were rushed by the police, and a splendid
haul resulted, 96 customers being arrested. The
catch was taken to the look-up in a long string of
cabs, the prisoners handcuffed in pairs. The tote-
keeper, whose establishment provided the prin-
cipal harvest, turned up shortly afterwards with
,£25 worth of stamps, with which to stamp the
bail-out documents, so that next year’s deficit is
already provided for. Most of the prisoners were
badly scratched with barbed wire, having got
themselves impaled on the Hebrew’s back fence in
their efforts to escape. The tote-man who puts
barbed-wire on his back fence plainly doesn’t
know bis business.
The wife of a certain ex-Maoriland knight
is reported to be indisposed, whereby hangs a
tale. The lady in question has been a social
failure of the first magnitude, and, despite her title,
she is so rarely mentioned that very few people
are even aware of her existence. So, at last—that
is how the allegation goes—she was fain to knock
her head hard against the wall, and then lie up
in bed and send round a notice setting forth that
she was indisposed, just for the sake of seeing her
own title in print. The poor little woman had
hardly so much as seen it in print before, and
didn’t know how it looked.
Ed. Bulletin, —Don’t bust, Mr. Bulletin,
over the curse of landlordism. True it is, that
C. G. Duffy is an Irishman, and that he was “ the
first to sow the dragon seed of Australian land-
lordism in Victoria.” True it is, also, that if you
transplant the Irishman to these provinces he
becomes a landgrabber, ready to add acre to acre
whenever he has the opportunity and the cash.
True, also, that when he becomes a landlord, he
is equal to his prototype of Ireland, and as great
a rack-renter. That being so, is it worth your
while to waste good ink in an attempt to make
the landocracy feel ashamed ? The most you can
do is to write, write, write, urging that not another
acre of your lands shall be sold as freehold. That
is what we are aiming for in Maoriland, and have
to a great extent attained. —W.U.
Victorian Labour Convention has adopted
a platform comprehensive enough to send one’s
forecast into the century after next Female
suffrage and one-adult-one-vote; referendum;
progressive tax on land values, exclusive of im-
provements ; cumulative tax on incomes over .£2OO
a year; tax on absentees ; continuance of protec-
tion ; State bank of issue; a law making eight
hours the maximum labour-day, “ wherever prac-
ticable;” a statutorily-declared minimum wage
for all labour employed on Government works;
establishment of a Department of Industry;
legally-constituted courts of conciliation and com-
pulsory arbitration; prohibition of Chinese and
other Asiatic labour and of “ workers under con-
tract” (?); a scheme of federation involving a
popularly-elected convention and a subsequent
referendum.
Editor Bulletin, —You have had a heap
of camel yarns; but nobody has told you that
there are as many breeds of camels as there are of
horses, from the little pony camels of Southern
Arabia to the big white Syrian beasts, measuring
10 feet or 11 feet to the top of the single hump, or
the heavy, long-bodied, two-humped camels of
Badakshan and Balkh. These last bear about the
same relation to the others that a Clydesdale bears
to a Cleveland horse. As for the “ original
African” camel ‘‘Randolph the Reckless” talks
about, I have always understood that all the
camels imported came from Karachi, which is not
in Africa. Seeing that most of the men concerned
in the camel business are Biluchis, it is not easy
to see why they should import the small, weak
African desert camels when they can get plenty of
better in their own country. Another little
blunder about the “aroma of coloured persons.”
The characteristic fetor of the negro is not
found among Asiatics. —J.R.
The opening at Pentridge of a new female
prison, to which most of the women previously
confined in the Melb. Gaol have been removed,
helps along the ancient agitation for the demolition
of the latter edifice. The site upon which it
stands is one of the best in the city, and has long
been coveted by the ever-watchful syndicate. It
is strange that during the land-boom, when
success would have been almost certain, the
agitators were silent. As an alternative to the
Gillies scheme of raising the wind by the sale of
the Kew Lunatic Asylum grounds, of which
Parliament approved in a special Act, a proposal
to sell the gaol site would have boomed. Looked
at now it was a marvellous dispensation by which
the prison was saved—at a time, too, when the
boomers were clamouring for the sacrifice of the
half-finished Anglican Cathedral so that its site
might be put on the market. Smellbourne’ s
gaols are used now much more than its churches.
General Debility.
The term General Debility means a depletion of
vital strength, a general break-up of the corporeal
health, due to over-consumption and wasting
away of the life-giving constituents of the blood
and nerve tissue, really a wearing out of the
animal structure. If your life is worth saving it
is worth your while to treat your case thoroughly.
If it is worth your while to treat your disease at
all, it is worth your while to continue the treat-
mentuntilyour health is thoroughly re-established;
you may feel all right after the first few bottles
of Clements Tonic, so prompt are its remedial and
curative effects; but a medicine, no matter how
good, cannot banish disease in a week, the seeds of
the complaint will still remain in your system and
break out afresh if you do not thoroughly eradicate
them. Don’t forget that a relapse is twice as
difficult to cure as the original attack, so if you
want to get cured give the remedy a fair chance
and recovery is certain. Clements Tonic is a
genuine article, it is prepared for a specific purpose
and contains the material to make it an effectual
remedy, and if used faithfully it cannot fail. We
do not claim it as a cure-all, we do not say it
will cure lock-jaw or bad spelling, but for all cases
of impaired vital strength it is a scientific and
infallible specific. Moreover, the cost of using
it is so small you can have a year’s course of
it for less than you can procure a week’s
medical attendance, and a tenfold better remedy
than any physician can prescribe for you.
Thousands of cases have been cured by
Clements Tonic, and it will cure you if you
give it a fair chance. Read the case of
Mr. John Sheridan, gardener, Katoomba, who
writes, Xmas, 1892 :—I have been a severe sufferer
from chronic indigestion for several years, accom-
panied by headaches, biliousness, extreme weak-
ness, and a general languishing depression, and
having been treated for same without success, I
was induced to try your Clements Tonic, and must
add my testimony to its great curative properties,
as after a few bottles I feel myself in every way a
new man. You can make what use you may
wish of this.—John Sheridan, botanical gardener,
Katoomba, N.S.W.

In Absence.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The moon and a star hung in blackness above me—
The moon and one star,
And I thought of the friends far away, those who
love me,
Where sweet wattles are ;
Where swamp-oaks, with musical, mystical motion,
Sing low to the sedges
Who whisper the song to the river, the ocean,
Along the creek-edges;
Where grey gums stand stretching gaunt arms to
high Heaven
In piteous despair,
As ghosts who pray grimly from sin to be shriven
By priests of the air ;
While on the horizon the heat-ripples shimmer,
Deceiving the eye.
And blue hills in distance grow dim, ever dimmer,
And melt in bbrs-sky.
Deserted the homestead, ablaze with bright
creepers,
Its rough walls transfigured.
As Death glorifies the hard faces of sleepers
Who found Life a niggard ;
Deserted the stockyard, no echo remaining
Of muster-whips’ rattle,
The shouts of the horsemen, the dogs’ angry
feigning,
The groaning of cattle.
And phantoms long fled came in order completest,
Rough beards and burnt faces —
Ah, surely the pang of the Past is the sweetest
Pain’s pleasure embraces —
Clear eyes, that met mine with a gaze frank and
steady,
Revealing hearts true ;
Strong hands, gripping friends’ hands to show
they were ready
To dare all for you.
They passed, and her form filled the shadowy
vista—
She the moon, I the star ;
And my pulse leapt again as it leapt when I kissed
her,
Where sweet wattles are.
Then days of delight, when desires were all sated—
Attained the ideal,
When the universe seemed but a figment, created
To make rapture real;
And then days of doubt, days of anguish and
longing,
In Love’s bitter title ;
Then days of despair, when black demons came
thronging
And urged black requital.
The moon shone above me, enthroned in white
splendour—
The moon and no star ;
O, madman, could I in that bright sphere attend
her!
Better thus, better far.
A billow of clouds surged aloft, tnen another,
Extinguishing light,
And Earth nestled close to the breast of her
mother,
The infinite Night.
1893.
A. G. SETPENS.
Pramama’s Prophecy: A
Malaccan Marvel.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Jt has in all ages been fortunate for the
prophet that mankind, in its craving for bara-
boozle, forgets to average results—remembers,
here and there, a hit; ignores the multitude of
misses
Mahometan and Malay by courtesy, the people
of tbe western Moluccas yet savour strongly, in
blood, tongue, and superstition, of the abori-
gbial races of the group. The gloomy witchcraft
of Gilolo—surviving imaum’s curse and Dutch-
lesi.l.tion-pMsesse. everywhere through-
out the other islands its female workers of for-
bidden wonders.
Pramama’s powers were great, said all men in
Klik. Within her proper sphere she was a woman
to be dreaded ; but, as for her predictions—the
Over-God had laughed to think Pramama wanted
aR bis gifts> g 0) though the people mightily
feared her spells, they privately derided her
vaticinations. Up till ’62, at all events, for in
that memora ble year Pramama gained, from
fcQ Rai6a) a prophetic fame that will long
survive the memory of her failures.
his bride. Two wives already had Jikar—hitherto
enough ; but, richer now by a lucky stroke in
pearls, he thought things would run to a third.
Which third was Fulsi—beautiful, from a Malay
point of view—substantially dowered, from any
point of view—and daughter of the chief of Klik
—Romongo.
Romongo it was who now held up his nng-
loaded hand. The gongs stopped all at once, and
jj kar>s vo , ce) by contrast with the clangour,
sounded like a child’s. But his words were a
man’s—pure, direct, and simple.
.< thank theej Father Romongoy-thee and thy
people! A good woman is the gift of Alla, and
Alla’s gifts are precious in the sight of Jikar.
Peace be with thee !—we go !”
“ Peace !” repeated a loud voice from without
the crowd. A man’s voice, Jikar thought, till
with the scared scattering of holiday sarongs and
bright bedangs he saw the speaker, a woman—
pramama i—for he heard the name passed round.
This, then, was the sorceress ! Jikar gently put
the veiled Falsi behind him and stood forth, the
upper extreme of the Malaysian race, as the fierce
virago that faced him was the lower. The Sibawi
chief, in snowy jacket and sarong, stood five-feet-
six—a very tall Malay. The sorceress, with but a
d j r t y re d chawat to hide her monstrous corpulence,
W as tall a 8 he—gigantic for a Malay woman. In
Jikar’s high, calm forehead appeared the Arab
B fc ra i nj as j n hf B shapely features, his easy dignity
of carriage. In Pramama’s beaky nose and close-
-Bftt eye a and faintly-crisping hair, the Papuan
affinity broke out, as in the blustering loudness of
her speech.
“Peace? What hast thou to do with peace,
thou mangy dog from Sibawi ? To my son was
this ff irl promised ! Take her, thou , and see
what comes of it 1”
Grey-headed Romongo blasphemed. “Pro-
mised ! Thy son !—she-beast!—accursed cow of
Sheetan ! The daughter of Romongo ”
From her dingy loin-cloth the sorceress took
suddenly an object that froze him into silence.
Only the skull of a snake—it was, to these Moluc-
cans—as to the Obi-dreaders of Africa —a thing of
ghastly possibilities. The people widened from
the witch like ripples from a stone, as, squatting
nimbly upon her colossal hams, she fitted the
skull upon a finger-tip and pointed with it straight
at Romongo.
The chief put up his arm as if to guard a blow.
“ What can I do, woman ?—they are married ! ”
“ Take her back ! ”
“H oy can I ? Married are they, I tell thee! ”
and Romongo’s voice rose nearly to a shriek as the
witch, with hideous leer and pointing finger, got
up and came slowly towards him. Then came a
collective gasp from the crowd, as Jikar, stepping
swiftly over, struck the fetish from the woman’s
finger and kicked it scornfully into the sea. In an
instant Pramama stood on the black basaltic edge.
The small white object, zig-zagging as it sank, was
still in sight, and with a tremendous souse the
witch went head - foremost after it. When,
with grampus-snortings, she came up again,
Romongo was vanishing amongst the sago-trees.
But him Pramama had forgotten. With fat
flanks heaving from her dive, she clambered out
and faced the bridegroom.
“ Take her, then,” she said between her gasps,
“to thy home—thine island of Sibawi. But one
night’s sail is it with such a wind as this. With
sunrise will ye be there— but no Sibaivi shall ye
find! Toman shall ye see, and Tarkat—but
Sibawi never more ! ”
• • «
It was a weird and unimaginable night.
Not stormy; steadily went the prahu before the
mild sou’-wester, powerless to raise a break upon
the sullen swell that set miraculously against it.
Like inky oil was the water —robbed of its phos-
phorescent sparkle as no man there had ever
before seen it. But, overhead, into a starless sky
came here and there a fitful gleaming as of molten
metal, while, all around, the circling sea-line
faintly marked itself upon a sickly greenish edging
to the upper gloom. Strange, far-off rumblings
came across the sea to Jikar’s trembling crew, and
“ Pramama !” went from mouth to mouth.
Jikar stood aft—steering—and thanking Alla
that Falsi slept. “ Else would she catch the
terrors of these quakers at an old hag’s babble !
What, now?”
The men—clustered forward in a muttering
group—had started into louder voice, and one—
thejuragan—called : “North-eastward, master!—
look ! look !” Beneath the foot of the inizzen
Jikar stooped, and looked.
Low upon the north-eastern heavens played a
lightning that changed its hue with every flash-
blood-red, steel-blue, and ghastly, vivid green.
Below this, after half-a-mmute, started out an eye
of crimson glare, that waxed and waned—went
out —then sprang again to fiercer life, as to the
watcher came, like thumps upon the heart, half-a-
dozen vast reports that shook the prahu from stem
to stern as if she had taken ground. Then Jikar
understood. “Again?” he muttered. “After
centuries !” Louder, then —“ It is *Makian, ye
fools!—broken out again at last!”
The men came aft, relieved. An erupting
volcano was, at all events, the known; a witch’s
curse was the iynotum pro terribile. But Makian
had not done with them. Cigars were glowing,
rice cooking, tongues wagging, when slowly
through the chatter rose a sound that stopped it.
“ A squall I”—and the men rushed to the halliards,
fore-and-aft. But the juragan—a, man of many
years and earthquakes bellowed, “ Touch
nothing, fools ! No squall is this !—it is the
earthquake-wave !”
He ran to Jikar at the helm. “Keep we her
head as it is, master. Stem on, there is little
danger ! ” Almost with the words the first wave
was upon them—unseen in that portentous black -
ness—but heaving the craft up, and up, till rolling
swiftly aft came a Bugis boy who had lost his grip
forward. Faster down the after-slope—a plunge
that nearly shook the spars out of her—then up
again and down—with smaller wave and smaller,
till, safe within the mighty arc, they slid once
more along the oily swell,
* * •
With awe . s truck faces—paler still by con-
trasfc with the sable pall tha f covered dee k and
ri i sail and c i oth es-Jikar and his men
lo^ed * round ’ tbem at dayligbt-or what should
have been dayli ht for t he black volcanic dust
made s till a darkness that defied the sun. A dim
red glitnmer in the East was all that showed hiB
presence, but from this came light enough to make
out Toman here, Tarkat there, but Sibawi—no-
where.
Pramama had “ fluked ” herself into fame—the
island had vanished.

Treasurer Carter is maundering once more
over his great scheme for converting the entire
Victorian debt, in one act, into an interminable
3 per cent, security. He thinks that, by making
the public creditor a present of £6,000,000, he will
be induced to effect the charge, and even with
this addition to the principal of the debt the
saving in interest would be .£286,000 per annum.
If the province will pass a law that this £286,000
per annum shall go into a sinking-fund for the
redemption of the debt, and that any Treasurer
who borrows the sinking fund, or suspends it, or
diverts it in any way from its purpose shall be
hanged, and that any politician who proposes to
revoke the law shall have all his property con-
fiscated and be exiled, then the addition to the
principal of the debt doesn’t matter much. But
otherwise it is only one more way of shoving off
the trouble until to-morrow.

author who is familiar with life in Northern Queensland, writes with vigour about the doings of the whites and the natives in these faraway
